T he Red Page
VISION OF WAR.
Along the edge of the thicket just now, as I
went to the orook doion there for renter,
I stumbled over something that must have
been left from the cliqrge a week ago:
A body that held the remnants of a man.
He had dragged himself to the brook, he lay
imbedded in tall reaving grass;
His stoma.fh had been ripped open by shrap-
_ j tel, maggots were, heaving in the wound;
(Did you know that v man could live while
maggots formed in his flesh?)
His muscles twitched convulsively, he was
barely conscious;
He did not notice the match I struck, his
eyes were filmed over, he would not
drink ;
The region that he inhabited was an un-
. known, unimaginable land,
(At home, a woman waits for news of him;
It is well that she can never hear.) ....
There are no illusions about the body, no
sorrowing or regret;
The body seen in its true place, and given its
just due.
(Hustle it off,' bury it quick, dig a shallow
hole;
Dust to dust—it will help the next year's
crops;
"No time to spare for foolish ceremonies, no
ammunition to spare for a parting
volley;
It is .only death—the man is done with his
body now —it is no more use to him or
anybody else;
It was never very important—not h< If so
important as he himself imagined;
Now it is the least important thing in the
w0r1d.) ....
Look at that overgrown baby, blubbering at
a cut finger;
I suppose it hurts, but what of it?—Ms hip is
broken—l wish it tvas his neck;
What a row he makes—for God’s sake, choke
him off, or take him away;
Wouldn’t you think a man with a body like
that would have some sand in his gutsf
A perfect physique—he was fond of showing
his strength, he ivas everlastingly talk-
ing about it;
He could do some wonderful things, too —the
strongest man I ever saw;
But God deliver us from these blatant lumps
of flesh;
Look behind the muscle, when there is work
to do:
Look beyond the body, when there is pain to
bear ....
Old man, you hold a wrong conception of this
thing called war;
The actual fighting is not of the least im-
portance;
The killing and being killed are not worth
talking about;
The willingness to be killed is the only vital
issue;
of war is its only argument. . 7".
O, sometimes it seems to me that after a
time of peace, men rush to war with
gladness, hearts relieved to live again!
O, to have done with this sad fraud! To
throw aside these chains! Be free, be
free!
O, to do something natural once more!
Forget the hideous pressure of the
world!
O, to escape the stifling air! To breathe the
open! To see the stars again!
O, to go out and die for an idea again!. ...
The peacemakers speak as if the truth had
already arrived;
They speak as if the world could not be
bettered much, society in. its final stage,
man nearing his perfection;
Go, ask tlie poor if this be so:—
They cry that war is a hideous waste of
ivealth, the stored-up labor of hands;
But if peace stores up the labor of hands for
other hands than those which performed
the labor, I say that such peace is more
hideous waste of wealth than war. ...
(I say; in war, something is being changed
— something, at least, is coming
through. ) ....
War offers a freer field for rich and poor to
suffer and pay alike;
And only out of war shall come eventual
universal equity. ...
Europe has been betrayed!—Civilisation has
been set back a century!—-
(As if men from some other planet had stuck
a finger in your pie!)
I think it is only Europe's business that has
been "betrayed"—the soul of Europe has
been affirmed with deathless affirmation;
I think it is only the falsehood of civilisa-
tion that has been “set back a century”;
The truth of civilisation has advanced an-
other imperceptible degree. ...
Come now, old man, we have been bluffing
long enough;
Shoio down your hand.
What is this tale that you are trying to tell
me?
Do you really expect to continue just as you
are, forever and ever?
Do you really expect this nation and that
nation to continue just as they are, for-
ever and ever?
Do you really expect this race and that race
to continue just as they are, forever and
ever?
How are you going to manage it?—l think
you will have your job cut out for
y0u ....
Ah, Germany, where are your aneient
dreams?
You are cruelly earnest, terribly serious;
You have fought with your soul —notv you
are fighting the world;
(But I think it was the world that you won
in your soul;
/ think it is your soul that fights you now in
the world.)
I think you are just another awful contra-
diction;
I think you have tried to make a religion out
of scepticism;
Something, at any rate, has kept you in fight-
ing trim;
Something has fired you with the reaction of
a dream;
I think you are a creative, religious power,
in arms against religion;
I think you are love gone mad, contending in
the dark with love.
I think you are monstrously, religiously
wrong..*.
Japan, you have learned it ivell —you may go
to the head of the class;
You speak it without a hitch —you can beat
the others at their own game.
(And that is why your day is not so long.)
Do you think that a race can throw over-
board a well-stowed cargo of two thou-
sand years, and tumble in an alien load,
and crack on sail?
There is no ballast in that ship—she cannot
stand against God’s gusty wind;
She loill go over in some squall, and drown
her company;
(The squalls come heavy off the China
shore.)
All the old beauty gone, the fine old truth,
The ancient honor of an upright race;
A nation’s birthright sold for the .world’s
pottage,
While the Elder Statesmen shake their heads,
prepared to go.
A century’s brief power—and then a century
to die;
For this, you dare to wake the sleeping
giant;
And this shall be your end....
America, I think you have not shoion your-
self....
I think that you have never been put to the
true test —the test of poverty;
I think that you have had too much success,
and altogether too much money to throw
around;
I think that when the real trial comes, you
will be found a land quick to battle and
revolution;
I hope, for the sake of your dreams, that you
may have many revolutions;
I hope, for the sake of your spirit, that you
may have many desperate wars....
I think that humanity is in for a long period
of confusion, disappointment—a period
of transition;
I think that the world has yet to pass through
the Dark Ages of democracy, while
practice is catching up with theory ....
I have been trying to say that all this peace
which we make so much fuss about is
perfectly obvious preparation for war;
(So long as we want and have material
possessions, we will be fighting either to
gain or to hold;)
I have been trying to say that the fighting
of wars is our atonement for the making
of them in times of peace;
I have been trying to say that if we like this
peace, and if we enjoy the world as it
is, then we are cowards and hypocrites
to complaAn of _ war.
From “Vision of War,” by Lincoln Colcord
(Macmillan, New York, per Sydney
Book Club.)
Lincoln Colcord is wearing Whitman’*
goloshes, and they fit. His Vision of War
(Macmillan, per Sydney Book Club) is just
the sort of vision that Whitman would have
had had he been able to fire his poetry and
his platitudes at this war. Lincoln Col-
cord shows how horribly easy it is to be
Whitmanesque, once Whitman had shown the
way. Yet his vision, of which the above
extracts represent only a negligible portion,
is worth reading, though it would be rather
easier if this prose were printed as prose
and if the author knew a little more about
the art of punctuation. But in this, the
first, effort to compass in one poem the whole
aspect of modern war, Lincoln Colcord
is, to a surprising extent, successful. And
from his confused and haphazard pictures,
his lurid rhetoric and the annoying frequency
with which he remarks “I think” or “I say”
or merely “O,” there does emerge a definite
picture of the soul of this war, and of all
wars. Being a fellow-citizen of Whitman’s
he is blatantly neutral, but though he casti-
gates England for her faults of the past, he
has nothing but admiration for the Empire’s
present efforts, and he looks back sadly at a
Germany that is gone. But the poeyn (or
prose-work) is, above all, a glorification of
“this serious new game for earnest, grown-
up children.” For Lincoln Colcord this war
is necessary and right. The advent of this
champion to deliver the world from the stag-
nation of our enervating peace, was, in this
author’s opinion, long overdue. The book is
serious and sincere.
War
Rumor, or, maybe, that reliable corres-
pondent “Anon,” mentions that the average
life of any aeronaut in this war is
merely 50 hours: —50 hours in the air. As
an aeronaut on active service is llying,
on an average, but a small portion of
each day, this would give him from four
weeks to, say, a couple of months before he
became a casualty or a corpse. Flight-Lieut.
Harold Rosher, R.N., became a member of
the naval-air service in August, 1914, and
was killed on February 16, just 22 years old.
So he had, comparatively, a long life in the
air. But In the Royal Naval Air Service
(Chatto and Windus; 3s. 6d.), which con-
sists cf his letters to his family, one of the
most frequent items of interest is his report
of the disablement or death of his flying
chums.
Rosher was a charming English lad; and
his letters, obviously never meant for publi-
cation, give not only a graphic picture of the
perils and joys of air service in war, but
reveal a modest and most engaging person-
ality. They are interesting, also, for their
new slang—the slang of the air. It is a
remarkably expressive slang, full of quaint
humor. Rosher took part in many famous
raids, and was killed at the Dover Aero-
plane Station, whither he had been returned
from France for instructional purposes.
There, the morning of his death, lie found a
pilot ready to ascend .in a machine that
had not been tested. Rosher ordered the
pilot out, and himself took it up for a brief
trial flight. The cause of the accident that
followed was not discovered. As he wrote:
“If one goes on flying long enough, one is
bound to get huffed [killed] in the end.”
Here is a typical extract from one of his
letters: “I crashed into the atmosphere first
thing this morning and flipped around for
55 minutes 1 ended up with a hair-
splitting spiral, with the machine banked
up to about 55 degrees. I only did three or
four complete turns, but kept on until
I was scared stiff. When you bank a machine
over 45 degrees, your rudder turns into your
elevator and vice versa. To come out of a
spiral, you just shove everything the wrong
way round and wait and see what happens.
.... ” Rosher had one curious experience;
he was chased by a Zep. “I sighted the Zep-
pelin coming home over Zeebrugge. I turned
off due east to avoid being seen, intending to
wait until he came down and then to catch
him sitting. But my luck was out. One of
the searchlights picked me up, and anti-air-
craft guns immediately opened fire on me.
Then a curious thing happened. The Zeppe-
lin sighted me (I think the searchlights
were signalling) and immediately came for
me. This was the tables turned on me with
a vengeance, and the very last thing I ever
dreamt of. It was a regular nightmare.
I was only 6000 ft. up, and the Zep., which
was very fast, must have been 10,000 ft.
Without being able to get above it I was, of
course, helpless and entirely at the mercy
of his Maxim guns. We had ‘some’ race I
He tried to cut me off from Holland, but X
got across his bows. He was a huge big
thing, most imposing, and turned rapidly
with the greatest of ease. I hung around.. ~
climbing hard, and reached - 8500 ft., but the
Zep. wasn’t having any. He wasn’t coming
down while I was there, and I, on the other-
hand, couldn’t get up to him, as he had
risen to some fabulous height, so after a bit
I pushed off home, feeling very discontented
at such an unsatisfactory ending....”
4*
Monica Cosens, who was one of the
“Miouws,” as the volunteer girls are called in
the munition factories of England, writes
brightly of her work in Lloyd George's
Munition Girls (Hutchinson, per Sydney
Book Club; Is.). It is very hard work, but
the spirit of these girls, most of whom are
of the “fact’ry ’ands” type, is remarkable.
And though these girls are engaged on men’s
work, they do not forget their sex. “It is
five minutes to one now, on the night shift.
The Khaki girls round me place their brown
cases on the ends of their machines, dive into
the depths and take out pieces of rag with
which to rub their besmeared faces. Next,
out come hand-mirrors, very beautiful things
in themselves, sometimes encircled with sea-
shells and sometimes mounted on blue plush,
which they poise against the lids of the cases
and stoop and bend before them as they tidy
their hair, powder their faces, touch up their
eyelashes ,and redden their lips. Conceited
little baggages, these Khaki girls! But, after
all, there are admirers in the factory, and it
would not do for them to appear at a dis-
advantage even though it is the middle of the
night!” And it is the greatest crime in the
eyes of a Khaki girl to be seen in the street
in any of her work-clothes. There are
fashions, too, in the workshop. “Lately....
many hands have discarded the frilly caps
in favor of red and yellow, or pink and green
bandana handkerchiefs, and the khaki over-
alls for those of butcher blue.” In the
author’s factory there was a great deal of
talk about “the proper smart kiddies up on
the hill” —Miss Tommy Atkins was refer-
ring to .Billjim’s camp close by. There was
hardly a girl who did not wear the regi-
mental badge belonging to one or another of
the Australian regiments. And “young Else
got spliced to an Orstralian this afternoon.”
Monica Cosens deplores the fact that the
strain of the night-shift is beginning to tell
on the health of the girls. “We oUlose our
looks here,” said one to her. “But what
does it matter if we do?” she went on.
“That’s better than losing a leg or an arm.
And what does it matter if a few of us do
get killed off? There are too many of us
already.” “So she goes on, as they all go
on—because they can go on. They are not
doing work beyond their endurance. They
can carry on, but it means an effort, and it is
not until the end of the war, when the
reaction sets in, that they will realise how
tired they are.”
Would women like these have voted No?
Carolyn Wells in The Century cheer-
fully offers her Salt River Anthology, in
imitation of Edgar Lee Masters’ famous, or
notorious, Spoon River Anthology. Here is
“Alice Ben Bolt”
I couldn’t help weeping with delight
When the boys kissed me and called me sweet.
It was foolish, I know,
To weep when 1 was glad;
But I was young and I wasn’t very well.
I was nervous, weak and anaemic,
A sort of human mimosa; and I hadn’t much brains,
And my minff wouldn’t jell, anyhow.
That’s why I trembled with fear when they frowned,
But they didn’t frown often,
For I was sweetly pretty and most pliable.
But, oh, the grim joke of asking Ben Bolt if he
remembered me 1
Me! .
Why, it was Ben Bolt who—
Well, never mind. He paid for this granite slab,
And it’s as stylish as any in the church-yard.
But I wish I had a more becoming shroud.
And, of course, the rather overworked
“Blessed Damosel”; —
I was one of those long, lanky, loose-jointed girls
Who fool people into believing
They are willowy and psychic and mysterious.
I was always hungry; I never ate enough to satisfy
me,
For fear I’d get. fat.
Oh, how little the world knows of the bitterness of
life
To a woman who tries to keep thin!
Many thought I died of a broken heart,
But it was of an empty stomach.
Then Mr. Rossetti wrote about me.
He described me all dolled up in some ladies’ wear-
ing apparel
That I wore at a fancy ball.
I had fasted all day, and had had my hair marcelled
And my face corrected.
And I was a dream.
But he seemed to think he really saw me,
Seemed to think I appeared to him after my death.
Oh, fudge!
These spiritualists are always seeing things!
And “Little Eva”:-
To be honest,
I didn’t mind dying.
For I had .y.
One of these here now
Dressy deaths.
It was staged, you know,
And, like Samson,
My death brought down the house.
I was a smarty kid,
And they were less frequent then than later.
Oh, I was the Mary Pickford of my time,
And I rest content
With my notoriety.
And, as Carolyn Wells has overlooked
Ginger MicJc, be shall speak for himself: —
’Striith, it’s a fair cow,
The way that bioke Dennis ’as treated me 1
I never even ’eard of the beggar down Spadger’s
Lane;
’B never showed his blanky dial there —
Or, if ’e did, ’e never ’ad the luck ter strike met
That blok® in my push ?
Come orf! . : .
Wot sort of a time ’ud Rosie give me if she seen me
with a poet-bloke for a cobber?
Next thing the cops ’ud be astin’ the Push ter teal
I knoo ’e wrote a lot er kid about a sentiblankimental
bloke ‘ : - -
And, ’strath, that’s orl this Dennis coot is!
But 1 ain’t never met one in the Push.
It’s tough luck, my oath!
Any’ow I’m orl right ’ere at Lonesome Pine:
It’s a long way from Spadger’s Lane,
But it’s a blanky sight further from that cow!
Square an’ orl, the bullet that outed me at Lonesome
Pine
the best bit er luck that Ginger Mick ever-
struck— ■■ . -
An’ the luckiest thing that ever ’appened
To that Dennis coot!
Me dirty left!
Me blanky oath!
WHAT THE SOLDIER READS.
“Red Cross” tells of Tommy’s taste for
literature: I was sorting out books to take to
the wards, in England, one day, and the
orderly helping me nearly had a heart attack
when I sniffed at a bundle of “comics” —the
sort the very young Australian boys spend
their pennies on. “Why, sister,” he said,
“they are just the thing the chaps will
rush.” And they did—“not ’arf”! One
patient, reading For the Term _of His
Natural Life, preferred “to stick it” than go
for a drive. Magazines are the most popu-
lar, as longer stories can’t be indulged in
in wards where there are so many distrac-
tions, and I guess the trenches would be the
same. V
The Conservatorium Magazine, edited
by Iloland Foster, the official organ of the
N. S. Wales Conservatorium of Music (Gov-
ernment Printer, Sydney; 6d.), gives good
literary and musical value for the money.
It happily lacks the dreariness of the usual
educational production; it is almost bright;
it is well illustrated. The enthusiasm that
pervades this excellent State institution is
evidently responsible for this production. The
next issue is announced for Deeemhw.

The following testimony is from Mr.
George Goodman, better known as
“Goody” in the profession, and one of
the most widely and professionally
known of a band of brilliant theatrical
managers who form an artistic link
between the theatrical past and
present.
Mr. Goodman has been associated with
the present firm of J. C. Williamson,
Ltd., for a long number of years, ex-
tending back to its inception, when
as “Williamson, Garner and Mus-
grove,” it occupied, as it does to-day,
the foremost position in the theatrical
world of Australia, and was respon-
sible for the finest imported produc-
tions of Grand Opera and versatile
drama and comedy.
Mr. Goodman’s experience with these
productions and association with the
many “stars” imported under the Wil-
liamson management, is proverbial,
and the following letter will be of
considerable interest to the many
members of the theatrical profession
throughout the Commonwealth, who,
physically exhausted by the exacting
nature of their many roles, will find
in the medicine Mr. Goodman advo-
cates a nerve rejuvenator and health
restorer of remarkable power and cer-
tainty. Mr. Goodman writes from his
Sydney residence thus: —■

ALMOST EVERYBODY COMPLAINING.
Kidney disease is most certainly on the in-
crease. Thousands and thousands of people
in the towns and villages of this country are
in terrible danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps on, gradu-
ally getting a firmer and firmer grip, until
when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease: Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first thing
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, puffiness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Cruel Wind of Springtime.
With dry eyes, aching, pressed against the
pillow,
And thwarted bosom heaving in the dark,
She felt the soft sweet breath of murm’rous
Springtime
Steal like a little ghost in from the park.
“O cruel little ghost!” she said, “you haunt
me
With visions of a future ne’er to be,
With dreams of joy and toil and shouting
children
And all my gallant lover meant for me.”
She sees him lying cold in far-off Flanders —•
One hero more has played the better part.
But wails of tender little spirit children
Pierce the sad darkness round her lonely
heart.
N.S.W.
S. RAVENSCROFT.
The Peacock.
Below the terrace urns of roses glowing
The wide walk spread, a riband of hot
gold,
And there the peacock met me, proudly
showing
His jewelled breast, right gorgeous to
behold,
Demanding like a king my humble duty,
Fit homage to his most surpassing beauty.
But clear above his splendor I saw shining
Two eyes of changeful emerald and blue,
A garland of gay tresses intertwining
About the fairest face I ever knew,
A mocking mouth that smiled not too un-
kindly.
I passed the bird uncivilly, and blindly.
My Lady stood beneath the crimson roses,
Herself a rarer rose, more fine, more pale;
I said: “While his fierce heart the sun dis-
closes
My lips shall tell to yours a fiery tale.”
But as we paced the path of gold together
Meseemed too bright the treacherous June
weather,
Too sweet the burden of the roses’ sighing,
Too sapphire-deep the sky that shone that
noon
On Eglantina, petulantly crying
“This gravel burns me through my satin
shoon!
Let us walk thither where the boughs are
shady.”
I entered the dim pinewood with my Lady.
Only the dusky solitudes were round us;
Somewhere unseen there moaned a woo-
ing dove;
Never a more hour had found
us;
The very air was passionate with love —
When sudden, through the ring-dove’s
scarce-heard singing,
The harsh screams of the peacock came a-
ringing.
“Beauty and Vanity you scorned unheed-
ing"—
Thus I interpreted the curse, alack —
“Beauty and Vanity shall leave you bleed-
ing!"
But Eglantina laughed: “We will go back.
Fear not, Faint Heart, that cry of wrath
and sorrow;
He would but have us know ’twill rain to-
morrow.”
* * * *
I watch the rose-leaves fall, wind-torn and
sodden;
My Lady’s eyes are turned another way.
Grey mists enshroud the terrace walks un-
trodden ;
My Lady’s locks have snared a newer
prey.
New lips teach hers my interrupted story...
The rain has drowned out all the golden
glory.
VICTORIA.
ELSIE COLE.
Plainte D’Amour.
Let be, delicious girl,
And cease to pet and tempt me!
My heart’s too worn to whirl,
My years should sure exempt me
From taxes that I paid,
Out-passing far my duty,
Ere I was wise and staid,
To wheedling youth and beauty.
Your mouth that masters mine
When you demurely curve it.
Turns all my blood to wine,
And how can I deserve it?
I’m old, you jade, I’m old,
My style’s the sere and yellow.
These ringlets of burnt-gold '
Are for some sprightlier fellow.
The dimples that adorn
Your shoulders and such places
Shrine each a Cupid born
To trip the sidling Graces;
But bald and graceless I,
An unrepentant fogey,
And soon I must espy
Approaching Death, that bogey.
Kissed, I will kiss again
In admirable fashion;
Nor need I yet to feign
The joys and toys of passion.
But life’s glad sphere for you
Is rounded in completeness:
Mine’s chipped and thumbed askew....
Ah, do not waste your sweetness!
Ere I was wise and staid,
To wheedling youth and beauty
The taxes that I paid
Out-passed by far my duty.
But now from life’s hot whirl
My years should sure exempt me....
Let be, delicious girl,
And cease to coax and tempt me!
N.S.W.
JOSEPHUS Q. HARRIS.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknouledge in the “Cor r espondence” column
■all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
■and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
■will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. ivhen
the address ts specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
■conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
■the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to Tiie Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
■applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 30, 1916.
“WHEN IT WAS DARK.”
,N that zigzag motion which it calls evolu-
ion, and in those circular sweeps which it
describes as progress, the world eddies and
halts and bows and pirouettes to a great
extent round certain ideas and formulas
which from time to time win general accept-
ance. One of the most important of these
is the notion that democracy is kind, that its
rough hand has a womanly tenderness
about it, and that shames and wrongs perpe-
trated by individuals in power and high
places would never be tolerated by the mass
of the people if they were once raised to the
full height of democratic authority. This
faith is not usually stated in words, but it
is left to be inferred whenever wider fran-
chises and popular control are under dis-
cussion ; and it is the pivot on which the
whole modern State turns. Despots may be
good or bad. If they are good—as they have
shown themselves to be two or three times
in the long history of the world —all the
garden breaks into fresh bloom, if they are
bad —as they usually are—the whole
national landscape becomes a howling desert
bursting out into spots of sheer hell. But
the soi.d conviction remains that democracy
is always good in intention, that the mass of
the people thinking as a whole will think no
wrong, and that no large body of citizens
would ever of themselves combine to injure
the community for purely selfish ends. Take
this faith aw r ay, prove it to be wrong in
essence, and there is no argument to be ad-
vanced for democracy. Government by the
few must always be easier than government
by the. many; and if the many govern no
more kindly, to place the rule in their hands
is manifestly absurd.
* * * *
Some years ago the reading public met a
mild literary sensation at the bookstalls,
when a writer of pious melodrama came
along . with a yarn called “When It Was
Dark.” The obscurity was caused by
the supposed discovery of the tomb of Jesus
Christ with the original bones in it. Imme-
diately the Christian religion was alleged to
have collapsed. Its central notion was gone;
the miracle of the Resurrection was proved
a gigantic fake ; and from Moody and Sankey
to the Archbishop of Canterbury and the
Pope all the pious world stopped praying,
choked on the last verse of the anthem, and
felt that the bottom had fallen out of the
cosmos. All morality was gone; Heaven’s
gate was shut with a loud bang; nothing of
all that was supposed to matter most was
worth bothering about. Of course, for the
purpose of the yarn, the light came back
again, and the fraudulent bones were re-
moved, while the scientific devil who had
planned and carried out the whole affair got
something of what was coming to him. Still,
there was a lot in the central idea. The
empty tomb and the solemn angels who
watched while Peter and Mary paid their
morning visit lay at the foundation of a
mighty mass of divinity, and gave form to
all the creeds. To pull away that underpin-
ning was to bring down a toppling mass of
church spires and mitres and bishops’ gaiters
and pew rents and presbyteries and altar
decorations and mission boards —in fact, the
whole lock, stock and barrel of a dozen eccle-
siastical systems. They all rested on an
idea, and once the idea was gone they were
like prudence without a chaperon or a fish-
shop bankrupt of ice.
Just as the great systems into which the
Christian religion is crystallised rest on the
fundamental belief in the Resurrection, so
democracy stands on the belief that kindly
human sympathy animates the mass of men.
Just now that kindly human sympathy is in
grave doubt. We are in the dark, politically
as well as after seyen o’clock. The black-
ness of our city streets through the coal
strike is typical of the attitude of a large
section of our alleged democracy towards all
the rest. To them it is nothing that a big
part of the public is gravely inconvenienced,
that a lot of people are already wondering
whether they can continue to make ends
meet, and that famine, striking at the little
children, may leap on the towns at any
moment. There is no kindliness in the
striker’s method. He neither trusts the
people nor considers their needs; and if his
attitude represents the spirit of the demo-
cracy, it is plain that the bones are in the
tomb, and that the world’s big hopes are
quenched in a very thick darkness. It might,
of course, be argued that the striker, from
definite and exalted notions of right, was
prepared to nail himself together with all the
rest of the people on a cross of suffering to
inaugurate something kindlier, higher and
holier in the future. But there is no indi-
cation at all of this. On the evidence, he
is ready to inflict any suffering on his fellow
citizens to gain a victory on a comparatively
small point—a victory that with a very little
exercise of patience could have been won
without serious loss or inconvenience to any-
body. No one credits the mine-owners, who
are the other parties to the shame of holding
up the public, with any altruistic motives.
The owners are frankly out. for gain, and
have never professed to be either kindly in
spirit or anxious for the public good ; yet
their action in the national crisis compares
favorably with those of the men who do pro-
fess to believe that the rule of the people
makes for the greatest good of the greatest
number. With the finding of the body in the
tomb the Christian system was alleged to
have fallen. Who is to say that with the
finding that the idea behind democracy is a
myth the w r hole structure of ballot-boxes,
leagues, unions, parliaments and the like
does not come down with a run, and that
Australia’s voice may not be heard insist-
ently demanding one strong man with a
whip to take charge of everything? Demo-
cracy is on its trial. If it has no good feel-
ing towards itself, nothin? can save it from
deserved destruction. Somewhere out of the
gloom a livht may suddenly flash forth and a
glory of high and kindly purpose animate the
mass of men now bent on the ruin of their
fellow workers; but at present it is very
dark, and the price even of feeble candles
has gone up a halfpenny per pound.
THE ENEMY IN THE HOUSE.
If the coal-owners shut down the mines,
threw thousands of people out of work,
plunged the public and private, enterprises of
the country into chaos, and forced the in-
offensive citizen to cook his meals on a fork
in the back yard, the yell from an outraged
nation would blow the roof off. If in addi-
tion to doing these things the aforesaid
owners offended against the community
while they were parties to legal proceedings,
and further announced that they were deter-
mined not to argue about the matter, and
that the Court and the community could go
to blazes, the subsequent proceedings would
be swift and definite. Seven Parliaments
would rush into special session, there would
be earnest talk about rescuing a vitally-
necessary commodity from the clutches of a
gang of pirates, the community’s interest in the
coal deposits would find immediate recogni-
tion, and amidst the applause of an infuri-
ated populace Fat would be torn from his
place of authority and treated as a public
enemy. There may at times be moral differ-
ences between such an outrageous abuse of
power by colliery owners and the action of
miners in goinsr out on strike. Where the
workers have a solid grievance, and have
exhausted the means at their disposal in
vain effort to get it remedied, this is cer-
tainly the case. But so far as the perform-
ance now in progress throughout Australia
is concerned, this paper has searched, with-
out success, for a reason why it should feel
happy because the community is being
throttled by the miners instead of by the
bosses.
* * * *
The claims that the men have made may
be proper. They may not. There is no way
of determining the point until the case has
been stated and replied to, and all the rele-
vant facts investigated. The bald asser-
tion that the men are striking _ for “eight
hours” settles nothing, because it leaves a
lot out of the calculation. What they cer-
tainly did was to ask the Arbitration Court
to place a special construction on “eight
hours,” and while the claim was pending
knocked-off work and started to find out if
they were strong enough to do without the
Court. The Bulletin expresses no opinion
about the claims of the miners on the colliery
proprietors, because the evidence is not forth-
coming. It does not believe that at the
moment they are of much importance, any-
way, or that they are the real foundation of
this disturbance. But this paper has very
strong opinions about the miners’ withdrawal
of their claims from settlement by decent,
civilised means; about their reckless blow at
thousands of other workers; their clamorous
resolution to prove that Labor is a cannibal;
the flagrant dishonesty of their talk about
being “refused justice” when they are sub-
jecting, without cause, the whole body of
Australian citizenship to injustices of the
grossest kind. Taking all the circumstances
of the day and hour into consideration, this
hold-up of the coal supply is an anti-social
act which no good Australian or sane Labor
man can view without much regret and some
disgust. The full' tragedy of the thing is not
on the surface.
* * * *
The position would be different if the com-
munity’s interest in the mining industry had
been disregarded by Parliament and the
electors— if the miners were working under
conditions for which their fellow-citizens had
shown themselves disinclined to accept re-
sponsibility. But it is not so. The Aus-
tralian miner has received from the people
of Australia more than the miner anywhere
else in the world, and he has answered by
striking a heavy blow at the worker in other
callings and at the country’s institutions. To
say that he was compelled to do this because
the community refused him justice is wickedly
untrue. It is a scandalous libel on the Aus-
tralian democracy to assert that the miners
of the country could only get a fair deal by
making war upon the State. What is indis-
putable is that the miners have repudiated the
claims and rights of the rest of the commu-
nity, declared that the State has no other
function than to enforce their demands on the
spot, and have set out to measure their
strength as industrial and political bullies.
If that were not the case, why decline to
accept the mediation of the State and take
up the bludgeon and the sandbag instead?
The public has heard of resolutions being
passed congratulating these “wage slaves”
on their magnificent demonstration of soli-
darity in “fighting for a great principle.”
The solidarity can be readily admitted, but
The Bulletin doubts whether a fight to
uppercut Labor principles looks any better
because a section of organised Labor is the
assailant than if Fat was guilty of it.
Nothing that has happened during the last
week or two changes the view of this paper
that the interests of the general body of the
people and of the nation are of more import-
ance than the affairs of the coal-owners or of
the miners or of any other section of the indus-
trial outfit. That theory is at the bottom of
all social progress, and will survive denials
by every combination of capitalists and
Labor unions on earth. Any action on the
part of Capital or Labor or the State rest-
ing on other considerations must in the end
be fatal to the cause in which it is taken.
A community that can be terrorised by a crowd
in moleskins is no better off than one that
can be driven by a mob in silk hats. Nor is
Labor using the advantages granted to it by
law to strangle the communal life and sand-
bag the industrial activities of a State
any more pleasant to contemplate than
Capital engaged on a same contract.
Strikes are. as foreign to the spirit which
gave birth to the better conception of citizen-
ship as retention of the duel would be where
a police court flourishes. And any settle-
ment of the present trouble except through
negotiation between the parties on the lines
contemplated by the Arbitration Act will in
the long run be a bad thing for Labor, and a
blow at the political and social ideals for
which Labor is supposed to stand.
AN APOLOGY FOR WINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Between Genoa and Turin and on the
watersheds of the Garonne, Dordogne and
Gironde, winter shows the serried rows of
dried sticks which next spring buds, summer
ripens, and autumn reaps in the wine that
is the life of man —a resurrection of the
dead, as Greece ascribed the vintage to the
blood of Dionysius. Old Noah, too,
built his boat, and, in the comforting vanity
that he had saved the world, planted his
vineyard, pressed the new wine and drank
it in celebration. The accidental fermenta-
tion of wild honey was the beginning of
mead which is the father of all beer ; but the
cultivation of the grape which is long to
maturity began the scientific agriculture
which is the beginning of civilisation. To
forget wine is to forget half of early chem-
istry. These old monks and alchemists,
searching for a universal solvent, found alco-
hol. the universal element, present in air,
fresh water and all vegetable matter; pre-
sent in the human body naturally; giving
even a reaction from clay, so that the pro-
hibitionist and the extreme wowser, being
dead, must either be burned by alcoholic
wood or buried in alcoholic earth.
The truth is, though, that the prohibi-
tionist and the extreme wowser are not
against alcohol, but against anybody who
requires and can absorb more alcohol than
they themselves. Some teetotallers are con-
tent with the alcohol of ginger-beer, others
with the alcohol derived from lollies)
Another requires a liquor of six or 10 or 15
per cent.; yet shall the first two demand
that the third shall get his alcohol in ginger-
beer and lollies or be prohibited of any. And
the teetotallers who drink patent medicines,
many of them of greater alcoholic strength
than most wines and many whiskies, are as
intolerant as the ginger-beer and lolly drunk-
ards. If the people with the greater powers
of alcoholic absorption were to talk of for-
cibly feeding the prohibitionists and the
wowser with drinks of high alcoholic
strength, as they might logically do in re-
taliation, there w T ould be a revolution.
Meantime of all alcoholic drinks the wines
of Australia are expected to be most apolo-
getic in their own country, although they
boldly offer themselves and are appreciated
abroad. The finest aid the teetotallers can
give to temperance is to help these
wines against the heavily-alcoholised
spirits of the old world, which health,
climate and experience should make the
Australian shun. Yet so slowly do the old
bad habits die that even the teetotallers are
more ready to tolerate 20 per cent, alcohol
in an imported gin than to see our own
light wines reach their proper position as a
beverage of the table in the home.
If any fair man is asked to point to the
sanest, most artistic, most sober and most
patriotic nation of the earth he will turn
immediately to France. And the French
consumption of wine per head is 30.8 gal-
lons per year, as against 25.1 gallons for
Italy, 18.5 for Spain and 17.1 gallons for
Portugal, the other conspicuously sober
nations of Europe. A long way after them
comes Austria with 3.9 gallons, and then
the two heavy-drinking nations, Germany
with 1.45 gallons and Britain with one-third
of a gallon. Despite the comparative new-
ness of the industry and the dead hand of
imported habit, Australia does better than
these with 1.30 gallons per head. When
Australia reaches the Italian figure Aus-
tralians will be one of the most sober peoples
of the earth.
The Champagne country of France covers
the country of the Marne, Haute Marne,
Aube and Ardennes, that beautiful region
of which much has been laid waste by the
beer-drinking German. A pity that the
fighting was not in a distillery or a ginger-
beer factory! Champagne as a wine was
discovered when Don Perignon, of the
abbey near Hautvilliers, invented the use
of cork as a bottle-stopper, thereby confining
the carbonic-acid gas. Then, of course, be-
cause nobody else had thought of corks, the
little wowsers of the time said that the
wine of Champagne was an invention of the
Devil and had come direct from Hell. And
champagne is a wine that is the Devil to a
full-blooded man, filling his veins with fish-
hooks. The Australians as a meat-eating
race are generally full-blooded, wherefore the
wines demanded for them are the light
clarets of certain valleys in the Eastern
States of Australia, and the hocks of. sun-
light, color and bouquet like the air of
Riverina.
Bordeaux takes our heavy wines by
the million gallons and sends it out again,
but not as Australian. The beverage wines
of the Garonne, Dordogne and Gironde—to
take the most representative of the wine
regions—are grown on light soils of gravel,
limestone, iron and clay. Australia, the oldest
country geologically on earth, has soils
stronger than those of Europe, where almost
every cultivable acre has been worked and
cropped and triturated for at least a cen-
tury or two. Our soils of lime and iron
produce clarets that are a burgundy, and
burgundies as strong as ports. But down
in many river valleys, where time and
weather and water-action have sifted and
diluted the calcareous and ferruginous soils
to silt, grow the wines that shall make Aus-
tralia a sober wine drinker and not a heavy
consumer of ardent spirits. The vineyards
are in existence and increasing; it only
requires the quicker education of public
opinion to bring the increased trade which
will provide the profit, and that in turn will
permit the general application of the only
betterment our Australian wine manufactu-
rer generally requires—the money (which
means the time) to give the wine a decent
age.
In Tuscany, where the grape is the chief
beauty of the three productive seasons of
life —the cirrous vines are there trained in
orchards from tree to tree because land is
dear —poverty makes the peasants drink in
January the wine of November. Probably
for this reason olive oil is used so freely, as
a corrective as much as a food. But Aus-
tralia is not poor, and with a great demand
there is no reason why the commonest bev-
erage wine should not be two years old
before it comes to table. The teetotallers
of South Australia directed much of their
hatred to all wines because of “pinky,” the
new vat-juice which was really not a wine
at all. But the wowser public opinion which
governs all that a man shall drink, while
caring nothing for the quality of that he
shall eat, has been against the marketing of
wine under proper conditions, in the very
correct fear that proper conditions of sale
would give the national beverage a national
popularity. In all Sydney there are prob-
ably not a dozen decent wineshops ; the
great bulk of them are run as a side-line by
harpies who bring in two other harpies to
drink out of the gentleman’s bottle —with
“Another kind love, my dear!” And the
wowsers prefer such conditions to decent
ways of retailing which would give the
national drink the result of its merits.
And its merits must win, in face of the
bad old habits of the old world that are
naturally opposed to sane life in Australia,
in face of the opposition of the drinker of
alcohol who finds his alcohol in lollies and
ginger- beer, and calls himself a teetotaller
for that reason.
This, which began as an apology for Aus-
tralian wines, ends as an apology for the
liquor-control authority; for the blind re-
formers who fight the good of the liquor
question as well as the evil of it; and for
the conservative w r ho drinks heavy spirits of
the North Atlantic in a temperate zone. All
these intentional and tacit oppositionists of
Australian wines are keeping thousands of
acres of vineyard out of cultivation in favor
of a few ships carrying whisky and gin, and
of the few wharf-lumpers who sling the kegs
and cases from hold to wharf. The fact that
the money for most of these un-Australian
drinkings is sent out of Australia doesn’t
seem to matter; but all the /ssame it needs an
apology of its very own. ■ }
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Constitutional Protection of a
Decorative Myth.
The P.L.L. having presented a petition to
Governor Stkickxand urging him to refuse
assent to the Bill prolonging the life of
the N. S. Wales Assembly, Premier Hol-
man. worked off some jocose remarks about
the inconsistency of people who urged the
abolition of the office of State Governor and
yet appealed to the Cocked Hat for assist-
ance. Why? There was nothing the mat-
ter with the P.L.L. petitioning the Gover-
nor, and no sane person ever suggested that
if the post of Governor was abolished the
constitutional place it allegedly occupies
should be left a gaping void for tricky poli-
ticians to conceal themselves in beyond
reach of the electors. As long as the State
has a Governor exercising certain func-
tions or pretending to, the electors who
believe that these activities should be exer-
cised through another agency have no more
to be. ashamed of in conforming with con-
stitutional procedure than in obeying a
law they believe to be unjust. That the
petition was a futility was not the fault
of the P.L.L. It had to appeal to an offi-
cial appointed by an outside authority and
bound down to accept the advice of Min-
isters even where this involves stealing
the people’s votes —all of which merely
goes to strengthen the case against
the Cocked Hat as an institution. It is
worse than useless. Under a more rational
system the P.L.L. would have been able
to lodge a document in the Supreme Court
calling on Holman to show cause why he
should not be restrained from prigging the
ballot-box; or, what would be even better,
the electors on petition would be able to
force the . Assembly to resign. As things
are, the citizens have no protection against
Parliament; and as institutions which are
responsible only to themselves are sure to
become tyrannies, the sooner the electors
wake up to the need for the referendum-
initiative the better. In the name of Demo-
cracy the Cocked Plat has been converted into
a constitutional myth-—and myths are not
much good to people who have to deal with
politicians. Anything less than a axe is a
nuisance.
«
Wanted—An Official Executioner.
The patience of the Australian people in the
hands of the looters and messers who are
running the country’s affairs is as amazing
as their readiness to go off into hysterics
about the price of cream of tartar. Every
day the evidence is accumulating that in each
State and in the Federal domain there is
waste of the most appalling character. The
evidence taken lately by the Commission in-
quiring into the state of affairs at Canberra
reads like the story of an organised effort
to reduce the taxpayers to beggary. This is
what the chief storekeeper of the Melbourne
Metropolitan Board of Works, who was
instructed to visit the scene, 1 reports that
he found: —
The value of the plant and stores was £88,274.
Although work had been in progress for some years
it was only recently that anything like a record of
the goods received had been made, with the result
that it was extremely difficult to trace anything
purchased prior to March, 1916. At present (exclu-
sive of material on outside works, purchases for
stock, cement, timber, fencing material and plant),
there was lying at the dep6t, unused, material to
the value of over £28,000. The bulk of this
material had either been ordered in excess of re-
quirements, or when received found unsuitable for
the works. In many cases similar material had
been ordered for new works when the material there
might have been used if proper supervision had been
exercised.... A large and valuable stock of elec-
trical materials was lying at the depot. Much of
it had been kept on hand for a considerable time.
No record was kept of it when used, the men
taking what they required without check of any
kind....
Early in the history of the Federal Territory
several contracts were let, for various kinds of
timber, with the object of having seasoned timber
available for the works. No steps were taken to
make proper provision for storing this material, and
even the ordinary precautions were not taken. In
those circumstances it was not to be wondered at
that a large quantity of it would be absolutely
wasted. In addition, a large proportion of it was
affected with dry rot and insects.
Owing to the careless manner in which the cement
was stacked, before an accurate tally could be taken
a large proportion of it had to be shifted and
restacked. It was found ! on comparing the tally
with the cards that there was a discrepancy of 4663
bags. Under present conditions there was practically
no supervision or control over the stores and plant.
And so it goes on. Every time an investi-
gation is made of the working of a State
or Federal department there is the same
yarn of waste and muddle and loss. If
Private Enterprise was forming himself into
trusts and combines to loot the public the
electors would rise up in their might and
drive out the politicians who allowed the
steal to go on. Yet all this Government
waste represents a steal as gigantic as any-
thing ever perpetrated at Chicago. Every
Prime Minister and Premier invents a new
portfolio or two. This paper implores the
next man to appoint a Minister equipped
with a guillotine and statutory authority
to use it on public servants who rob their
masters at a time when, of all others, they
ought to most sedulously guard their inter-
ests.
The Hun’s Attack on J. Bull’s Butcher
and Baker.
It seems as if it is going to be the Corned
Beef of Old England this Christmas, and
for a day or two later. The Hun submarine
is knocking a large hole in John’s pantry,
and it is no good pretending that the per-
formance makes the situation look any more
cheerful. Perhaps John sees already that it
would be very much better for him if he
had contracted the habit of raising his own
steak instead of pheasants. An abattoir in
the Highlands would nowadays be a lot
more useful than a Yankee millionaire’s
shooting box, and animals that could be
driven in by a dog preferable to wide,
branching horns which have to be pursued
with telescope and rifle. But regrets on
that subject are nc\t going to keep John’s
belt tight, and there is nothing for it but a
systematic distribution of the tucker that
escapes the Hun’s torpedo. How much
pinch under the waist-line is being felt in
Britain in consequence of inflated prices
and vanished cargoes is a matter for specula-
tion. Probably not much —yet. But the six-
penny egg. the appointment of a Food
Commission, the promulgation of regula-
tions about the use of flour, the intimation
conveyed to hotelkeepers that they must
carve the menu down, and Walter
Runciman’s warning that there may be
fishless and meatless days ahead, all point
to the conclusion that large appetites are
not the nation’s most valuable asset just
now ; and the production of munitions for the
community’s innards appears not unlikely
to become as great a problem as the produc-
tion of shells for the guns on the Somme.
All these things ought to have some mean-
ing for us in Australia. If they don’t
satisfy us of the extreme seriousness of the
position nothing can do it. Yet matters will
almost certainly go on getting more serious;
wherefore the urgency of hitting swiftly
with all our strength. The reinforcements
we send away a year hence, two years
hence, may be too late.
The Union that Destroys Strength.
Of the antis who lately complained that the
war is robbing the country of industrial
workers, not one has been heard to suggest
that their non-enlisted brothers, who run no
risk of losing life or limb in the trenches,
should at least be willing to lose a little
recreation, or put more industry into their
ordinary daily work. There is but one way
to make up the deficit in national production
due to the exodus of workers who were ready
and willing to fight for Civilisation, and that
is by increasing the output of the workers
who remain in the Commonwealth. It is
for them to say whether they prefer to work
longer or work stronger, and it is time that
they admitted themselves in honor bound to
adopt one or other alternative. There is a
lamentable rule of loafing in most of the
factories of Private Enterprise; but in those
cases the taxation falls only on the profits
of the individual employers. The slacking
in Government employ, on the other hand,
falls on the incomes of taxpayers in general. It
is wicked slacking very often, but the truth
about it is never fully revealed in Parlia-
ment or on the platform, because the publica-
tion of details would bring wrath upon
the informers’ heads. In many cases, the very
foreman whose duty it should be to keep slack-
ers up to the mark has no compunction about
bringing an honest worker down to the rogue’s
level. Yet Treasurers, whilst imposing new
taxation to meet further expenditure, have
the audacity to declare that economy is being
duly studied. The scandal cannot go on for
ever. The war debts which this country is
incurring must be paid by increased produc-
tion of wealth. Australia must work harder
without necessarily forfeiting its claim to be
the workers’ paradise. If the present bosses
of trades unionism are committed to the Go
Slow doctrine, and cannot be dissuaded from
prostituting a noble cause to the requirements
of. ignoble hangers-on. the Governments,
being the biggest employers of labor, must
fight the tyrants instead of being co,wed by
them. At the present time, under Slacker
rule, Australian unionists are living in a
fools’ paradise rather than the paradise of
the working man.

The Cost of Living—and of Wasting.
The latest promise by Hughes is to do
something or other to prevent the price of
commodities soaring up further and to shield
the community from the high freight curse.
This paper wishes him luck. It is easy to
talk about, anyway. But there are one or two
matters which no amount of conversation
regarding the cauliflower and the rasher
should be allowed to obscure. The amount
the public has to pay for food and rent and
other things is important, but the quantity
of cash that is taken from the people’s wages
by way of taxation is something they are
equally interested in. Hughes ought to
look into this business and investigate some
of the expenditure of public money by his
own and the six other Ministries. He will
there find evidence of an extravagance
unparalleled in the history of the country.
While Australia has been engaged in war,
heaping up an enormous debt, the Federal
and other Governments have gone wildly on
increasing their 'expenditure from month to
month. To permit this lunacy to proceed, and
at the same time babble about the high price
of bread and chops and American salmon,
would look about as sensible as to climb on
top of a locomotive boiler and maunder about
the high temperature. Hughes may be able
to do something in the way of keeping some
prices down. It will be a big contract, and
involves getting right down to the rockbottom
of production and distribution. But any-
thing he may do in that direction will be a
futility unless the shocking waste of the
public estate and national earnings now in
progress is checked. Hughes knows this,
and The Bulletin has no reason to suspect
that he does not foresee the squall Australia
is going to be swept by. But it would like
to be sure that he has the grit to start cut-
ting the top hamper away before the storm
breaks. If he has not, his experiments as a
price-fixer are going to be of less service to
Australia than a shower of rain.
+
Coal is Black.
The accident by which somebody discovered
a coal seam instead of stumbling on the
principle of deriving electricity from waste
water-power has been responsible for a heap
of things—including the big strike which
has been barking the shins of the Common-
wealth. Coal is supposed to play a big part
in civilisation, but it is difficult to see, in
spite. of all the wheels that are set flying by
coal-induced steam, how anyone can argue
that the coal-miner is taking his place as a
civilised man. Dirty, always dirty, with
smarting eyes and roughened hands and, a
raw chest, he is shut away for long hours
from sun and air and all that gives sweet-
ness and color to life; and if after years of
that sort of thing he isn’t the gentlest and
most contented person in the State, the fact
seems in no way remarkable. If this old
world is to get any sort of decent move on
in the direction even of a very second-class
millennium it will have to concern itself about
eliminating some of the rougher varieties of
toil. While any large proportion of the citi-
zens of a democratic State are engaged in
work of a degrading nature —of a degrading-
nature because it lowers the human standard
and shuts its victims out from the finer
things of life —it is impossible that the demo-
cracy should reach its highest and best. Fill
a man’s ears and eyes and lungs with coal-
dust, and he must inevitably think and act
to some extent in keeping with the ceaseless
irritation. Put a man in an atmosphere of
unpleasant smells and hideous sights, as we
do with those who slaughter our daily beef
and mutton, and in the course of years the
thing must get into his soul and discolor
all his thought. There is no getting away
from the fact that the ideal of an educated,
thoughtful, sympathetic democracy is practi-
cally impossible while coal remains black
and occupations which were once the duties
of degraded slaves must be performed by per-
sons with full franchise and standing on
civic equality with their employers. Demo-
cracy is hideously wrong when it regards
the ballot-box as the be-all and end-all of
things. An electric current, properly ap-
plied, may have more national uplift than
all the political organisations and convinc-
ing propagandist speeches and printed works
on world-reform. To get coal nominally
white by a process of better conditions for
workers and higher wages is at best only a
temporary expedient. Coal is black and will
remain black whatever theoretical color the
Unions like to paint it.
The Army Remount.
“Aneb” : The Defence Department talks of
raising the standard of the Australian army
remount, and to that end has linked arms
with various agricultural societies and race
clubs which provide rich prizes for the
fastest mokes. It doesn’t matter if the win-
ners be broken-winded or spavin-hocked—•
the money goes to the fastest. On Cup Day
we saw a mean, undersized, curby-hocked
gelding make every other competitor look
like animated rubbish in the big handicap.
Our agricultural societies are just about as
useful. One sees a prize-winning “heavy-
weight hack” collar the cash and ribbon
among “ladies’ backs” two minutes later —
and nobody seems to spot the joke. These
societies consider the cavalry charger and*
artillery wheeler one and the same thing. I
have been asked to judge classes for Indian
remounts which the catalogue said “must
be over 16 hands.” And the Indian Gov-
ernment won’t buy a remount over 16
hands! If the Defence Department will
only state what it wants and is prepared
to pay, and employ reputable and experienced
buyers, breeders will accommodate them if
it’s made worth their while. There is no
coherence in the Departmental buying at
present. I have seen two members of a
Purchase Board inspect a mob and do a deal,
and a short time after —not long enough
for prices or mokes to improve appreciably
—another two members have inspected the
remains of the same mob and paid more
money for the previous buyers’ rejects. In-
cidentally if the Department can popularise
the breeding and use of mules it will do the
country a vast amount of good. This would
utilise thousands of unsound mares. (Ill-
shaped and unsound mares do not transmit
their beauty spots when bred to a donkey.)
But, one minute! We must educate Bill-
Jiat to the use of them at the same time.
I once told an Australian employee in
Africa to saddle a mule and come along.
“Not me!” he growled. “Do you take me
for a white Kaffir or a ruddy g'aucho?” But
I doubt whether we can reasonably expect
anything from the Defence Department as
at present run. A couple of weeks ago what
the Argus called “an important conference”
was held in Melbourne to consider raising
the general standard of the Australian horse,
having special regard to remounts. The
chairman was Brigadier-General Stanley,
who admittedly knows nothing of the sub-
ject. And the entire personnel of the con-
ference was almost as ridiculous.
The Adelaide Advertiser gave a general
support to the State Government’s amuse-
ment tax as an emergency measure; and the
amusement managers have replied by unani-
mously withdrawing their advts. Naturally
the Advertiser feels hurt. But the public
has a right to feel hurt, too. For this is not
an attack merely upon Langdon Bony-
ti-ion’s pocket. It is an obvious attempt to
make the press say what the advertiser
wants it to say, to advocate what he wants
it to advocate, and to deceive the public by
representing somebody else’s perverted judg-
ment as the paper’s own unbiased view on
a matter of public policy. In the opinion of
the amusement managers of Adelaide a
newspaper, so far from being an honest
guide to the public, ought to be a prostitute:
and if it refuses to be a prostitute, then in
their judgment it ought to be punished.
Whether men who hold such immoral
opinions are fit persons to have charge of the
entertainment of the public is a matter which
that public might very properly consider.
Prima facie they are not.
Interesting yarn from the Department of
External Affairs: —
You will recollect that some months since I was
in communication with you in regard to an article
which had appeared in The Bulletin of the 17th
February last relating to an advertisement which
had recently appeared in American papers regarding:
alleged vacancies for service with a company-
described as the “Wallaroo Moonta and Northern
Railway Company of Australia.” The article sug-
gested that as there was no such company iif Aus-
tralia the whole thing was a German plot designed
to put a stop to recruiting in Australia.
The matter was taken up by this Department, and
the British Ambassador at Washington was asked
to assist in ascertaining the identity of the persons
responsible for the advertisement and take any
action that might be possible with a view of pre-
venting any further prejudicial action of this kind
against the Empire.
The result of the inquiries shows that the man
at the back of the movement was one calling himself
Middaugh, an American who had been employed on
railway construction work in Mexico and Brazil,
and who has several times visited Europe. Middaugh
stated that he represented the firm of Norton
Griffiths, of London, and professed to be desirous
of taking with him to Australia as many as pos-
sible of the men who had worked under him before
in Brazil and Mexico. He seemed to be a man of
the world and gave the impression of being a
responsible individual. One of the persons employed
and defrauded by Middaugh made a statement to
the effect that his experience was practically the
same as that of numerous others who were engaged
by Middaugh. Several of them actually came from
distant States to Chicago in order to fulfil their
engagement. A strange feature of Middaugii’s pro-
ceedings is that in some cases he took deposits from
acquaintances whom he had known before, and in
others he refused deposits from total strangers.
Middaugh had absconded and was still at large;
it is said that he made between three and four
thousand dollars out of the fraud.
While working on his fraudulent scheme Middaugh.
occupied desk room in a respectable office in Chicago.
He seemed to court inquiry and actually called al
the British Consulate on one occasion to show what
purported to be a telegram from Norton Griffiths,
entrusting him with the work. He stated that he
did this as he had heard that quite a number of
applicants were approaching the Consulate with a
view of obtaining information in regard to the
railway. On several occasions the Consul was
approached for information, but he always dis-
claimed all knowledge of the movement.
The Consul states that it is not generally the
view that Middaugh’s movement was in any way
connected with German activities in America, and
that it is almost universally attributed to a deli-
berate intent to raise money by fraud. The
Ambassador shares this opinion.
But that was only part of the story which
The Bulletin told on February 17.
Whether Middaugh was a fraud or not
GernAan agents in America saw their oppor-
tunity, and sent large numbers of copies of
the advt. into Australia along with this
circular
The contracts entered into at the agents’ office in
Chicago are for a period of two years certain, with
an extension to five years. So when the heroes return
from the trenches at the end of the war they will
have the doubtful pleasure of walking the streets
looking for work, their families starving, during
which time the American blackleg will have a good
position and have been able to ship home a goodly
portion of the wages he has received during the
time the Australian workman has been giving his
time and risking his life for the defence of the
Empire.
A money-making rogue -wouldn’t have sent
thht kind of circular to Australia. No! —if
German agents didn’t hatch the fraud they
promptly made use of it.

“Globe” : When the Navy Wireless Depart-
ment has a few minutes to spare, it might
ask itself why Billjim, returning to Aus-
tralia on a hospital ship, should be charged
lid. per word for his winged message home.
The usual rate on Australian- and M.L.-
registered ships is 6d. per word. To charge
poor old Bill nearly double seems too much
like penalising him for fighting for his coun-
try. Bill’s lid. is cut up this way. The
Private Enterprise which owns the ship-
wireless installation grabs 4d. f that being
the maximum allowed by law. The Com-
monwealth coast station pockets 6d., which
is twice as much as would be required from
Bill as a private citizen fighting nobody’s
battles and voting “No.” Finally, the
Postal Department, which delivers the mes-
sage, demands a penny a word for the job.
It is scandalously unfair and I feel like a
bushranger every time I have to explain the
outrage. I’m a hospital ship’s operator.
An American hits back: —
George Meudell’s account of the use of electricity
generated by water-power in the United States, and
the contemporary lack of it in Australia, is sugges-
tive. But there is a vital difference between the two
countries. The U.S.A., whatever their faults, believe
in people and work and the development and utilisa-
tion of everything that tends to make men pros-
perous and productive. Judged by its actions Aus-
tralia believes in keeping a large, rich continent
almost empty and a large percentage of the inhabi-
tants doing nothing. There isn’t as much water-
power in Australia as in some countries, but in pro-
portion to population there is a good deal. To the
average American a huge country with practically
nobody in it and that didn’t want any more, and
whose motto was “Give me a spell,” would seem
the maddest concern under Heaven. .But, then, the
Yankee mind is a warped article with a pathetic
belief that man’s first duty in the world
is to do something and improve something
and wrestle with something. If he had
arrived, many years ago, at the conclusion that a
million people were enough for his country, that a
policy of borrowing and importing was best for it
and that any industry except pawnbroking was un-
important, comparisons between the two countries
might be allowable. Unfortunately, Uncle Sam’s
adherence to the policy of work instead of shirk has
made comparison not Only odoriferous but posi-
tively useless. Wilson may be “too proud to
fight”; but, anyhow, Wilson’s countrymen are not
too proud to toil.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Francis Joseph of Austria died November 21.
The grey king flutters out. The world goes
on
The stricken legions stagger, drunk with
pain.
Scarce knowing that a inighty chief is gone,
They face the front of battle loud and vain.
Hunger with bitter grip holds town and vale.
Kings pass. But woe it is that bread should
fail.
He made this war. A catspaw in the hand
Of mightier plotters, on the fatal gong
His withered fist smote out across the land
The jarring clamor of a brazen song ;
And flinging down the gage to force and fate
He coughed and trembled his thin hymn of
hate.
The world that rioted in turn and change
Had borne him on upon its mounting wave,
An ancient emblem in a setting strange.
A luckless thing that tottered to a grave.
He made the war. Hunger, defeat and
shame
Write their slow epitaph below his nhme.
N.S.W.
C. O. M.

+
What is happening in Roumania is put for-
ward as proof of the German’s despair. As
he had been getting such a doing at various
places it is represented that he came to the
conclusion that the only way to save his
vanishing reserves was to throw them all at
Roumania and achieve a victory. This may
be right enough, but there seem to be one
or two holes in the yarn. For instance, it is
not beyond the region of possibility that the
German thought he was strong enough not
only to hold on to what he had before, but
also to grab some more; and he certainly
has gone a considerable distance towards
getting what he was after. If this means
that he is despairing of himself, the less he
does of it the better for us. Meanwhile all
the things that were going to occur when
Roumania butted in have been postponed,
and a fresh set of circumstances have com-
menced to develop with great rapidity. How
much nearer this brings the end The
Bulletin will not try to guess; but it seems
a reasonably fair thing to suspect (1)
that peace is not yet on the horizon, (2)
that winning the war still involves a tremen-
dous amount of hard work, and (3) that
Germany is going to win if its armies are
more effectively reinforced and employed
than ours, and if it has the greatest measure
of sustained and organised national service
behind its armies. It may be just as well
to stop there, and let the last-mentioned fact
soak in for a week.
+
There is to be another announcement this
week as to Australia’s “policy” with regard
to the reinforcing of the army. The appoint-
ment of a Director-General'of Recruiting is
spoken of, and there is to be more canvass-
ing and all the rest of it. The net result will
be an appearance of mild activity, an in-
crease of officials, the writing of vast num-
bers of reports—and the capture of an occa-
sional Billjim. The absurd; performance
in connection with the “call-up” ; the exemp-
tion tribunals; the gaolings, censorings and
strikes; the pathetic,.spectacle presented by
the Federal and State Parliaments; the
wicked stories told about the progress of the
war and the condition of the enemy, while
the truth was shamefully suppressed—all
these incidents have created throughout Aus-
tralia an atmosphere far more unfavorable
to recruiting than prevailed originally. There
is no reason why we should go on fooling
ourselves any more or putting up an elabo-
rate sham. For this and other reasons The
Bulletin suggests that a proclamation or
post-card or something should be issued
calling upon every person willing to go to tbe
assistance of Billjim to deliver himself at
some specified address on or before February
1. Whereupon he should be taken into camp,
and with the numerous other persons who
are in khaki —including a few who have been
in khaki from near the beginning—sent for-
ward to the place where they are wanted.
The whole blessed thing could be washed up
within six weeks, and instead of wasting
time on what threatens to develop into a
mockery the Commonwealth could say:
“There, that’s the end ! We can do no more
that is worth bothering about” —which
would be honest and true, even if it
sounded rotten. Then the Australian people
would no longer be burdened with Parlia-
merits making use of the war as a cover for
shirking, and could apply themselves to the
consideration of the tremendous problems
the war has created and is still creating.
There is much to be gained by this. There is
a lot to be lost by neglecting it in order to
spend a long time finding out what everyone
knows already.

“The Orchid,” revived at Melbourne
Majesty’s last Saturday, is a little more than
12 years old in Australia, and it wears fairly
well. The Bulletin of “The Orchid’s” first
period remarked upon its color, movement,
catchy melody and George Lauri. One
mourns the loss of Lauri and his full round
voice every time Phil Smith appears as
Meakin, the unique gardener in a bright
green apron, red hair and crushed straw-
berry countenance, who rears the bulb
whereby Bantock, the British Minister of
Commerce, wins a wager from an incredible
little Frenchman. But one misses more than
Lauri. There is a prevailing lack of voice in
the present company. For instance, Evelyn
Scott, Celia. Ghiloni, Connie Milne, W. S.
Percy and Fred Leslie of yore were a dis-
tinctly louder lot of principals than Minnie
Love, Addie Lennard, Nellie Payne, Clyde
Cook and Charles Albert, though the old lot
were not as well placed in every case as their
successors. The ditty of “Little Mary” as
Minnie Love sings it sounds all the better
for a mild and gentle rendering. And then
there is Ethel Cadman, tame and tuneful, in
the part where Florence Young used to be a
perturbed bride, fated to trip to Nice with
the wrong bridegroom. Florence is now an
amorous Caroline, wooed by Meakin by false
pretences and licensed to utter the broadest
remarks in the show. The character in which
tall Clara Clifton was quite at home comes
new and"strange to Miss Young, and it is
hard to accept her as a lovesick, brainless
spinster, seeking the original of a fraudulent
photo, and quite prepared to buy the best
imitation she can get. Nevertheless her vital-
ity and volume of voice are welcome in any
part. Her exclamation. “Naughty!” is a
prodigious hit. Leslie Holland is a success
as the goose-stepping dude, and Reg Roberts
