At the Sydney Society of Artists show .
The job of an artist is art, and it is a common-
place of art history that wars have reacted hardly
at all upon their contemporary painters. The
absence of war subjects from the Society of Artists’
show at the Queen Victoria Markets was therefore to
be expected, given a competent jury. The main
characteristic of the show is the commendable absence
of the “big” picture. The sole large one, an
“Evening Symphony” by Elliott Gruner, happens
to be the worst of this artist’s panel. It is depress-
ingly big.
The features of the show are Norman Lindsay s
“WHILE THE
BILLY BOILS”
AT MELBOURNE
KING’S.
Joe feels the re-
sponsibilities of
fatherhood.
audacious plunge into water-colors, Hardy Wilson’s
architectural pencil drawings and his panel of
water-colors, Elliott Gruner’s brilliant analysis of
the difficult problem of light, the high quality of
the etchings of John Shirlow, J. B. Godson, Lionel
Lindsay and Sydney Ure Smith, and Norman Lind-
say’s masterpiece in pen-and-ink. The last,
“Apotheosis of Francois Villon,” shows Lindsay’s
hand and his constructive artistic vision at their
highest. “The Law Courts,” by Shirlow, “Old
Bridge,” by J. B. Godson, “Governor Bourke Hotel”
and “The Terrace, Admiralty House,” by Lionel
Lindsay, and “Argvle Cut” and “No. 15, Jamieson-
street,” by Sydney Ure Smith, are the best of a
brilliant etching exhibit. The illustration by George
W. Lambert is obviously far below this artist’s
level.
Howard Ashton exhibits a glowing panel of
brilliant realism in oils, the finest being “Summer
Morning,” and he also essays pastel with apparent
ease. But his direct painting does not attempt to
solve the problem of light to which Gruner bravely
applies himself. Half a dozen of this artist’s paint-
ings of light are permanent achievements. Evidently
Gruner is but entering upon his path. Florence
Rodway’s pastels of children have all their well-
known charm; but from the joy of setting
down • with absolute faithfulness the faces of
children she has turned in one picture to the
problem of the backgrounds. “The New Tea-
pot” is a picture full of atmosphere, not a
sketch. Her large pastel is a failure. George
Dancey shows some unattractive but correct designs.
In the water-colors, John D. Moore exhibits interest-
ing work, and Alfred T. Clint has various so-so
landscapes and a muddy fan. One delightful land-
scape, charmingly composed, represents the best of
B. E. Minns’s small exhibit. Julian Ashton offers
some delicate atmospheric effects. Blamire Young’s
unique enamelled effects are seen in a small collec-
tion. Albert Collis has one delightful impression,
“Morning Light.”
In Norman Lindsay’s dash into water-color he
puts into this medium all the brilliance of his pen-
work ; he tackles problems of atmosphere and com-
position with a zest that carries him through, and
by his own unorthodox methods he gives to his
work an astonishing depth and richness of color.
These pictures of nymphs and nudes challenge every
other picture in the room. But so far his handling
of shadows on flesh is sometimes aggressively hard.
Hardy Wilson’s “The Deserted Home” is a delight-
ful composition. His “Souvenir of Corot’s Concert
Room” is masterly, though one misses any in-
dication of a wall to Corot’s concert room.
But his pencil studies of Georgian architecture in
Australia, which he has delightfully peopled W 1 .
characters in the costumes of that age, stand apar
as an achievement in a most difficult medium.
The merit of Gruner’s work has been acknowledger
by the trustees of the Wynne Art Prize, who have
awarded it to the artist for one of his most
difficult and successful studies, “Morning L>g ■
This is the first time the prize has been won by a
member of the Society of Artists.

On Sydney screens this week: —
Lyceum : “Friday the 13th”—-a romance of the
Stock Exchange, wherein Love collides with the
gamblers of Wall-street; a film of the recent huge
munitions explosion in New York Harbor ; and usual
comedy features.
Crystal Palace: “The Return of ‘Draw’ Egan, ’
the story of the taming of a bandit; and “Man-
hattan Madness,” a Triangle comedy-drama of the
frivolous moments of the U.s. citizen.
“The Sign of the Cross,” at Melbourne
Royal, is again presenting Julius Knight in
the trappings of Nero’s favorite lion. But
Julius is not the Superbus that Melbourne
first saw over 20 years ago. The figure that
broke a thousand hearts is beginning to show
the generosity of luxuriant middle-age. Like-
wise the voice that was beguilement to
Berenice’s wicked ear now lacks the ring of
heartfelt youthful passion. Nevertheless,
Knight is still a Y’ery passable Marcus, and
with the help of pink limelight and classic
garments almost looks the part of a fetching
canoodler when he strives to add the Chris-
tian Mercia to his menu of pagan dainties.
Lizette Parkes’s demeanor as Mercia is an
acceptable mixture of somnambulism and
spiritual exaltation. She is only shaken out
of her attitude of dazed piety by the gaudy
attentions of Glabrio and Superbus and the
insults of the latter’s supper party. And
hereabouts she shows the sign of the cross, so
to speak, rather than the pain of a saint
suffering for the sins of her tormentors.
Jean Robertson, as Berenice, is important in
diction but less striking in deportment;
whereas Eileen Sparks, as the light-hearted
Dacia, conducts herself capitally, and tips
every word with frivolity. Millie Langley
is a well-nourished Stephanus, while the
jealousy of Ancaria is delivered like a verbal
proclamation by Francee Anderson. Elsie
Bellew is mostly purple and purposeless as
Nero’s faded missus, Poppea. The Glabrio
of Eardley Turner has the customary head
of wine, and the legs and language of the
actor furnish the usual corroborative evi-
dence. James Hughes provides a familiar
colored supplement picture of Nero, Hugh
Huntley is gracefully trivial as Philodemus,
Boyd Irwin robs Tigillinus of any character
the role possesses, and Atholwood is artisti-
cally asphyxiated in the small part of Ser-
villius. The hymn-tunes of the faithful con-
tinue to make midnight mournful on the
banks of the Tiber, and an introduced
feature is the brunette pug., Starlight,
wrapped in a tiger-skin and much Sambo
dignity as Berenice’s chair-bearer and gener-
ally useful. Next Saturday “Damaged
Goods” will venture a few remarks on the
syphilis problem.

Besides providing a flicker of mirth in the
concerted work with their serious colleagues,
the three had spasms of frivolity in separate
items, whilst Brennan and Campbell united
with tenor Agnew in a small bit of female
impersonation. This last-mentioned specialty,
however, was not a success. Indeed, almost
anything on the bill was to be preferred to
the trio’s mild efforts to gag as one man and

features of the evening was the profound,
artesian bass revealed bv Ford Waltham in
a song as deep as the bog that William
Morris Hughes has either fallen or been
manoeuvred into.
** * *
“Mr. 44” and “The Broken Law” were
unreeled at Hoyt’s Melbourne Theatres last
Saturday. “Mr. 44,” a Metro production,
discloses the mutual magnetism of Harold
Lockwood and Beverly Bane in four or five
reels of comedy-drama. The feature of the
film is some lake scenery which is too lovely
to serve as a background for hugger-mugger
mechanics like Harold and Beverly. “The
Broken Law,” showing William Farnum and
Dorothy Bernard in the leading rOles, is
a mixture of “Komany Rye,” “Lavengro”
and the producer’s imagination—a fairly-
attractive Fox film, blessed with very good
photography.
The Takinesses, billed as eccentric duet-
tists, are givipg a joint song and zither re-
cital at Melbourne Tivoli this week. The
turn, which comes from South Africa, has
nothing in it but the remarkable vocal manner-
isms of Mr. Takiness, who sounds like a pro-
test in Flemish when he doesn’t more re-
semble an attack of croup. Jamie Kelly, the
alleged “tunnel foreman” from Amurka, will
have to rely on the aroma of his old trade
to give him any individuality. As a mere
singer and raconteur he is unarresting. In
other sectors of the entertainment the
Parshleys are inflicting chastisement on
Liszt’s Rhapsody (No. 2). Togo, the Jap,
makes a thrilling slide for life, and conjurer
Merlin maintains the uneven splutter of his
conversation.

Wirths’ Circus, having been stuck up in
Melbourne by the strike, seems to have pro-
fited by the delay. Public opinion counts for
more than posters when a circus has been
going some weeks on the same site, and
Wirths’ show, lately reinforced by Bailey’s
posing dogs, is well talked about.
All Adelaide theatrical houses continue to
boycott the Advertiser for the stand it took
on the new amusement tax. The advts. of
two city and one suburban shows continue to
appear in it, but all three print solemn
declarations in the other papers that they

have neither authorised nor paid for the
same. Also they, with nearly a dozen others,
issue proclamations that for ' the genuine
article one should go to the Register or
Herald. The boycotted paper has had a
dignified leader, pointing out that advertisers
cannot be allowed to dictate the line to be
followed by a newspaper on a matter of
public policy. Its one error, made at the
beginning of the trouble, was in giving a
thorough slating to a Majestic programme of
quite average merit—somewhat above the
merit of similar programmes to which it
had for .months been giving warm praise. *
The William Anderson Co. is still going
strong in melodrama at Adelaide Tivoli.
George Cross and Agnes Keogh are the latest
additions to the team, and the capable Fitz-
herbert keeps on playing villains, though he
had one pleasant interlude in a comic charac-
ter-part. Another Anderson, James A.,
teacher of elocution, had the unusual experi-
ence of selling out the Queen’s Hall in
advance for his annual dramatic evening, so
mere cash was unavailing as a bribe for
admission. The Majestic has the Le Grohs,
fine acrobats and funny. Marvelle’s cockatoos
and Charley’s dogs imitate the human, and
in return Poincaire imitates animals and
birds. Miss Aird and other clever women
got into trousers and played for charity a
farce adapted from the American by herself;
and the Dandies in the open make the best
of the stormiest November on record. Mary
Pickford is at Wondergraph, Chaplin at
West’s, Tyrone Power at Empire, and a new
girl, Jose Collins, at the Royal.
AT SYDNEY TIVOLI.
Short and Little in an acrobatic exercise.
The 19th “Federal” Art Exhibition in
Adelaide reflects the general feeling in that
city that a new start is necessary. If it
were held only every third year, Sydney and
Melbourne taking their turns in between,
artists might be keener, and the various gal-
leries would get a chance of seeing and
acquiring their work. In this instance,
apart from S. Aus., only Victoria is repre-
sented, and the Adelaide Art Gallery has cut
down its usual annual purchase to two
things—a crayon of a native, by Frederick
Britton, a local man, and a portrait by Miss
Cumbrae Stewart, one more of the many
clever girls-in-black which the Gallery has.
Miss Stewart’s figures are among the best
things in this show. Edward Davis exhibits
three very well-known local men on a lawn;
but the execution is not equal to the enter-
prise. Percy Leason goes to history for one
of the subjects which ought to be so frequent
and tempting, and depicts explorer Stuart
turning back, beaten by thirst. Gustave
Barnes has some striking blackfellows on a
Murray cliff, and a charming bit of subur-
ban scenery; Janies Ashton does nothing
striking, his son Will is away, Hans Heysen
is not represented, and John White has but
a few small things. Other items worth
noting are the European figure-studies of
Marie Tuck, a mistv Ford Paterson from
Gipnsland, Gwen Barringer’s gumtrees in
the Heysen manner, a batch of clever aqua-
tints by Fred Campbell, and flowers by
Miss Bosworth and others. Also Leason
has two clever little panels of Shakspeare
characters, and Miss M. Richardson breaks
new ground with paintinas that look like
illustrations for fairy-stories. It is not a
large exhibition; and the selectors might
well have made it smaller.
Coal may be black at some places but
at Newcastle they swear it is Brown.
Ex-Minister for Lands Niels Nielsen, who
has been serving N. S. Wales at £4 4s. a
day, arrived from America on Tuesday,
Nielsen has no doubt come back to make
certain that the “National” Government
wants him to co-operate in winning the war
by continuing to accept the four guineas
per diem.
Dr. Wade, who is testing the Papuan
strata, _ reports good oil at a depth of 187 ft.
There is another Wade who did some pretty
smart underground boring in N. S. Wales
lately and struck good oil, too. But he
didn’t have to go as deep as the doctor. The
oil came up and met him half-way.
Justice Higgins was right in describing as
a “malignant growth” the system of appoint-
ing “temporary” employees to the Federal
Public Service. The Public Service Commis-
sioner’s last report shows that there are
15,405 temporary and exempt employees, who
last year were paid in wages £1,054,915.
These men who have entered the service by
other than the front door, owing to the tem-
porary “temporary” nature of their jobs, in
which many of them have grown bald, are
paid at a higher rate than mere permanent
employees, who have come in by the front
door after their qualifications have been
ascertained.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Norbell”: The front seat has its casual-
ties, too. When the Gonsalez Co. was doing
“II Trovatore” at H. M. Theatre, Brisbane,
the other night, during the banging “Anvil
Chorus” a chip of steel whizzed out into the
stalls and wounded a soldier. The missile
got him in the eye and the doctors doubt
whether he will be able to see out of it
again.
Passed out in London. Edward Sass, a
conscientious mummer whom Australia knew
in the Brough days. Sass afterwards had a
turn at management, mostly in S. Africa.
In this he was encouraged by George
Edwardes; his wife was a sister of that big
manager’s widow, the Julia Gwynne of the
original “Mikado” cast.
“8.. T.” : The skull to which Allan Wilkie
remarks “Alas, poor Yorick!” was originally
used by Charles Kean. It was brought to
this country by George Ward in 1851, and
most of the famous Hamlets we have had
here, including G. V. Brooke and Barry
Sullivan, have used it. Wilkie obtained it
from Ward while in Dunedin recently.
“R.R.R.”: “Melb” is wrong in his asser-
tion (B. 26/10/’l6) that the Finkelsteins
abandoned the Jewish drama project two
and a half years ago. As a fact the Yid-
dish play, under Finkelstein direction, is
flourishing to such an extent that Mrs. F.
is noAV in Sydney negotiating for a lease of
the Little Theatre, with a view to the pro-
duction of Jewish plays there. For the past
18 months the co. has been showing at the
suburban Carlton Hall every Saturday night.
The Finkelsteins have now been in Aus-
tralia six years. Two and a half years ago
they produced Jewish drama in the Little
Theatre and Concordia Hall (Sydney), also
in Perth, at the Princess Hall. As the
players are all professionals, it is self-evi-
dent that the venture is a financial as
well as an artistic success. The Strunin
show mentioned by “Melb” was merely a
hash-up of alleged Yiddish drama and vau-
deville turns.
Allen Doone hasn’t the time to do his own
stage-managing any longer, so he has per-
suaded Harrington Reynolds (Father Kelly,
of the “Rosary”), to leave off his soutane,
and attend to the job.
“The Cracked Te or”: F. S. Kelly, just
killed in Europe, was unlike most musicians
I have met, inasmuch as he knew little or
nothing of the art of blowing his own trum-
pet. When I first saw him, five years ago
in Sydney, he insisted upon talking, not
about himself, but about Leonard Borwick,
then on his way to Australia, and about the
wonderful music Sydney would enjoy when
Borwick arrived. And yet Kelly himself
was no dullard in interpreting Bach. Bee-
thoven or Brahms. Those who heard him
play the big G Major Concerto of Beethoven
with the Symphony Orchestra at the Town
Hall at the June concert of 1911 will agree
that he knew his job thoroughly. He was
also a composer, and had numerous piano-
forte pieces and songs to his credit. The
horrible waste of war!
“Die Mihi” : The Melbourne Presbyterian
Assembly held a long and anxious debate on
a requisition from a number of parents
whose girls attend the Presbyterian Ladies’
College, known to fame because it was the
place where M‘elba was taught how to
become a diva. The petitioners wanted the
Assembly to sack Goll, who manages the
musical department of the college. Finally
the Rev. Gray explained that Goll was not
an Austrian, but a Czech, and his Czech
forefathers had hated the Austrians for
years and years and years. So by an
overwhelming majority the petition was
refused, and Goll keeps his billet while some
good Australian musician is playing Brahms’s
rliapsodie with a pair of cutters on a barbed
wire fence at the Ancre. These dear old
patriotic Scotchbyterians!
From E. C. Davidson, director of the
“Australian National Theatre” :
Being a thoroughbred Australian, and one who
does not wait for the older world’s approval of our
art before I can accept it, I am going right on with
the work I have set myself to do, and shall not rest
until the Australian National Theatre is a firmly-
established fact. If the playwrights will stand the
fusillade of discouragement Bulletin scribes seem
determined to pour upon us, the day is not far
distant when they as well as the local charitable
institutions will materially benefit.
Well, The Bulletin is affable and hopes so.
Billy Salisbury, head mechanist at Mel-
bourne Majesty’s, who passed out the other
week, was only 45. He had been iden-
tified with the Firm as far back as the
period when J. C. Williamson played “Rip
Y’an Winkle”; he was one of the kiddies in
the cast. He next figured as call-boy, and
eventually went behind under old Bridger,
the mechanist of the time. It has been ad-
mitted by all the famous actors who have
strutted hereabouts, including Wilson Bar-
rett, Margaret Anglin, H. B. Irving and
William Collier, that Salisbury’s ability
amounted to genius. The producer of “Peter
Pan” was flabbergasted when Salisbury pro-
duced a flying machine for fairies that
knocked the latest London idea kite-high.
Salisbury’s flying scheme had been devised
years before for a blue-ballet effect when
Mary Weir did the flying. Billy had long
hoped to chuck the stage and go on the land ;
but he didn’t move till Death took him.
“Theorbo”: After her Honolulu holiday
Melba returns to Australia to do some con-
cert Avork. Then she goes to England to
fulfil engagements, including a provincial
tour. She intends to reappear in opera
Avhen it is resumed. But wouldn’t it be
better for her to retire at the height of her
powers, like Macready, and not hang on, like
Albani and Irving? Money was a considera-
tion to both those. It isn’t to Melba.
“Rab Cilbup”: Isn’t it about time drama-
tists gave up the idea of starting plays by
switching on the electric-light? You all
know the scene: blue moonlight flooding into
the darkened room; general air of a coal-
strike. Enter a butler or the hostess, who
solemnly turns on the light. What is the
use of making a ceremony of pressing an
electric button? It happens regularly in the
best houses. In “Common Clay” this con-
vention is absurdly overworked. The hostess
is giving a dance upstairs, yet the hall,
reached by stairs from the ballroom, and
into which the front door opens, is kept in
total darkness during that dance. Only at
midnight does the hostess decide to risk the
expense and light up, and let the play begin.
From the U.S.A.: —•
Our old friend Andrew Higginson, who played a
brief part as a Bung at Wangaratta (Vie.), was
lately in the cast of “The Amber Princess” in
N’Yark. Cyril Keightley, who herds among the elect
of the profesh at Long Island, is getting a big salary-
in a bit of Broadway din called “Cheating Cheaters.”
Henry liolker, who is still in a flourishing physical
condition, is head man in “The Silent Witness” in
Boston. Willie Collier, whom Australia will remem-
ber with gratitude for showing it “The Dictator,” is
the rage in a N’Yark comedy, “Nothing but the
Truth.” Assisting Willie is Rapley Holmes, the
delicate creature who was the beauteous Waldron’s
foreman in the Australian production of “The Squaw
Man.” Rapley can now make a weighbridge tremble
to its foundations. He is just over 3001 b. and is
still fattening.
“De Oriel” : All performers are aware of
the immense social barriers betwen dramatic
and vaudeville artists. Willowby, the famous
tragedian, is of course a much superior per-
son to Wilkins, the warbling woodchopper,
Legges Duo, the songandance sisters, or
Winnie, the whistling waitress. Well the
melo. manager lately ordered Willowby to
purchase a better silk hat, many years of
“East Lynne” having sadly reduced the
artistic polish of Levison’s headgear. Seek-
ing an adjacent pawnshop, Willowby de-
manded to see some secondhand silk hats.
Moses produced three. “All I have left,”
he explained. “A dollar the lot.” “I only
require one,” said Willowby coldly. “I
need it for stage work.” “Well, Mister,”
the Jew exclaimed impatiently, “surely to
God you can juggle three.”
“0.K.”: Australia saw a replica of the
original Mrs. Dion Boucicault in her pretty
little daughter Nina, who came here with
her father and stayed with her brother as a
member of the first B. and B. Co. Nina
remains neat and pretty in her middle-age.
She has the matron part in “The Old
Country” at Wyndham’s Theatre. The
author of the play is her nephew, Com-
mander Dion Clayton Calthrop, of the
British Navy. Calthrop, by the way, is a
nephew of the well-known painter. His
father, who dropped the Calthorp and be-
came John Clayton for stage purposes, mar-
ried old Boucicault’s eldest daughter.

AN ABSENT-MINDED BEGGAR
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Spriggs was a picture-mummer when
A slack civilian ;
And now a hero he—of men
“One in a million!”
A soldier whose brave deeds have moved,
This hideous war time,
One-half the world. He stands approved,
As ne’er aforetime.
The battlefield, “somewhere in France,”
His stage; he held it
And led the Huns a dev’lish dance.
None paralleled it.
Saving a gun, by that the tide
Of battle turning;
He lived, though scores of Germans died,
The V.C. earning.
“Brave lad!” the Colonel cried, “but why,
I ask you, sonny,
Such recklessness, dare-devilry ?”
Said Spriggs, “It’s funny!
“I know I, by the rules of war,
Deserve your strictures;
I thought, sir, I was acting for
The blessed pictures!”
victoria.

TO PUT ON FLESH AND INCREASE
WEIGHT.
A SPECIALIST’S ADVICE.
Most thin people eat from four to six
pounds of good, solid, fat-making food every
day, and still do not increase in weight one
ounce, while on the other hand many of the
plump, chunky folks eat very lightly, and
keep gaining all the time. It’s all bosh to
say that this is the nature of the individual.
It isn’t Nature’s way at all. Thin folks
stay thin because their powers of assimila-
tion are defective. The just absorb enough
of the food they eat to maintain life and a
semblance of health and strength. Stuffing
won’t help them. A dozen meals a day won’t
make them gain a single “stay there” pound.
All the fat-producing elements of their food
just stay in the intestines until they pass
from the body as waste. What such people
need is something that will prepare these
fatty food elements so that their blood can
absorb them and deposit them all about the
body; something, too, that will multiply their
red blood corpuscles and increase their
blood’s carrying power. For such a condi-
tion I always prescribe the following for-
mula :— §oz. Tincture of Cardamoms Com-
pound, 8.P., 2oz. Salrado Compound, 3oz.
Elixir Simplex, and sufficient water to fill
an Boz. bottle. This formula can be filled
by any chemist, and one or two tablespoon-
fuls taken regularly 20 minutes before each
ineal will cause any thin man or woman to
put on from 2 to 3 lb. of solid healthy flesh
every week. It is also a splendid aid to the
digestion, has a fine tonic effect, and is
unequalled for people who are run down, or
for old people who are lacking in nerve
force and energy. If you are below normal
weight, suffer from indigestion, are nervous
or weak, try this prescription, and see how
rapidly you pick up.—S.C.T.— Advt.

EXCELLENT CUISINE.
SOCIETY
The latest triumph of the Defence Depart-
ment is that achieved in the case of Francis
A. Bent, of the A.1.F., who, after having
enlisted at the Town Hall recruiting depot
in Melbourne, and having spent some weeks
in camp, was sentenced to three months’
imprisonment at the Richmond Court for
COMPARATIVE ANATOMY.
The first case of appendicitis.
failing to report himself under the Hughes
proclamation, now repealed. With singular
prevision of Australian Defence methods, he
had asked at the Victoria Barracks before
joining the A.I.F. whether he need also
enrol under the Hughes (home service)
proclamation, and was told that of course he
need not. After he had been served with a
summons by a constable who refused to take
it back and report that Bent was in camp,
the court case came on, and at last the
soldier thought it was high time to write
to the papers about it. “It was not reported
to Us,” was Authority’s lofty explanation,
’“that the young man had enlisted!” Hadn’t
we better turn over the whole business of
running the Australian end of this war to
the Salvation Army?
“Fax”: As the women of Sydney (or
some of them) propose to form a National
party, and as they seem to have some diffi-
culty about their Constitution, may I pro-
duce for them the Draft which in exactly
similar circumstances The Freeholder
suggested to the dames of Great Britain in
far-back 1716: —
We, the Consorts, Relicts and Spinsters of the
Isle of Great Britain, being most passionately
offended at the falsehood and perfidiousness of cer-
tain faithless men, and at the lukewarmth and in-
difference of others, have entered into a voluntary
Association for the good and safety of our Constitu-
tion. .. .We promise publickly and openly to avow
the Loyalty of our principles in every word we shall
utter, and every Patch we shall stick on. We do
further promise to annoy the Enemy with all the
Flames, Darts and Arrows with which Nature has
armed us; never to correspond with Sigh, Ogle or
Billet-doux; nor to accept the civility of any Man's
Hand who is not ready to use it in the Defence of
his Country. We are determined in so good a Cause
to endure the greatest Hardships and Severities ; and
even to wear the Manufacture of our Country, rather
than appear the friends of a foreign interest in the
richest French Brocade.
The gibe is very just. Patriotism in clothing
has been the principle which has come hard-
est to the naicest members of the Ladies’
National League in any political age.
* * * *
“John Dishragge”: Woman is taking a
weird view of what she calls “war work.”
A lucky friend who can still afford to pay
£1 a week to a resident lady-help is going
to lose her treasure of years past because
there is a lift-attendant job at £2 15s. wait-
ing. Now, there are plenty of soldiers, re-
turned damaged, who could fill the lift job
and keep a wife and kids on the screw, while
there are few girls capable and willing
enough to earn that domestic £1 a week.
The young woman in this case fires off the
usual gag that she is “going to do her bit
by taking the place of a man, who can thus
go to the war.” It is time we threw that
nonsense overboard and assured her that
she is dropping the work in which she can
do the greatest service to the nation and
taking the place of a man who has come
back battered from the war.
* * * *
“C.F.M.”: A fair young friend of mine
who is learning how to juggle top-notes
under Melba’s tuition, has lately been
acquiring a matronly plumpness which dis-
pleases the diva’s critical eye. “This will
never do,” she said, touching the pupil
lightly on a well-moulded shoulder. “Watch
how i keep my figure down.” Then followed
a demonstration or elucidation of her sys-
tem, which is, while lying full length on the
carpet, to revolve in all directions like the
spoke of a gigantic wheel. But it isn’t safe
to try it in your best dress,

Every dog has his day—only for some the
sun sets sooner than for others. The mana-
ger of the King Edward liome for Hounds
lately appeared in the Redfern (Sydney)
court and laid out a trophy before the pre-
siding magistrate—3s tails belonging to dogs
who would wag no more. The exhibitor
claimed half a crown per tail under a
venerable Dog and Goat Act, which pre-
scribes that small reward for any raider
who will seize and destroy any un-
registered dog and produce the tail before
a magistrate or Jaypee. He claimed
£4 7s. 6d. for the Home, remarking that “so
far as he, knew” all the victims had been
unregistered. The remains had been burnt.
The magistrate looked at the cold, still tails,
shuddered and allowed the claim. Name of
magistrate: Love!

“H.S.”: Here in Launceston (Tas.) we
have what is called a Naval Reserve, about
150 strong. In most of the towns where
there are facilities they are in proportion.
At present they are just a lot of young
landsmen who have been taught to row a
gig, and once or twice have been made
seasick for a week
on a torpedo boat.
They are the pick
of the local boys,
and about SO per
cent, of them have
volunteered for ac-
tive service; but
while one or two
have been accepted
as signallers, etc.,
the majority have
been refused permis-
sion to serve. Why
not accept them for
the term of the war,
and send them to
England to sample
active service with
the fleet? If that
were done, at the
end of the war
the Commonwealth
would have a genuine
Naval Reserve, not a number of willing land-
lubbers in a naval dress.
* * * *
Australia isn’t all picture shows and
strikes. A trepang cutter which went out
at the end of August with two Filipinos in
charge blew back to Darwin the other day
with only its crew of three black boys and
two lubras. The Filipinos, whilst working
their curing-pots with the help of friendly
blacks near the Goyder River, had been
attacked by wild abos. from another tribe.
The Filipinos made a dash for the water and
tried to swim off to the cutter; but the
hissing spears caught them and the bodies
sank. To the story the N.T. police add a
footnote that the same murderers have been
having quite a busy season . and that the
coast is only safe for armed parties. Thank
Heaven, there are still some people ready
to defend Australia!
“K.Q.”: Mudgee (N.S.W.) claims to be
the only inland town which fires a one
o’clock gun. It only happens on Saturdays
but is waited for by all hands and never
misses. The old breech-loader —of the kind
Grenville used on the little Revenge-Ms
mounted on a wooden stand about 10ft. high
and frowns on the bank of the Cudgegong
River, near the main street. The post office
receives “time” at one o’clock from Sydney,
and a boy with a red flag signals to the
municipal council’s employee, who touches
her off. Some local hard-doer fired her off
the other night in the middle of a war pic-
ture entertainment, but it didn’t stimulate
recruiting.
Wood, of the Melbourne Board of Public
Health, has been throwing out a vague sug-
gestion that he deserves a statue in the
market-place and a testimonial engrossed on
vellum. Some years ago he “directed atten-
tion” to the dangers of long skirts as germ
collectors and carriers. The women didn't
immediately shorten their skirts and petties,
but latterly they have reformed their dress
beyond his wildest dreams. Therefore he

REALITY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Did we laugh too well ?
Was the world too kind
When the twilight fell
And the night was blind ?
Roses breathed around.
Stars were white above;
In the gloom we found
Little frightened love.
All the laughter died,
We had naught to say—
That we tried to hide
Caught us both at play.
Love, you trembled so—
Breast and silken sleeve.
Half an hour ago
All was make-believe.
Did we laugh too well? -
Was the dark too kind ?
Soft rose petals fell,
All the trees were blind.
Then your yielding lips
Paid love’s first hot tax —
Cabbages and chips!
Shoes and sealing-wax!
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

“Petifi”: When the grand uproar is over
the full company might do worse than ad-
vance to the footlights and give three cheers
for that enthusiastic front-stallite—Den-
mark. In the early days of the commotion
the military doctors were horrified at the
frequency of tetanus cases among the
wounded, and at their wits’ end to cope
with the trouble. Then enter Hamlet.
Dr. Th. Madsen, director of the Danish
Serum Institute, bought 60 horses and got
busy making the anti-toxin that gives bro-
ther Tetanus the knock-out, passing it
along, literally by the gallon, to the battle-
fronts. So far over 2,000,000 “por-
tions” have been distributed among the hos-
pitals, and Heaven only knows how many
otherwise dead soldiers have, in conse-
quence, got another chance. If ever T.
Atkins gets to know how the Danish Serum
Institute has done its little bit he’ll want to
pass round the hat that signifieth gratitude.

The whip to flog it with.

An Australian artist’s half-hour at the
Front:—■
I am writing this at the end of a dull, grey day
in France. My studio is a cold, allegedly shell-
proof dugout with an arched roof of iron cupolas
covered with bags of sodden clay and a crop of lank
grass. The other occupants are an A.M.C. corporal,
a sergeant-armament artificer and a tall, clear-
skinned youth from an English Flying Corps—a wire-
less telegraphist attached for the convenience of our
artillery, and a corner of the boudoir is his. We
are almost in darkness, having exhausted our candle
supply; and through the opening in the upper half
of the front-wall, where one might expect light, is
nothing but a scowling, leaden sky heralding the
close of another hilarious day.
Opposite me the young Englishman is crouched
over a rickety table bearing his portable receiving
apparatus. The aluminium what-you-may-call-it is
clamped to his ears, and he has just got on to
Eiffel Tower and is taking down the latest war-news.
This branch of the business is rather interesting,
inasmuch as messages may be picked up from Paris,
Berlin and Sausage balloons. Faint buzzing words
from London and other English stations raise vain
longings in our hearts. Finished at last, he hands
the communique to the sergeant, who reads it aloud
by the wretched dim light. But his voice is sud-
denly drowned by whang-bang! whang-bang! “That’s
the battery again commencing its evening
strafe,” remarks the corporal. “Trying to get a bit of
their own back. Seven of ’em killed by one shell
in the gun-pit last week.” With each shot the
water-sodden landscape vibrates and quakes, and con-
versation soon lags. Somehow my pipe won’t draw;
and we remain staring silently and gloomily out
into the drizzling twilight.
The first evening starshell creeps up and hovers
a moment against the murky background framed in
the dark square of our doorway. I try to find
dissipation in watching for the next one to appear,
while .the sergeant seats himself on a box and stares
moodily down on our patchwork floor. I follow
his gaze and notice one piece of board gently bob
and sway like a loose plank in the floor of a leaky
HATED TO THINK OF IT.
DAPHNE: “What is your idea of not letting
him propose?”
DOLLY: “Well, when we are engaged it will
be my sacred duty to make him

dinghy. With a Parliamentary remark he commences
to lift and fling the uneven lengths of board to one
side. The movement causes water to ooze up through
the cracks in other places, and soon a charming lake
is revealed. With much language and a jam-tin he
proceeds to dispose of the surface waters through
the doorway, leaving the oil-drum (placed under
our floor to catch the drainage, but which has over-
flowed) to be emptied later on.
The A.M.C. man is squatting in the one dry corner
looking through some ill-treated gramophone records,
the gift of a cable-station official in Hong Kong.
By now our guns have ceased and there is only
the customary faint rumbling in the distance. The
airman and I are still sucking our pipes, staring
out into the sloppy gloom and thinking silent pro-
fanities. The only sound affecting the conscious
mind is the tssip-tssip and gurgle of everlasting
damnable waters. We listen to it in silent wretched-

ness. My feet feel damp and chilled, and I can
almost see my nose taking on a pink-and-blue frozen-
mutton hue. My mind will dwell on images of a
roaring coal fire, soft floor rugs and a nicely-set
dinner table.. Someone sniffles his nose wretchedly,
and unnecessarily observes that it is going to be a
co-o-old night. Then from the vicinity of the A.M.C.
man comes a low, harsh, grating sound and an
irritating parody of a human voice commences “A
sho-o-ort life but a ga-a-a-ay one!” This is too
much, “....the Kaiser!” someone explodes; and
the motion is carried without a referendum.

“T. 0.1.”: Some interesting applications
are on the file in Sydney for registration
under the Real Property Act. One lot is part
of the estate at Newtown granted to Gover-
nor Bligh by his predecessor, Governor
King, before the formality of “swearing
in” was gone through. When sworn in
and Governor de facto, Bligh granted
King’s wife a thousand or more acres at
South Creek, now St. Mary’s. Another
lot is part of a grant to Dr. Balmain,
who when leaving Botany Bay sold the
whole of the site of the grimy suburb to
a friend in India for 10s. This friend
was Dr. John Gilchrist, professor of
Hindustanee in Calcutta, who passed the
bargain on to an English company.

“D.D.S.”: Even the regimental dentist’s
chair seems to have red-tape bows on it. A
young Billjim the other morning found that
the filling had come out 'of a tooth. He re-
ported to the uniformed dentist on the spot,
and was informed that he could have the
tooth pulled if he liked, but that Lieutenant
Fangs had no authority to refill it for him.
Billjim leapt out of the chair and was
making for the door, when Fangs added a
postcrint that if he (Billjim) liked to get
an order on his own dentist he could have
the filling done and keep his tooth.. But why
couldn’t the military dentist do the job?
What absurd regulation is getting in the
way of men who are employed to save grind-
ers, not to squander them? Can’t the stupid
military people see that the rule encourages
the sacrifice of teeth, just as though it were
an advantage in a soldier to be toothless?
* * * *
A story from the place where Billjim is
emulating the habits of the wombats of his
native land. On the morning following a
particularly dirty dust-up Bill bared his
shoulder and showed two or three comrades
a wound that looked just about awful enough
to stop a horse. “Name o’ !” cried
Jim, “ain’t you showed it to the doctor?”
“No,” replied Bill, “not me!” “But,
strewth! don’t it hurt?” “Oh, yes, it ’urts.
But I ain’t goin’ to pamper the
thing.”

be pleased to recom-

by O’Malley regret-

undertake work in the

“McNorth”: The Outback hangman men-
tioned by “Splodger” (B. 2S/9/T6) who got
£6O a head for stringing up a couple of nig-
gers must have belonged to the union to
get such a fee. In Papua, a few years ago,
niggers convicted of murdering white men
were jerked into the hereafter by anyone
who fancied the job, and the question of
payment was seldom pressed. But if the
operator demanded it a fiver a neck was
paid. It is alleged that on one occasion
Governor MacGregor asked a hard doer
named Ole Bill to hang a couple of blacks.
“Ye’ll be paid ten pounds for the job,” said
Wee Mac. “Wull ye do it for that?” “Five
pounds each!” said Ole Bill. “’Struth, I’d
hang all the niggers in New Guinea, an’ you,
too, for a fiver a time!” He got the job ;
MacGregor liked enthusiasts. Incidentally,
in the mountainous inland country of Papua,
where, because of the difficulties of transit, a
scaffold was impracticable, it was the cus-
tom to hoist the condemned by a rope under
the armpits and over a convenient branch
of a tree. The usual rope round the neck
was placed in position and the other end
made fast to the limb. Then at the word
“Go !” the first rope was cut, and the vic-
tim dropped suddenly for a few feet, and
then remained suspended in the climate.

Again the graphic Anzac: —
We are so close to Fritz’s line that we can almost
throw a kiss into it and hear it drop.
The writer is Darcy McDougall, a Victorian
with one of the machine-gun companies.
But he doesn’t mind his proximity to Fritz.
All he has to complain about is that the
trench rats have eaten his cake of soap.

THE SIMPLE LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hence! Bring me not the mocking heat and pale
That glows from frail contrivances of tin!
Let others fry the chop as day grows stale
On lamps that seek a short respite to win
From the disordered purpose of the mob
Whose principles, per gross, might fetch five bob t
Coal fails to-day. To-morrow kerosene
Is scarce as nuggets of unsullied gold.
Give me the simple things these eyes have seen
Playing their humble, honest part of old.
I go to nature, clear of fraud and tricks,
And cook my daily cow on blue-gum sticks.
Civilisation crumbles to its fall.
Its halo of electric light goes out.
Its stove is black ; the darkness like a pall
Has wrapped its streets and houses round about.
Yet shall I triumph in the cave-man’s way,
And boldly front my three square meals a day.
Here, where the rosebud to the fuchsia nods
And all the air with summer’s breath is sweet,
I rear a kitchen-range of verdant sods
And stir hot embers round the poppy’s feet.
The garden looks surprised; but what care I
While merry lambkin chops are mine to fry?
I shall not chase the wilding kerosene,
Nor hunt the petrol to its smellful lair.
Joyous beneath the waving bough and green *
I rear my altar in the open air—
My kind domestic altar fair and bright
To that triumphant godlet, Appetite.
Strike one, strike all! The world may come and go
As long as I can find a match to strike
And chips of wood to make a little glow
Beneath the broiling viands that I like.
I feel elate, untrammelled, glad and free,
Picnicing in the teeth of anarchy.
N.S.W.
MARY McC.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
PREMIER RYAN, Queensland.
W. G. Spence, who is 70, is the eldest
man in Hughes’s new team. Queenslander
Bamford, native of Dubfco (N.S.W.), now
Minister for Home Affairs, is in his 68th
summer. Archibald, the humorous Minister
for Customs, is 65 ; Treasurer Alex. Poynton,
born in Victoria, is 63; and P.M.G. Web-
ster 55. Hughes is 52, Senator Lynch 49
and Senator Pearce, another Australian
born, 44.
A. G. Pendleton, dead in Adelaide at 79,
had valuable experience in the Manchester
district and in India before arriving in
South Aus. in 1877. He took hold there
as traffic-manager, and in 1895 became Rail-
ways Commissioner, in succession to the
Board which for seven years had consisted
of an imported chairman (J. H. Smith),
with Neill and John Hill. Since then it
has been a one-man job. Pendleton had
14 years as sole Commissioner, and retired
in 1909. Like Moncrieff, his successor, and
McGuire, the present holder of the position,
he was an Irishman.
“W.J.H.”: As “Loco” says (8.2/11/T6),
time has proved that the just-dead Alison
Smith was mostly right and the Age mostly
wrong in the tremendous row over the Vic.
railways in the early ’nineties. Events
have justified the late superintendent in
every innovation of his for which he was
condemned by the Judge Casey Board of
Inquiry, and afterwards dis-
missed. His big engines have
brought blessings from weary
engine-drivers ever since. The
Board of long ago decided
against these very types. Smith
was condemned for laying down
heavy rails. Heavier rails have
been adopted everywhere since.
He was denounced for extrava-
gance in attaching the Westing-
house brake to goods trains.
Now every truck has it. He
was sentenced to Sheol for build-
ing the first decent railway-car-
riage in Victoria. Now we are
educated up to pretty fair coach-
ing-stock. As one who served
directly under Smith and par-
ticipated in his downfall, it gives
me great pleasure to testify to
the foresight and ability of the
much-misrepresented and badly-
treated engineer. He may have
made mistakes, but what experimenter
doesn’t? It is the fear of making mistakes
that makes slaves of most of us. Strange
to say, Smith’s chief antagonist, ex-Commis-
sioner K. L. Murray, has just accompanied
him past the Home signal.
Thomas Prout Webb, K.C., Commissioner
of Taxes in Vic., is dead at 71. He was
born at Newtown, now Fitzroy, in 1845, son
of Robert Saunders Webb, who in the mid-
’thirties was clerk to the Collector of Cus-
toms in Sydney—the old Customs House
still stands in Argyle Cut. Webb, sen.,
went with Robert Hoddle to Melbourne in
1837 and acted as clerk at the first land sale
(June 1, 1837), Robert Hoddle, Chief Sur-
veyor, being auctioneer. At this sale Webb
secured an allotment at Williamstown for
£4O, and at the second sale (Nov. 1) he
bought several Melbourne city blocks for a
few pounds apiece. The son was for some
years a barrister on the Equity side, and in
1884 succeeded Frederick Wilkinson as
Master in Equity and so passed to his recent
billet. He married a daughter of John
Thomas Smith, seven times Mayor of Mel-
bourne.
McGirr and the burly Stuart-Robertson
have worked their way to a place somewhere
near the front in the much reduced N.S.W.
Labor party. A tale is told that 11 years
back, when the P.L.L. executive was select-
ing a bunch of three to run for the Senate,
they were both competitors for a place. Cold-
tea Clark and McDougall were already
selected, and a third was being achieved by
a process of elimination. Stuart-Robertson
and McGirr remained. “Anyone know R. J.
Stuart-hyphen-liobertson of Cobar?” the
Chairman asked. The late Donald Macdonell
was present, and said he knew him well.
Then Donald asked: “Anyone know
McGirr?” and nobody did. “Then you’d
better run McGirr,” says Donald, and they
took his advice.
The vague “conscientious objectors” struck
a snag in South Aus. when they brought
their appeals before Judge John Gordon. The
wise and worldly old Judge, coming out of
a Presbyterian manse, includes in his rare
mental furnishing a thorough knowledge of
the Bible, and met them on their own
ground. The man who claimed that Christ
said “Thou shalt not kill” was asked to show
the text, and couldn’t. If he chose to stand
by the Old Testament, he was asked to
defend the law allowing two wives, or was
referred to the Lord’s command to make a
list of all the men of 20 and over “that are
able to go forth to the war.” If he elected
for the New Testament, he would be ques-
tioned as to his savings bank account, and
invited to reconcile it with the injunction
not to “take thought saying what shall we
eat or drink, or wherewithal shall we be
clothed?” It was an exhilarating and quite
unusual scene for a law court, and the per-
fectly obstinate “Turn the other cheek”
wowser was told to go into camp for a
month, do non-combatamt duties, and “pray
for the Germans —tliey need it badly
enough.”

Abel’s brother or not. On the other hand,
the shifty Premier has signed on with Wade
and others to whom the ex-Speaker is a
serpent. As if that weren’t enough dis-

Rubicund' N. Johnson, N. S. Wales Com-
missioner for Stamps, and the State’s most
ardent hunter after a forgotten tuppence,
resigns on the score of ill-health at 68 years
of age. He was born in the Rocks area,
where his father had a cooperage. After a
probation in a mercantile office, young John-
son joined the Stamp Office as a junior
under William Hemming—a Sydney law
stationer before he became Stamp Commis-
sioner, and the man who introduced the
common or house sparrow to Australia.
When the then Stamp Act was repealed
young Johnson became a clerk in the Sheriff’s
Department, and as such acted as Deputy
Sheriff at a blackfellow’s execution in
Dubbo. When stamp duties were re-
imposed he returned to the office and worked
up to the Chief’s chair. He was a brother-
in-law of the late J. H. Rainford, old-time
actor. Nobody who merely knew him as an
angry bulldog worrying a citizen for a 'bit
of duty which he thought was being with-
held is likely to believe that as a young
man his hobby was the rearing of canaries.
Burnt to death in France, while attend-
ing to his gun, Sergeant William Young,
who fired Australia’s first shot in this war.
Before he joined the A.I.F, two years ago,
Young, then 22 years of age, was with the
Permanent Artillery, and was stationed
at the Point Nepean (Vic.) fort at Mel-
bourne Heads when war was declared in
August, 1914. The German cargo steamer
Pfalz made an attempt to escape. It was a
rash attempt, for worse would have been
in store for her had she cleared the Heads.
Possibly she “had a meet,” as the boys say,
with the Scharnhorst and the Gneisenau,
then in Australian waters. But Young
changed her mind by firing a shell which fell
in the water close to the vessel, causing her
to return to Hobson’s Bay. Young at the
time of his death was in the Heavy How it
zer Brigade. He had been wounded in
Gallipoli, and when convalescent f & s trans-
ferred to Egypt, and afterwards to liance.
He was a St. Arnaud boy.
The Kaiser is responsible for the death of
Captain F. S. Kelly, tlie best amateur
sculler Australia has produced. He won tne
Diamond Sculls at Henley-on-Thames three
times and the Wingfield Sculls (amateui
championship of the Thames) once ; was in
the Oxford eight that was beaten m IJUo,
and helped the Leander Club to win tne
Grand Challenge Cup at Henley on three
occasions. Kelly was a member of the Royal
Naval Volunteer Reserve, and only a tew
weeks ago was awarded a D.S.O. for bravery
at Gallipoli. Among his hobbies were piavo-
playing and musical composition. Wulie-
motor Kelly and Alderman Kelly, ot Syd-
ney, have the distinction of being brotheis.
They were sons of “Tin” Kelly, who made a
pot of money out of smelting the metal the
billy is coated with.
C. J. Dennis, father of the Sentimental
Bloke, Doreen and Ginger Mick, is leaving
his political secretaryship and making back
to his bush home at Toolangi (Vic.), where
the splitters live and a writer lias time to
think.
Private Donald H. Campigli, not yet -.1,
stood up in the South Melbourne drill-hall
last week while the G.-G. pinned the Dis-
tinguished Conduct Medal on his chest. This
youth, not then 19, shared with his pals ot
the Stli Battalion the fame of the Landing
and the glory of Ilelles when McCay sooled
the 2nd Brigade onward. It was in Shrap-
nel Gully that he won his medal. From the
front of a trench from which the Australians
had driven the Turks came the cries of our
lads for water. Captain Hartland, after-
wards killed, refused Campigli’s plea to go
to his comrades’ succor. But Campigli
couldn’t stand those cries, and waiting till
Hartland’s back was turned, he went "over
the top” with a full canteen, and dragged a
wounded man into shelter. Going out again
he gave drink to an other, shot near the
spine, and then heaved him on to his own
back, though the stricken soldier’s 13st. was
a burden almost too great for the youngster.
Within 15ft. of safety the wounded man was
shot again, and yet a third time, the last
bullet being fatal. Campigli is an old boy of
the Searsdale State School.

can be managed easily by one man and runs
to just three-and-six, as compared with

MAYOR
McLEOD,
of Hobart.
Old Hiram Maxim goes out peacefully at
76, after two years of the briskest business
a gunmaker could ever hope to see. Inven-
tor of the squat, chattering terror that mows
men clown like grass, he has mountains of
dead men to his credit —and they wear all
the uniforms of the war. One battlefield
writer compared the sleeting rattle of the
busy Maxims to the sound made by a million
typewriters all furiously tapping out death-
warrants. The only time men ever turned
to a Maxim for a new lease of life was when
Colonel Younghusband was climbing with a
British force into exclusive Tibet. The
terrible cold gripped the vitals of the men,
and, as they knew rum had been poured into
the water-jacket of the Maxim to keep the
water in it from freezing, the nearest Mul-
vaney slily took out the plug and sampled it.
But 'some brute higher up who knew
Mulvaney had slily added a heavy dose of
kerosene.
THE GREEN HILL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I lie upon the grass, defiled, obscene,
Thrust out, a pariah, from the ways of men,
And leper-like I wail “Unclean! Unclean!”
And close my eyes and long to dream again!
I try to feel the pride of manhood strong
That thrilled my pulses at the clamant call,
When I stood up, or whether right or wrong,
In honor’s cause, to freely give my all.
But now, dishonored, profligate and vile,
Foul victim of libidinous desire,
A lust-stained derelict at which men smile,
A noisome thing, I languish in the mire.
At church parade they whine the hymns and pray,
And ev’ry supplication seems a sneer;
They drone “There is a green hill far away”—
Christ, my green hill, my Calvary, is here!
Here, where a flow’r-starred slope of sun-kist green
Encompassed by the crooning summer sea,
And that curved, wid’ning river’s restless sheen,
Is paradise—and my Gethsemane !
I watch the river’s busy traffic pass,
I trace the crowded troopship to the sea,
And as I groan upon the verdant grass,
The distant cheers are faintly borne to me.
Between me and that sweep of olive green,
Where mangroves fringe the river and the sea,
There winds a turbid belt of ooze unclean
That girds my green hill and imprisons me!
And from that ooze rise wretches such as I,
Merged in a slough, so near the open sea!
I watch the fairy fleets of sails go by,
And know that none may help—God pity me!
And, lest revolting at my shameful plight,
I would be free, stern sentries stand alway,
Their watch-fires glow throughout the weary night,
Their shining arms gleam through the garish day.
By day I hear the bush-bird call his mate,
At night, the curlew’s wild, despairing cry,—
Dear Christ! for lepers still compassionate,
Help me to save myself, help me to die!
V. D. COMPOUND, MILITARY CAMP.

No Australian co. working in competition
with foreign concerns has made better pro-
gress in 10 years. Down to the opening
years of this century the co., which was
originally a small offshoot from Burns
Philp’s, was satisfied to pay either small divi-
dends or no dividend at all. Down to 1899
inclusive nothing more than 6 p.c. was paid,
and mostly less; and the co. was then 14
years old. and, moreover, had had the back-
ing of the prosperous Burns-Pliilp Co. into
the bargain.
If an insurance concern can get through
the teething period, nothing holds out a
much brighter promise. The Queensland
not only got through that period but found
itself then blessed with a vigorous manage-
ment which was prepared to cry at the
top of its voice that it was Australian and
entitled to Australian support on that
account, other things being equal. Aus-
tralia was only waiting to be appealed to
in that way: and the next table shows some
of the results :
Loss Expense

In 10 years the business has trebled and
in six years it has doubled. And it has been
on the whole such profitable business that
in 10 years no less than £369,000 has been
saved to Australia compared with what
would have been the result had the business
gone to foreign cos., as it mostly used to.
Of that £369,000 no less than £175,000 has
been permanently retained in the business,
being transferred to capital; and, in all,
another £109,000 has been added to re-
serves. Approximately only one-third of
the profit has been divided among share-
holders ; the other two-thirds has been left
in the business so that policy-holders may
sleep more soundly in their, beds. Last
year, apparently recognising that a co. was
liable to be indirectly penalised for its early
providence, something extra in the way of
capitalising reserves was done. The capital
was taken from £150,000 to £250,000 in
one lift. And though p.c. is now paid
upon the whole lot, very little more than
half the profit is thus consumed. But the
co. can hardly expect to do as well nor-
mally. In spite of Wilhelm and his ally
the 1.W.W., the loss rate was under the
average of recent years. Which seems to
mean that, where there were extra risks,
the extra rates more than covered them.
Qwing to the rapid increase of business
the co. for some years did not show up very
well in the matter of reserves against risks.
But, owing to the policy of the co. in hang-
ing on to .so large a proportion of its profits,
the position to-day is quite satisfactory.
Capital and reserves combined total
£421.980. This is 173 p.c. of last year’s
net premiums, which is rather better than
the average of the best English cos. In
cash and well-written-down Government
securities alone, after paying all liabilities,
the co. had on September 30 enough to pav
nearly three years’ normal claims. Given
an average expense rate this year and a
premium income equal to last year’s, then
during the current year it could pay with
its cash and public securities alone losses
four times greater than those of any year
in the co.’s history. Nobody can reasonably
want anything better than that.

The deficit is represented as usual by a
“Foundation, organisation and extension of
business ' account. No doubt it is argued
that the policies which have been got are
worth money—that if the co. left off hunt-
ing for new business and simply wound-up
what. it. has it would make a very fair profit
out of it, enough to at least wipe out some
°/ .the deficit shown above. But unfortunately
£25,092 isn’t the full amount of the deficit,
because every policy in force represents a
liability which is not shown as such in the
balance-sheet. So far, in other words, we
have the income on one side and the actual
outgoings on the other; and the actual
outgoings are £25,092 in excess of the income.
But in addition to the actual outgoings a
certain amount ought to have been put by
towards paying the policies when they
become claims. What that amount is it is
impossible to say, but an actuarial valuation
is now in progress, and the conclusions are
expected by the end of the year. Only small
concerns like the A.M.P. and M.L.C. can
manage to get out their actuarial reports
simultaneously with their accounts.
The outgoings in excess of the income have
been paid out of share capital, of which there
is still £31,802 intact, represented by: Cash
on deposit, £16,121; loans on mortgage,
£13,818; and sundries. So, even though the
actuary will report that there is a liability
of some thousands in connection with the
policies in force, the policyholders are well
protected ; and it will be noticed by the first
table that the total outgoings have now been
brought down to less than the income. It
doesn’t follow that the office, though in its
sixth year, is yet living within its means,
because £l4l6—the so-called surplus of the
year—is not likely to be as much as the co.
ought to put by in twelve months towards
meeting its policy liabilities. Still the move-
ment is in the right direction ; and the pre-
sent figures, taking all circumstances into
account, are reassuring.
The co. issues house-purchase policies, both
Ordinary and Industrial. So far the actual
advances have been small. Since commenc-
ing business 70 advances have been made, 30
of which have been wholly repaid. Only
three advances were made last year, and the
total outstanding at Sept. 30 was £13,818.
The management doesn’t tell us the amount
for which the co. is liable to be asked ; and
that might be valuable information. Last
year, for instance, though only the three
new advances were made, first premiums were
paid on 25 new policies.

War profits, of course ; and no special pro-
vision seems to be made for war taxes. Some-
thing substantial may therefore have to
come off the figures later on. Nevertheless
one thing ought to be remembered to the
advantage of this co. in connection with
any scheme for collecting war taxes. If
the War Department wanted bricks des-
perately and a working brickmaker, in
order to supply them, toiled 15 hours a day
for a while, nobody would be so unfair as
to say that he should draw no more money
than if he had worked only eight hours and
produced little more than half as many
bricks. But in connection with the earn-
ings of a business the theory generally put
forward is that profits in excess of the
average made in the pre-war period should
be more or less commandeered, no matter
how they have been made; so that the
merchant who has made his war profit by
charging four times more for his stocks, or
the shipowner who charges 40 times higher
freight, would be on exactly the same foot-
ing as a manufacturer who made his war
profit by making four huiidred times more
goods. Actually he may sell the individual
article for much less than he formerly did ;
for the high cost of Australian and M.L.
manufactured goods has been mostly due to
the small output. It seems a fair thing,
then, that in fixing the amount of war tax
to be paid regard should be had to the
means by which the war profit has been
made.
The co., managed with Caledonian cau-
tion, has not been addicted to paying away
more than it has earned; wherefore, though
its dividend was as little as 2$ p.c. 10
years ago, it has put by enough to make
its position fine and strong. Take a 10-year
comparison:—-

On the surface plant and fixed property
are of less value than they were 10 years
ago; in other respects the position has im-
proved by £27,962. Whether the plant,
etc., really is worth less now than it was
in 1906 this paper doesn’t know. It only
knows that as much as £17,895 was spent
on new plant, etc., in the three years
1913-4-5; and that alone would counter-
balance a good deal of depreciation.

The drought of 18 months ago affected the
past year’s commission earnings in prac-
tically all departments; but, as the directors
put it, “the decrease in the production of
wool and the total cessation of our cus-
tomary large export trade in frozen meat
have to a large extent been compensated for
by the abnormal prices ruling throughout
the year for wool, produce and stock.” On
the other hand, there have been large in-
creases in taxation, with higher wages
under awards of Arbitration Courts and
Boards. High prices for live stock seem
certain for many years to come; but the
future of wool is less certain, and this is
the department in which Harrison Jones
are particularly interested. As a partial
set-off wage-costs may improve. It is im-
probable that there will be any serious reduc-
tion in wage rates. But that is of little
consequence. Wage rates are not the factor
of importance. Wage costs are. And already
there is in some directions a marked ad-
vance in labor efficiency with a correspond-
ing reduction in costs. In the building
trade in Sydney, for instance, since the
State Government discovered that the pawn-
shop is a cold place the private builder has
found that labor efficiency is from 20 to 334
p.c. higher than it was at the height of the
recent building boom. This is going to be
a pretty general experience before very
long.
The co. has done better, no doubt, than
its figures show over a long range of years,
because as the result of loans made in the
late ’Bo’s.and early ’9o’s it acquired many
assets which had to be quietly written down.
Even so. however, the visible progress has
been good enough :

in the right direction.

This CO. has practically only two assets—
(l) 'Shares and interests in jam manufac-
turing and fruit cos. and timber mills in
the _ Commonwealth of Australia, South
Africa and England,” £674,764, and ad-
vances to these cos., £25,750; (2) Common-
wealth war loan, £15,000. The co. began
wuth Henry .Tones and Co., who ran the
lAL jam-factory at Hobart. Henry .Tones
and his partner Ach. Palfreyman saw that
there was serious waste, not only in manu-
facture but in selling, which was to the
disadvantage alike of the producer of the
raw material, the manufacturer and the
public. This co. was formed to. organise the
business. It purchased all or most of the
shares in various manufacturing cos. in.
Tasmania, Victoria and N-. S. Wales; and
later on the business was extended to
Queensland and S’Africa, where other cos.
were formed.
The accounts of these subsidiary cos. are
not published; and the solitary item on the
revenue side of the P. and L. account of
this co. is “Income earned from investments
in the Commonwealth of Australia.” For
the past year this was £68,096; and, includ-
ing Federal taxes (£2193), the entire out-
goings were £2945. “Very satisfactory
results,” the directors say, “have been ob-
tained from the S’African factory”; but
“the profits are again being retained in that
country to meet the continued expansion of
trade there.” Exactly the same thing may
be happening in connection with the Aus-
tralian cos. They may be dividing only
three-fourths of their profits and using the
other fourth in extending their business. On
the other hand, certainly, they may be divid-
ing so much that they may not be laying
by sufficient for replacement of plant, etc.
It doesn’t, however, look the sort of Board
which would sanction that policy. In the
same way, while the P. and L. account gives
very little real information about the co.’s
business, or the businesses that support the
co., the balance-sheet doesn’t tell you the
real value of the assets. The “shares and
interests” in the subsidiary cos. are “invest-
ments at cost,” and some of these cos. may
now have extensive reserves, which, of
course, really belong to this co. On the
other hand, though this co. shows no good-
will or other hypothetical asset, a large
amount of the capital of the subsidiary cos.
may be represented by goodwill. The in-
vestor has to take a lot on trust; and thus
far he has been quite prepared to do it, and
it has paid him very well. In 1915, in addi-
tion to the 1\ p.c. dividend, he got a 5 p.c.
bonus. This year the directors say: “The
times we are passing through, and the uncer-
tainty of all financial conditions, including
the unknown quantity of war taxation, make
it desirable to pursue a conservative policy,
and to forego, for this year at least, the
payment of any bonus”—and the shareholder
who isn't blind will no doubt find the wink
as good as a nod. The co.’s liabilities total
£21,946, practically all money due to sub-
cos. The^ capital is £605,000.

A year ago this co.’s finance was a puzzle
to The Bulletin. On a capital of £29,198
it was holding £104,490 of deposits, while
it had no less than £45.801 in the bank on
fixed deposit, besides about £14,000 of till
money. This paper didn’t see how it could
pay a co. of this kind to take deposits and
pass them on to a bank, for it didn’t seem
probable that this co. could get money
cheaper than the banks. Apparently the
management wasn’t satisfied itself, and
during the past year it has paid off some
deposits. The total of the latter is therefore
reduced to £90,968. But it still has no
less than £31,233, for which it could find
no better use than to deposit with banks ;
and this is exclusive of £10,223 of till money
and money on current account. The posi-
tion has changed as follows in the 12
months: —
1915. 1916.
Capital .. £29,193 £29,305
Deposit-6 and other liabilities.. 108,086 93,979

Reserves 14,675 16,264
Advances 86,030 90,256
Properties, etc 15,663 14,012
Investments . . .. .. 3,209 6,234
Deposits, etc 59,552 41,546
Its own funds plus the debentures on Sep-
tember 30 amounted to £58,069; and loans,
properties and balances owing on sales
totalled £104,268. The balance of £47,780
was in cash and as good as cash. From the
creditors’ point of view the position is A 1:
from the profit-earning point of view it is
nearer Zl. . Probably the co. accepted long-
term deposits in anticipation of business
which it is now disinclined to take; and
that disinclination does it credit. But the
means to do ill deeds make ill deeds done;
so the sooner the unnecessary deposits can
be paid off the better.

or “converted,” to employ a euphemism, at
2 p.c. over the rate of £3 12s. 6d. usually
paid by the six States. Before 192-'
£30,000,000 of Australian loans fall due, so
that it looks as though the new borrowings
will cost at least 2 p.c. more in interest, or
£6,000,000 per annum altogether, on money
borrowed and spent 30 years ago. The Vic-.
Railway Commissioners blame higher inter-
est for part of their deficit; the Premier
explains that he, too, suffers from mterest-
itis; and the Melbourne Board of Works
openly wails that it cannot convert its 3*
p.c. expiring loan to a p.c. issue because
the Broken Hill Proprietary offers 6 p.c.
for the £400,000 it needs to complete its iron
works at Newcastle. So that what The
Bulletin has preached for 30 years on the
subject of the thraldom created by having
to pay heavy interest to foreigners is being
graphically impressed upon all who are in
the habit of living on borrowed money. At
June, 1917, Australian Governments, muni-
cipalities and public boards will owe
£500,000,000; and 2 p.c. on that means
£10,000,000 more every year to be provided
by taxation. And don’t let us forget that
taxation means work. We’ve got that much
more work to do to keep the bailiff out.

It seems that it only just happens that way:
to be precise, the year’s net profit was
£150,000 18s. Bd., according to the books.
But, after all, the books only make a guess
at these matters. “Taxes, including provi-
sion for Federal and State income taxes and
war-profits taxation,” account for no less
than £102,713 of the revenue ; and it is at
least possible that some of this amount will
not be called for. Since the war started pro-
vision has been made for taxation as follows:
At Credit of
Appropriation. Taxation Account.
Sept., 1914 . . £3lB
Mar., 1915 .. 3,900
Sept., 1915 . . 18,401
Mar., 1916 .. 65,054 £81,750
Sept., 1916 . . 102,713 164,724
So the amount actually paid away in taxes
in the past two half-years was £1705 and
£19,(39; and £164,724 is now available to
meet future calls. The directors say—
The position in regard to taxation is quite inde-
finite, and the total amount may be higher or lower
than the provision made, which is based on war-time
profits tax at the rate of 50 p.c. As the Government
proposal is to take all excess profits after June 30,
1916, a further deduction from the net profit may
have to be made, but the directors hope that this
proposal will be modified.
Another sort of Board wouldn’t stuff money
away like that. This canny lot evidently
reckons that £150,000 is as much profit as a
co. of right-thinking patriots ought to be
showing in a half-year on a capital of
£1,289,000, for that’s 25 p.c. So it puts the
balance where the moths won’t eat it even
if the taxation commissioners do leave some
of it.
And that little matter of provision for taxes
that may or may not be collected is not the
only factor that makes the “profit” a mere
guess. At March 31 the co. had 2452 tons
copper not settled for, and this was taken
into account at £6O ner ton. The metal actu-
ally brought £136 Bs. 9d. Thus, over £187,000
of the past half-year’s profit consists of sur-
pluses realised on the previous half-year’s
product. Of the past half-year’s output only
473 tons were sold and settled for; the
balance, 2666 tons, though sold under con-
tract on a basis of average monthly prices,
has been taken into account at the usual £6O.
Assuming that £125 is realised, this will
mean that a surplus of £173.290 will fall into
the current half-year’s profit. In any case,
214 tons more copper was on hand at the
end than at the beginning of the half-year;
and if a surplus of £6O is computed on that,
it will give £12,840. which may fairly be
added to the past half-year’s profit. Apropos
of copper sales, the directors make the tran-
saction with the British Munitions Depart-
ment clearer than the politicians left it.
Off his own bat Hughes had, so it was
announced, sold the output of Lyell, Morgan
and the others. The directors don’t mention
Hughes in the matter. They say they have
sold their output “at a price equivalent to
£l2O per ton c.i.f. London, subject to the
renewal of existing contracts ; and if our con-
tract with the British Insulated and Helsby
Cables, Ltd., is renewed, this will mean that
the bulk of our production: will continue to
be sold at average prices.”
The next comparison shows what has been
done in this co.’s life and what remains to be
done:—
Extraction Reserves
since 10/8/’O3. at 31/3/’l6.
Mt. Lvell . . 3,094,357 tons 2,177,046 tons
North Lyell.. 1,480,704 „ 1,083,211 „
Two years ago North Lyell was estimated to
have 959,832 tons in reserve. Since then
247,224 tons have been extracted, and the
reserves are now estimated at 1,083,211 tons
—or were six months ago. And the bottom
hasn’t been found yet.
Out of the 4,575,061 tons of Lyell and
North Lyell ore handled by the present co.,
with a few thousand tons of odds and ends
from the smaller mines, £2.188,838 has been
paid in dividends = 9s. 7d. per ton. There
are 3,260,257 tons in reserve. On the same
scale the dividends would amount to
£1,562,500 = 245. 6d. per share. Bet there
are other things to be taken into account.
Besides paying the dividends the co. has
accumulated £433,088 of liquid assets in
excess of liabilities. Also, it has put large
sums of recent years into superphosphate
plants; and this business should be very
profitable in the future. On the other hand,
the ore left to be worked is rather poorer
than the ore from which the dividends have
been paid. But as against that must be set
the application of new processes. Costs, too,
are higher. But too much can be, and
generally is, made of the probable effect of
that. If costs are higher it is largely because
of the general rise in the gold-value of com-
modities ; and copper is just as likely to
share in that rise as anything. So the rise
in costs is very likely to be neutralised, or
partly so, by the improved value of the
product.
* * * •
Boyd’s molybdenite mine, near Bathurst
(N.S.W.), is in the hands of a strong Syd-
ney-Melbourne option syndicate, which pro-
poses to get over the difficulty regarding the
formation of new cos. in Australia by
registering in Maoriland. There are, on
present appearances, larger possibilities in
this property than in any other molybdenite
show under notice in Australia.
* * * *
White - Crystal, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), is
considering a scheme to increase its share
capital. The contract to crush 4000 tons of
Carpathia ore was about half through when
the mill had to be hung up for repairs. This
concern doesn’t expect to earn dividends on
120,000 shares by treating customs ore, so
money is required to increase ore reserves.
Unfortunately, it isn’t a thing money can
always do.
* * * *
The Palmer party scored a win in the
struggle between the two sections fighting
for control of the B. H. Junction North.
The chairman and two directors resigned,
leaving the Palmer party free to send in its
own men. This will silence for the time the
suggestion that the control of the mine and
works should be handed over to 8., M. and
Co., who run the Zinc Corporation.
* * * *
Amalgamated Hill End (N.S.W.) cleaned
up 158 oz. retorted gold from 90 tons, which
isn’t payable under existing conditions. An
inch or two of rich stone along the mica
vein provided most of the metal. But the
little mica vein is very short. From the
lower tunnel a crosscut has been driven east
in search of the Star of Peace reef. As luck
would have it, two veins were met with
carrying gold; but as it isn’t certain whether
the Star of Peace has been cut, driving lias
been continued.
* * * *
In No. 2 run of the remodelled plant Y
Water (N.S.W.) in 1131 hours’ sluicing
time handled 147,000 cub. yds. for 50 tons
black tin. This was a big improvement on
No. 1 run, which was naturally affected by
opening out new ground and other pioneering
circumstances. The plant has proved itself
capable of treating 129 yards of washdirt
per hour, and this rapid sluicing on ground
that will yield over fib. of oxide per yard
must be dividend-paying at anything like
present prices. The unprofitable results
obtained last half-year were no criterion,
because of the shifting of the plant, the
opening-out operations on the western area,
etc. Manager Warlow Davies has gone into
tamp with the Engineers. He chose to be
a corporal at 9s. a day rather than accept
the offer of a big assistant-manager’s job,
worth anything from £l2OO to £l5OO a year.
That’s the effect the “No” vote has had on
some men.
* * * *
Bullrush (W.A.) is still unfortunate.
During the past year shortage and slackness
of labor have interfered with operations on
its sole surviving holding at Yuin, and now
work has ceased there altogether. The man-
ager reports that there are 5000 tons of
fully-developed payable ore above the 270 ft.
level, besides a few thousand tons of broken
stuff in the stopes; but to obtain the best
financial results about £7OOO more capital is
needed for development, which has been at a
standstill for some time.
* * * *
A fine storm in a teapot occurred on the
Melbourne Stock Exchange when some
Westonia sports set a yarn afloat that
granite was shrouding the winzes at the
Edna Deeps. The young generation of hullo
wanted the nasty tittle-tattlers caged up,
but Bill Roberts isn’t to be caught that way.
Even the news tnat water was dribbling into
the stopes wouldn’t soften his resolution to
let rumor die a natural death. Stuckey
wired a comprehensive denial, but he doesn’t
seem to be quite the same old oracle. More-
over, the market got a shock when a day or
two later he related that greenstone or some-
thing equally troublesome was in the shaft,
and that the cement was being resumed—of
course, to keep the water out. But what
right La: there to be any dyke in the shaft
at all? Didn’t a bore down from 480 ft,
advise striking the reef at 30ft. below the
level with good stuff in the core? To gei
greenstone and oz. dirt looks a bit of n
come-down. But the dreamy bulls aren’t
bellowing over that. The reason is that
see that if the dyke is the one that shows at
the tail end of the Edna May lode in the
big mine there has been a surprising shorten-
ing of the shoot of ore. Besides, the dreary
fact is being driven home that as the under-
writers of the Deeps bonds got left with the
wad of £lO,OOO debentures offered to share-
holders, and as a lot of cash is still needed,
the co. may not be so very far off the call-
list as some people think. Altogether things
don’t look sweet at the Deeps.
