“C.8.W.” : “Mararoa has taken the Bronco
Horseshoe (W.A.) on approval.” So The
Bulletin. It may do it—if the Federal
Treasurer allows registration, which is
doubtful. The Bronco, The Bulletin adds,
crushed 200 tons from development work at
the 50ft. for a value of 16s. per ton over a
width of 80ft. That’s right. I know,
because I bogged in and got out the dirt
single-handed. It left me heavily in debt, as
I anticipated. But I wanted to ascertain
true values over such an enormous body of
pre. The next crushings were taken from
the 135 ft. level to water (200 ft.) over a
width of about 40ft. Result: Approxi-
mately 2000 tons; value, 445. (from develop-
ment work). Great Victoria, same line,
covers costs of mining and milling at ss. Id.
per ton. The dirt can be broken for lOd. Costs
will be lower in the Bronco, where tributors
made as much as £S per week per man,
after carting eight miles —a w r aste of 255. per
ton. The Bronco is all right. It would
have been a big producer to-day if everybody
was in gaol who ought to be.
Williams’ United. Bendigo, having spent a
lot of cash dodging among the spurs at
2100 ft., gave up work at depth and had a
shot at the reef east of the shaft at 1644 ft.
The same old story was repeated there. Lots
of quartz in the reef and spurs, but mighty
little gold. Now the country of the 993 ft.
level is to be tested. T. J. Banner looks on
it as certain that he can get gold at 844 ft. if
he is allowed to put in a drive south to run
into the spurs in that direction. Though
the co. averaged only 4dwt. stone for the
last six months, it got through with one call
plus a lean on the banker to the extent of
£748. Teddy Dyason has swung on to the
board of this co., so look out!
. Support has been got for the Mitta Spring-
time, the Adelaide concern which is tackling
the big tin-bearing formations above Mitta
Mitta. Of the £25 reserved shares 200 go
out, and if the holders are lucky only a tenner
will be called up, w r hereupon the device will
be adopted of writing up the shares to £25
by making a call of £l5 and paying a bonus
at once of a like amount. This arrangement
will permit the newcomers to rank equal as
to rights with old holders. Manager Bill
McGregor plans to have 12 faces of ore to
draw from, and he italicises that he expects
to keep up a 2 p.c. to 3 p.c. average in tin
oxide. _lf he does, Mount Bischoff will have
to go into retirement, and things will hum
round Mitta Mitta as they never did before.
McGregor’s estimate of “all in” costs is ss.
per ton. This is considerably above J. B.
Lewis’s record at the Anchor mine in Tas-
mania : but Lewis only had about 4s. 6d.
stuff to treat, so McGregor ought to have a
pretty solid margin to work on. He isn’t
sticking to the old-fashioned battery, but
pins faith to an entirely new invention that
is to do the work of a 30-head stamp mill.
Experimenting in plant on a new show
doesn’t usually spell economy, though.
What with scratches to show hilly ground,
green ink to point out rising slopes and
black lettering to let the world know “This
area has been close bored and proved unpay-
able,” the Trong Tin, Perak, plan looks un-
healthy. This explains the muffled tone in
which the office talks nowadays, compared
with its joyous chatter before the dredge,
started. If the dredge plunges along as it
has done in the last six months it won’t be
long before it will be nosing round for some-
thing to do. The half-year’s record was
397,000 cub. yds., giving 144 tons tin oxide,
or an average of 0.8121 b. per cub. yd. Bar-
rister Meagher explains: “The ground is of
very uneven values. At times the tin oxide
shows freelv in the buckets, wdiich indicates
rich ground : at others it is very poor; all
of which, however, is not inconsistent with
the values as shown in the original boring.”
The co. at the end of October had a credit
balance of £4250. This was after wiping
out sundry _ creditors and paying a shilling
div., absorbing £3300. November has seen
another shilling, and as the last clean-up has
been most profitable, there ought to be a
cheery Christmas ahead. But, after all,
what barrister Meagher has to explain is
whether Trong has come g crash, or whether
only a small crack has been revealed.
8., M. & Co. are about to have another
shuffle. Coring, who now has his own letter

WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, November 27, 1016.
My Dear Arini, —
Pilgrims travelling to the Society of
Artists’ show in Queen Victoria Markets
on Saturday afternoon panted up the stairs.
Lifts were “off” after midday—like toast in
those exclusive boarding-houses where gas
has been the fuel. There w r ere various
cryptic notices to the effect that if you
chanced to break your neck on the darker
stairway, Heaven help you! It was to be
.no concern of the City Council. The final
climb, up a corkscrew staircase, is always
liftless, so that wasn’t an unexpected worry
for plump citizens. But the heat was. Pink
and purple countenances, under the glazed
roof, were assiduously mopped before the
visitors gasped their way into the speech-
making.
Julian Ashton, in the course of his re-
marks, referred to the good effect of the
Society’s homely walls of sacking—a dull
buff that doesn’t hit the eye—and he hoped
the mulberry stain in the Education Gallery
would soon be obliterated. Artists, by the
way, were allowed to choose the color for
that long picture tunnel. Women, accus-
tomed to buy from drapers’ patterns, can
evidently judge better than men of the
effect of a big piece from a small one. The
artists liked mulberry in a sample. But
they’ve hated the new gallery walls as
fiercely as the lay visitor t ever since they
saw it in gross. After Julian had laid down
the law brand-new Minister for Educa-
tion James intimated his desire to see the
Government found an Art School—to help,
not rival, local artists. All is going to be
lovely in the garden whenever a new gar-
dener comes along.
The Tom Hughes lady was as cool as any
in a soft muslin gown; Mrs. Max Allan
floated around in similar airy drapery.
Artists Minns, Gruner, Florence Rodway,
Lionel Lindsay and Syd Ure Smith
struggled through the crowd to personally
conduct their friends to the other fellow’s
wares. John Lane Mullins and his slim
lady, Justice Street (a sympathetic buyer
of Australian artists’ work), G. W.
Hadfield (from the rival art shop) and
goodness knows how many more were
squeezed in.
The concert to help the Lewisham Hos-
pital was to have been held in the Town
Hall on Saturday night. Indefinitely post-
poned. They haven’t started yet to light-up
that big barn with hurricane lamps. For a
like reason the St. John Ambulance meet-
ing, the annual affair, is also crossed off.
In the thick of the coal strike, when the
world was dark, Vivian Wybern Smith,
lieutenant of Field Artillery, made Meta
Victoria Parkes a soldier’s wife. But there
was plenty of color and light at St.
Stephen’s, with a side-line of kerosene lamps
in case the electrics changed their mind,
and a bower of white and pink blooms to go
wfith the bridal silver-and-pink and lace and
tulle. The only bridesmaiden w T as a Watteau
in floral silkery, while a brother-in-arms,
Norman Macleod, was on hand to produce
the ring at the right moment. After the
wedding the ’groom’s unit from the Warren
formed an arch of ribboned whips, and then,
regimental banner flying (given to the bride
afterwards), towed the newly-weds to the
Australia, where more festivities were pro-
ceeded with. The ’groom is a son of “Pearl”
Smith, who owns a captain’s uniform as well
and gave it an airing last Tuesday.
That hearty old chestnut, “Charley’s
Aunt,” will be dusted and brought out of
its locker on December 2 at the Palace
Theatre. The show is for the Cremorne
branch of the Red Cross.
The French Music Society had its third
concert just before the lights went out; so
the luscious smell of smoky kerosene-lamps
didn’t distract the audience. Eugenie
Boland, Arthur Appleby—in better voice
than at some former sing-songs—and Frank
Hutchens assisted.
Prof. Andy Stuart opened the exhibition
of R. Sidney Cocks’s water-colors in Hor-
derns’ gallery. In a fine Doric roll of the
R’s, the strenuous Scot explained the length
and breadth of the all-Australian show. This
done, he cajoled the long, shy artist, who
looked as if he’d rather be hanged, to mur-
mur a breathless sentence about the exhibi-
tion being now open. It was manager
Gillett, of Horderns’, who, so that the public
might see the work of a man who hid his
easel and himself away in a Blue Mountain
eyrie, arranged the show; and he had a few
words to too.
The National Gallery owns samples of
Sidney Cocks’s brushwork. In the years
that are dust he wandered over Europe
painting as he wandered, and in Italy
picked up the delicate fantasy of mist and
cloud effect which he now wreathes round
his Mountain sketches.
Candies have returned, with all their
spluttering wickedness, to every other tea-
room in our twilight city. Greatly daring,
the manageresses of some of the smaller
retreats drape the protesting sperm with
crinkled paper. Only the inscrutable ways
of Providence stand between the flimsy
decorations and a bonfire. Perhaps, when
one comes to think of it. Providence is
helped by the watchfulness of the patrons
who hurriedly bang down a saucer and put
out the flame just as the crisis approaches.
City women are apparently as scared of
lamps as our grandmothers would have been
of bottled lightning. In a little back shop
in an arcade I saw two villainous-looking
caudles—of the melted-fat persuasion—send-
ing rivers of grease down their guttering
sides. “The draught catches them,” ex-
plained the lady behind the counter. “But
they’re much safer than lamps!” Then she
snatched some lacy hangings from the fierce
red end of the wick, and the fire brigade
wasn’t sent for.
Since the return of candles no one is sur-
prised at the once fervent admiration of
beauties in their 60’s. Wasn’t Ninon de
l’Enclos supposed to have been nearly three-
score when she was doing a record business
in breaking hearts? Modern arc-lights are
trying to even ivory-white complexions, and
the present candle gleam is borne with
equanimity by the lady who knows it adds
50 per cent, to her charms.
The Wilfred Stead pair spent a few days
in Sydney before boarding the Niagara for
their native heath near Napier. The success-
ful Sasanoff got a berth, too, after some
delay. It is rumored he had the cabin de
luxe. Anyway, it cost some cash to house
the golden horse. Partner Ted Luttrell sped
back to Christchurch a week earlier.
The Mosrnan Musical Society has just
finished a five-nights’ incursion into opera.
“Martha” was the lady’s name. The leafy
suburb gave a reasonable measure of sup-
port, and exes, were about cleared. Mabel
Batchelor, Dorrie Ward, J. Yuill and Willie
Beattie were the principals. Beattie en-
listed, and was just about to sing his little
song .to Fritz when a bout of pneumonia
knocked that season on the head —for a
while, anyhow.
Pianist F. S. Kelly, who delighted Sydney
audiences with his recitals a few winters
ago, has been killed in France. He was the
bright and shining member of the “Tin”
Kelly family—a distinguished success in
everything he undertook.
Did you happen to meet Jack London
when he was here? Most of his time in Syd-
ney was spent in a private hospital recover-
ing from the effects of his “pleasure” trip
in the Snark. That was after he and his
plucky little wife had just missed being
eaten by the Best People in our cannibal
isles. Jack’s personality was charming. He
was a tall, fair-haired, clean-shaved, blue-
eyed, typical outdoor man, and looked
younger than his years, which only reached
41 in the end. At a local dinner given in
his honor the guests were solemnly warned
not to wear evening-dress. That was against
the lion’s unconventional beliefs. So the
high-necks waited, expecting the famous
Jack to dash into the house clad in miner’s
garb or wearing some kind of weird nauti-
cal kit." Instead, he was as spruce as a
tailor could make him. Cream silk shirt and
superfine black suit showed that the lion
had been mindful of his mane. The coat
was* a dinner-jacket—the swallow without a
tail so much favored in modern suburbs.
Mrs. Jack’s hobbies appeared to be to travel
as uncomfortably as possible and to wear
enormous rings on every other finger.
Mrs. Herbert Cunningham, of Chip Chase,
Wollstonecraft, and her daughter, Mrs.
Henderson, left on Friday with passports
and pyjamas for London, by way of Panama.
Farewells occupied their final fortnight.
Partly the flight is due to artist Fairlie
Cunningham. She decided not to return to
Sydney yet. but to sign on for six months
more V.A.D. at Brighton; and her sister
takes up a similar strenuous life. Her hus-
band, Mac Alister Henderson, died of pneu-
monia in camp here just after the great
strife commenced. A squatter brother,
Robert Henderson, from Queensland, left
on Thursday in the Niagara for the other
end and khaki. His wife and nursery went,
too, as far as Vancouver.
The friends and pupils of teacher Ada Gee
gave her a good-bye concert in the Repertory
the other night. Then she left for England
to chaperon her star contralto, Vera Cree.
Vera, as you know, goes to a scholarship
at the Royal Academy.
Graythwaite convalescent home for
soldiers at North Sydney hopes to get. an
extra chicken or two in the pot when the
cash from a recent fete at Crow’s Nest is
counted. The local branch of the Red Cross
was the lever that worked the effort, and the
Hay dame put on her best bonnet to set it
going.
Special interest attaches to the Apollo
Club’s sing-song on December 6. Alfred
Hill, now in charge, will steer the voices
through some of his “Hinemoa” music. Max
Vogrich, a conductor of the society in the
early days, and now leading a choir
beyond the Shadows, is to be remembered.
His arrangement of “Nazareth” makes the
special Christmas item.
The French-Australian League is mother-
ing Marie de Valmont, who was studying
singing at the Paris Conservatoire when the
Germans got busy. The refugee hailed
originally from Smyrna, whose trench ana
British inhabitants have been mostly caged
in that hornets’-nest. After fleeing hither
and thither, and losing her brother, and
being attacked on a Greek steamer by the
Hun’s submarine, Miss de almont con-
trived to reach Sydney. There’s a concert
in St. James's Hall on November 00, at
which some of the singing will be done by
this heroine of adventures that would have
filled the Castle of Otranto with sighs. Then
she hopes to take her voice to ’Murka till
the storm blows over.
The Battleplane Committee frizzled in the
Sock Fund’s room the other afternoon while
Mrs. Tom Watson read the latest news.
Money comes in slowly. But it comes, and
the steady drip of the wash-house tap has
filled many a tub before to-day. The Albert
Gould lady reigned serenely in the chair;
and treasurer Bryant, of the Union Bank, is
still waiting for your shilling.
The Anglican Synod will use its best
apron for a bib at a luncheon to mark its
jubilee. December 5 is the date, and Gover-
nor Strickland will handle a knife and fork
at the party.
Another clever Sydney girl, Gladys, the
versatile daughter of Langer Owen, K.C.,
looks at you in this little portrait, by Misses
C. and B. Macfarlane. An artist by instinct,
Gladys is also a true daughter of Australia.
Since the war. dropping her palette, she has
a fine record of Red Cross service. As one
of the joint hon. secs, of the Sydney league,
she must sign her name times innumerable
every week. But this is only the quiet side.
The conscription issue brought out a new
Gladys Owen —a magnetic speaker who, at
the clear call of her brothers in the trenches,
clambered on any handy vehicle and poured
out rousing appeals to Australia to vote
“Yes.”
Among his friends Sydney Ure Smith is
regarded as a rare humorist. If he and
his partner Harry Julius could not make
a living as commercial artists, they ought
to be able to coin money in a vaudeville
turn. In office hours Syd designs ads. which
are effective as well as artistic, and he was
responsible for the charming get-up of the
Ililder memorial volume. At home he
makes etchings and prints them on the same
press that “Hop” used over 20 years ago.
“Hop” was the pioneer of the etchers’ art
in Australia and induced a number of other
artists to take up the needle. The public
wasn’t interested and their enthusiasm died.
Now there is a demand for this form of
art, especially when it preserves what there
is of interest in our architectural past.
Syd Smith had made a great many pen
or pencil drawings of these relics and illus-
trated three or four books relating to Old
Sydney before he began to use the new
medium in earnest. Most of his etchings
are of the survivals of Macquarie’s building
frenzy, and the old mills and doorways are
handled with the gentle precision of one
who loves them. The N.S.W. Art Gallery
has bought five of these prints during
the year, a number are now at the Society
of Artists’ show, and early next month a
collection of them will be exhibited in the
Centreway, Melbourne, together with a few
black-and-white drawings and water-colors
by Norman Lindsay and some paintings by
J. R. Jackson.
Bishop Julius, of Christchurch (M.L.), is
giving up his episcopal palace and retiring
into a small humpy. No, not humility;
merely Mary Ann. It’s a shack or a board-
ing-house, and a boarding-house is ecclesias-
tically impossible. “Field Flowers Mel-
bourne, Fourth Floor,” would break up any
diocese.
Mrs. Dicky Richards had to send in her
papers to the Red Cross executive, and
everyone is sorry. Sydney’s gentle ex-
Mayoress isn’t improving in health, ana
she’s going to sit back for a while and watch
the other girls make the wheels spin.
That fine old sport Dr. Fiaschi, who did
such heavy bullockirtg at Lemnos Hospital,
is now with the Italian army. And as most
of the army is mounting dizzy heights,
Fiaschi will have to climb up after it.
From a Sydney girl at the Duchess of
Westminster’s Hospital:—
Out of some 40 Australian officers in hospital here,
somewhere in France, there are three D.S.O. s in-
cluding a mere babe of 21, and a captain at that
and two M.C.’s; and two others have V.C.’s in the
family: not bad, eh ? And there are lots more who
wish they wore the Billjim stars. A good many of
those who came across in the early days of the war
to get commissions are just aching to join the Aus-
tralian Army—now that they cannot. Two Aus-
tralian bands visit us weekly and amuse and inter-
est the French populace—which consists mostly of
convalescents —by the vigor and enthusiasm they
put into what the English call “their strange
music.’’ The members of one band gave a rattling
good concert the other night. But why, oh why,
does the reciter always choose Kipling in prefer-
ence to home-made verse and story ? It is
good to see the brown faces and jaunty hats; even
to hear an occasional good frank old blanky.
Dr. Lucy Gullett, back from the thrills of
surgical work in France, told the L- e d
Cross dames all about it the other after-
noon. A daughter of the late noted ;jour-
nalist Henry, she has a graphic style _of
condensing her facts. What France is doing
for the wounded, backed by the British Red
Cross, is nearing the perfection of human
organisation. The Sydney woman saw the
miserable wrecks, kindly returned by the
enemy when his internment camps have con-
verted them into hopeless consumptives.
Here we fatten the Hun till he has to puft
out his old refrain: “Ve vill vin—ve vill vin
in der longk run.”
The Russian duet, Mirovitch and Piastro,
passed through Sydney on their way to
Adelaide from Maoriland. There’s a hint ot
their coming back to Sydney either after
another look at the East or before they tap
the golden Orient.
Mrs. Edmund Fosbery, the homely old
lady whose husband was Sydney’s principal
policeman for so many years, died last week.
She passed away in Eaton, one of Darling-
hurst’s old homes, which turns its green
ficus whiskers to a road filled with the clatter
of trams. She was the one member of a
lively family—the Gordon girls are her
granddaughters —for whom Society pranks
had no lure.
John Mac Cormack has been inquiring how
everyone is at this comparatively peaceful
end of the world. You know what that
means. With all his personal charm and
silvery song, John is hard to handle, from
a manager’s point of view. So he may bring
himself, with the help of his devoted ally,
the Immense Me Sweeney.
The Babies’ Kit Society, now in 17 Bond-
street, is still pegging away at building and
rebuilding garments for desolate Belgian
infants. There’s a fairly comforting
thought in sending these kits across the
water. The German soldier can’t eat a
petti even though it’s only the size of Miss
Muffitt’s drapery; and he’s mostly too fat
to wear it. So baby is pretty sure to get
all that’s sent her. About 100,000 garments
have already gone. Hundreds of the tod-
dlers are in Northern France, in the care of
nuns, and other kits are distributed amongst
the baby refugees in England.
Dot Brunton opened the Peter Pan Club's
effort for the Alexandra Hospital. Tittell
Brune gave her name to the club in its
early days, so it likes to retain the theatri-
cal flavor. The party was in the roomy
garden of the Cornish mansion out Ocean-
street way. Secretary Clarence Moss told
the company of the dwindling funds of this
Children’s Hospital. And then a storm broke
like a gigantic packet of crackers, and the
frothy muslin frocks of the youngsters flew
across the lawn like balls of thistledown.
Leonard Redmond, of Charters Towers,
where father has a doctor’s practice, is
engaged to a former Bananaland girl.
Christina Williamson. She’s a cousin of
Mrs. Andy Fisher, and lives nowadays in
Sydney.
The cables told us of Captain de la Poer
Beresford’s marriage. The Australian man,
who is in the 10th Lancers, India, has been
staff-officer in France since the early days of
the war. The bride, Margaret Thynne, is
the daughter of an English parson.
Fancy the feelings of a burglar who got
away with the Russian order of St. Ann!
Yet that’s amongst the loot lately taken
from the Henry Katers’ house in Woollahra.
There’s a reward of £2OO for the missing
Ann and other valuables that belonged to
Kater, pere, B\R.S.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN`.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, November 27, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Steeplechase Day arrived at last on Satur-
day, and though it began with blazing sun-
shine, a thunderstorm crashed in and tried
to stop the big race itself with torrents
of tropical rain. The horses were then
lining up for the start and were held back
for a while in hopes ol a break, but the field
eventually scuttered through mud with the
rearmost horses and jockeys coming in
blinded with rain and flying clods. When
the heavens burst again secretary Byron
Moore had a despairing look in his eye. He
had come out in fine summer-day rig—pale
grey, with lovely cat’s-eye buttons jewelling
the waistcoat —and it may have been the
possible ruination of his lovely costume that
troubled him. In a twinkling the world of
Flemington seemed to turn back to its old
flooded state. People had to splash through
a river to reach the elms from the stand or
return from the birdcage to the lawn.
Women penned up in the members’ tea-rooms
were rescued by their menfolk with um-
brellas, and then the dainty white race-boots
and shoes had to go paddling. (Even poor
Billjim in the trenches has to paddle.) The
rattled tea girls and waiters fell into a panic
and rushed about, dashing crockery in
front of females who were wailing for justice
and tea. The thunder turned the milk sour
and curdled the temper of the tea drinkers,
for a ravenous horde had got in early and
devoured most of the food. Poor wretches
of women!
Before the Deluge there were some dainty
gowns on view. Mrs. Lavendo Tye wore
pink muslin and stuck a saucy little pastel-
pink hat over one eyebrow. She walked the
birdcage chaperoning two eligible young
bachelors, while hovering flappers viewed the
visitors with the appraising eye that punters
cast over the processional horses. Mrs.
Ambrose Pratt wore the white muslin of a
summer day. The Forrest dame’s niece
Muriel walked in muslin and blue spots.
Mrs. Andelosia Cooper’s frock was a dainty
all-in-one draping of dull blue and em-
broidery. Mrs. Leslie Macdonald got a great
lawn effect out of one garment of black
broadcloth that had been poured on to her;
there were no signs of fastenings or joinings
anywhere. A large, fat woman in navy blue,
with startled ospreys in her hat, topped the
whole with an absurd little Early Victorian
parasol—three inches of petunia silk and six
inches of lace. Mrs. Jeffries, in fawn, piloted
a fair daughter in black and gold. Gaud
Mayor Hennessy, in the fashionable mascu-
line pale grey, risked a pinch of gold on a
horse in the Steeplechase. Mrs. Tom Whyte,
in blue and white, scored with a gold-
embroidered veil. Mrs. Barry Thompson
wore very short blue skirts, with silk stock-
ings and shoes to match. Mrs. Connolly’s
black taffetas race-coat had a fashionable
flare.
The huge crowd on the flat seemed to
melt in the rain. The first downpour was
so overwhelming that not one black speck
hesitated. A running tide of humans scur-
ried for the fences everywhere, and one girl
in white, making for shelter, took all the
jumps in great style, but was finally baulked
by the rose-garlanded fence before the
grandstand lawn. After trying this, she
retreated, amid applause, and steeplechased
back to the flat.
Just before Cup Day the dress specialist
at a big shop missed one of her precious
race-frocks. Instinct led her to Flemington
with two detectives in leash. They spotted
the masterpiece on a lawn promenader, and
there was a terrific scene, during which the
wearer all but fainted. She said she bought
the dress for £4 10s. from a woman un-
known. The usual yarn, of course : but in
this case it was true. At the City Court it
was proved that a woman had offered the
dream to several other persons. A barmaid
said she would have taken it, but there was
rather too much of her. The widow in dis-
tress was then discharged. Thereupon the
dress lynx, as authorised on proof of inno-
cence, gave the costume to her as a keep-
sake. The poor thing had earned it.
One young woman down for the races
almost caused a feminine riot. She is little
more than a bride and has so much money
that she was expected to show at least two
dazzling costumes per day. Instead of
doing so, she wore the same plain, comfort-
able coat and skirt to every meeting. This
in spite of the fact that she was staying at
the smartest hotel in the midst of the chronic
over-dressers.
The wails of racing women were loud in
the land at the end of last week. The rich
young Falkiner dame, who has just taken
possession of Redcourt, her new Dandenong-
road town house, arranged a patriotic party
and fete for Saturday, and heaps of the most
swaggah young matrons engaged themselves
in advance as stallholders or assistants,
never dreaming that bad weather would
string out the Flemington races till the
postponed Steeplechase fell on that date.
Hastings Cooper, a paper merchant and
rich young eligible widower, is back from
an American trip still unattached. Mel-
bourne girls noted the fact with sighs of re-
lief. The young man was prominent during
the mild race-time frivols and made the
flapper diners-out yearn prettily when he
talked of caf€ dinners in New York. One
youth with a lot of money he hadn’t earned
gave a dinner at which girls danced on glass
above the diners’ heads. The houris let
themselves down on golden wires to snatch
champagne glasses from the masculine guests.
Just the sort of innocent merriment for
war time. At another dinner party (men
only) a huge melon was served to each
guest. As the melon was cut out leaped a
lovely dancing deaconess and handed her
liberator a tract. At least, I hope that’s
what happened.
Kerriesdale, the homestead and squattage
of rich mining-man Doolette, has been
bought by the Etheridges, and Mrs. Etheridge
celebrated it by giving a race-time tea-party
that stretched the whole length of the new
Australia supper-room. The affair reeked
with war-time economy. The dishes were
served in confectionery baskets; the straw-
berries reposed in a bed of sugar roses
veiled in a gold spider’s web of spun sugar;
the fish savories came out of a basket
of coral and pearl shells. High, slender sil-
ver vases held orchids. Mrs. Saddler, of
Toorak, wore black and veiled-gold trim-
mings; Mrs. Thompson (Brighton) nodded
pink roses over her coffee; and Mrs. Barry
Thompson wore brown fluffy frills and a
Dolly Varden hat. Mrs. Connolly’s hat
boasted pansies, like real flowers, flattened
on the crown. The hostess was clad in blue,
with a cute little French cockade in her
hat. Mostly, as you will see, a sporting lot.
Secretary Byron Moore has had such
worries this race-time in looking over Flem-
ington when it is in flood that mere women
wonder how he keeps his lovely flowing beard
dry. He never wears it bundled into a
bathing-cap on race days, and it must be
quite as much worry as the long flowing
locks of womenkind. A mere woman can
never go bathing without resorting to the
rubber cap or spending an hour or two getting
the mane dry. That lovely, well-groomed
beard is a wonder.
BYRON MOORE.
When Melba slid off to Sydney en route
to Honolulu, Spencer-street writhed with ex-
citement. The application for platform-
tickets was like the storming of the gallery
early-door on a first-night, and the singer’s
carriage resembled a florist’s shop. Whilst
telegram boys strolled up furiously with
farewell wires “Melba’s girls” swarmed round
the window and yelped for notice. Some-
where handy stood the two Honolulans,
young Carruthers and Peggie Center, the
latter wearing a pretty bonnet and a bad
toothache. The station was decorated with
George Allan, John Lemmone and Fritz
Hart, in various shades of loyal dejection ;
whilst daughter-in-law Mrs. George Arm-
strong winked away a tear as the Conserva-
torium girls tipped a stave of “Auld Lang
Syne.” Finally, Julius Knight, wearing a
very pink shirt and beads of perspiration,
flung a bunch of Beaucaire roses after the
disappearing train.
Mrs. lies is in Melbourne on a visit. Her
husband is a young Tasmanian who has
made his name as a mining expert in Lon-
don. He picks up something like £5OOO a
year just for giving a little advice now and
then. Mrs. lies is an Englishwoman and is
trying to overcome her terror of submarines
so as to return to hubby. On her journey
hither she saw two boats torpedoed.
Dr. Janet Greig is in hospital suffering
from severe concussion. She was knocked
down in St. Ivilda-road by a motor-cyclist
while waiting for a tram outside her own
gate.
1 Chaplain William Hall, who was senior
instructor at the Naval College at Jervis
Bay before he got back to his job with the
British bovs, has been killed. Hall, though
good at his work, was a neurotic sqbject
who did not hit it with our Naval Board.
His wife, who lived with a son and daughter
at Toorak whilst the Naval Instructor was
on duty at Geelong (one simply could n’t
live in Geelong, you know!), openly ad-
mitted an antipathy to Australia and Aus-
tralians. Naturally it did not help her
socially. When last heard of the naval cleric
had a job on a battleship.
Western District heiress Violet Thornley,
who drifted to London with the Fred Fair-
bairn family and Ivath McCulloch in search
of a war job, was disappointed. Anyhow,
she is coming back to help with Australian
patriotics.
From “Rosna,” of London: —
The newest weddings in this Isle included those
of Capt. Frank Weir, of the Ist Light Horse, who is
one of the Deniliquin Weirs, to Miss Isherwood, of
Manchester; and Lieut. Leslie Marks, son of the
late Albert Marks, one time of Maoriland, to Lea
Benjamin, daughter of the Saunders Benjamins, who
are now dwelling at Hampstead.
The Montague Laings—he is managing director
for Sargoods in London and hails from Wellington
(M.L.)—are mourning the death of their elder
daughter, Mollie, a charming and popular girl, who
has been strenuously war-working, like most Miss
Anglo-Australanas. The Laings’ only son Charles
has a commission in the Royal Scots Fusiliers and
won a Military Cross for bravery at Gallipoli.
Engagements that have just burst upon Anglo-
Australia include that of Harold Penfold, who has
a commission in the Royal Engineers and is a son
of Dr. Penfold, of Bendigo, to Erica Blunden, of this
Isle.
Young Keith George, of the chiffon family,
who laid down his job as advertising mana-
ger of the firm in time to get away with our
first Broadmeadows lot, has been killed in
France. For two long years he was in the
firing-line, where he earned his commission.
A mate who dined with this good-looking
young Australian when he was in London on
short leave, says he had become almost
stone-deaf from shell shock, a matter which
worried him not at all. A pretty fiancee
mourns with the dead soldier’s people.
Mrs. Fred Rowe is killing the fatted
turkey for daughter Jessie (Mrs. Richards),
who is arriving from India with her soldier-
husband, now on furlough. He is a wireless
man of high degree, and before the war used
to plant in Ceylon.
The Philip Mortons of Sydney are tarry-
ing in Melbourne after the Cup, because
Mrs. Philip has to. She is recuperating in
Mount St. Evins hospital after a successful
operation by Dr. Tom Dunhill. The Mel-
bourne florists are praying that her recovery
may be slow though sure, as their shops are
ransacked daily for presentation blooms for
the invalid’s room. Pretty girleens Ruth
and Jean decorate dull old Eastern Hill
during their frequent visits to cheer the
sufferer.
The death of Brig.-General Duncan Glas-
furd will sadden a lot of friends. Glasfurd,
a fighting Scot, had served with the Argyle
and Sutherland Highlanders, and came to
Australia as director of military training, in
which job he did excellent but unostentatious
work. He leaves behind him three young
boys, one of whom has just entered Osborne
College; the other two are still at school in
Geelong. Mrs. Stewart Balmain acted as
foster-mother whilst she was here, Glasfurd
being an old regimental mate of her hus-
band’s. During her absence solicitor Ted
Fitz Gerald has kept an eye on the fatherless
kiddies.
Adelaide’s diary for the week :—•
The Acacias, the Edwin Smith home at Marryat-
ville, was handed over to a committee on Saturday
as a money-magnet for the benefit of the Queen’s
Home. For a change the weather was fine, and cash
rolled in. A couple of good concerts in the ball-
room and picturesque tea- and sweet-stalls helped to
swell the purse. The Cheer-Up Society opened its
billiard-room on the same afternoon, and Brig.-
General Irving was the marker. That day, too,
Prospect staged its postponed “repatriation” car-
nival. The Governor attended this show, and the
rush on the early-door tickets brought in an extra
few shillings.
Good reports arrive of the work done by Merle
Robertson, the Elder Scholar, who is at the Royal
College of Music, London. Sir C. H. Parry sums
the results up: “A capital and creditable record for
the first term.”
Olive Norman, who left as a nurse for the
French Red Cross League, is now at work at the
Hospital Militaire Anglais, Limoges, after a spell in
London.
There is still a bit of money about, wherefore
Grenfell-street is to have new buildings. Some tumble-
down places near Gawler Place are to make way
for new premises for Harris-Scarfe’s, and Tatter-
sail’s Club is pulling down the quarters it has
occupied for the last 22 years so that it may build
new ones on the site. Time was when the spot
was occupied by a one-storey house in which the late
Dr. Gosse had his surgery, and after him came G.
and J. Morphett and R. I. and W. Stow.
The omission of Mount Schank, near Mount Gam-
bier, from the nomenclature committee’s list of
names to be changed, upset some patriotic spirits
until it was pointed out that the hill was called
after a British admiral who laid the foundations of
the Society for the Promotion of Naval Architecture.
In the midst of the ignorance a descendant has
dropped a note to W. J. T. Clarke to say that the
family owes nothing to foreign blood, and that
Schank is a good old British name. (Hear, hear.)
The Red Cross Committee has been faced with
the problem that confronts Red Cross committees in
other States, i.e., what use to make of Rest Homes
when English hospitals are absorbing our wounded.
In agreement with the military authorities it has
been decided to keep the Mareeba Home going, and
to temporarily cut down the duties and hours of
attendance of the Voluntary Aids. But don’t let
that interfere with your enthusiasm for the support
of the ordinary work of the Society.
The engagement is announced of Nancy, second
daughter of the C. L. Taylors, of Strathalbyn, to
Hubert, the third son of the late Mortimer Gileses.
The soldiers’ toy factory is so rushed with orders
that larger premises are wanted. Already contracts
have come in from as far afield as Westralia, and
the returned Billjims are enthusiastic about the
job, which, incidentally, is being run on the co-
operative system, under the management of the State
War Council.
The crowd that gathered to celebrate J. J. Virgo’s
30 years of Y.M.C.A. work nearly filled the. Exhibi-
tion Hall on Wednesday evening, when the Governor
and other wellwishers said their complimentary piece.
“J.J.,” an Adelaide boy, began as General Secretary
of this State’s branch of the Association 30 years
ago, and graduated to headquarters at London. This
week’s appeal for funds for Adelaide’s new Y.M.C.A.
building brought in nearly £13,000, including a
bequest of £lOOO made by the late R. Barr Smith
and £2600 which was in reserve.
The Repatriation Day Committee has its nose
well to the grindstone over the arrangements for
December 15, which are to be much the same as
those that ruled Australia Day. Mrs. Crawford
Vaughan is chief miracle-worker of the committee
that will feed the multitude on Parliament House
lawn, at the Exhibition Building, in the Town Hall
and at Victoria Square. The Repatriation race meet-
ing brought nearly £l5OO to the Fund, and other
nettings have scooped over £2OOO.
Mary Kitson, a daughter of a well-known detective,
will, all going well, be admitted to the Bar next
year. She will be our first LL.B. Novelist Doris
Egerton Jones tried her hand at the horsetail trade
several years ago, but gave it up. It was for her
that the Act allowing women to practise as solicitors
was originally passed.
George Marlow came to Melbourne for the
Cup that was filled with rain, and dashed
away in a fury on Friday’s express, the day
before the gallop. He is now in Brisbane
seeing that his wife is being nursed pro-
perly. The plump little lady has had hard
luck lately; she had only just recovered
from appendicitis when some careless hand
at a N. S. Wales mountain hotel left a cellar
trapdoor ajar. The convalescent fell several
feet and hurt her knee badly.
The Tommy Reynolds pair are coming to
settle in Melbourne (Australian playgoers
knew Mrs. Tommy as “Suzanne” Maggie
Jarvis). They have just taken the St.
Ivilda-road house of that Jacobs who is
brother of Justice Isaacs’s wife. The Jacobean
furniture was sold last Monday—a dusty
north-wind day that took all the starch and
enthusiasm out of the cloud of dames who
wafted along to have a good inspection of
the innards of the house.
Melba’s stage manager, Rigaud, is still in
Melbourne putting in a quiet time at Coombe
while the chieftainess is away.
Bindley, of the Bendigo Conservatorium of
Music (located in Collins-street), is whip-
ping up local enthusiasm for his 21st Stu-
dents’ Concert this week.
The Con. pupils—more especially Melba’s
bunch —are involved in a forthcoming pro-
duction at the Playhouse, when Gluck’s
three-act opera “Iphigenia” will be given
with full orchestra and chorus of 70 voices,
under the direction of Fritz Hart. It will
all happen on Thursday evening, December 7.
Melba, of course, will be away, but she has
had a great deal to do with the rehearsals.
The aesthetic Carruthers, who is travelling
back to Honolulu on the same ship, proved
himself “a perfect dear ” by stencilling lovely
robes for the chorus. He bequeathed the
balance of his job to Mrs. Fritz Hart when
he departed, and she has been working hard
at it ever since. The girls have helped at
the more prosaic stitching. Little Mrs. Fritz
is crowing with delight over a new present.
While she was on a visit to Coombe Melba
was exhibiting a case full of antique jewel-
lery and presented the visitor with an old
turnip watch that looks as if it had a steam
engine inside.
Mrs. Boyce Gibson, wife of the Univer-
sity professor, is going to improve our minds
at the Repertory Club on Tuesday, Decem-
ber 5, by expounding Rabindranath Tagore,
the Hindu poet. The fragile little lecturer
is versed in Sanskrit and studied Oriental
languages at Cambridge and Paris. She
carries her weight of learning lightly and is
an intensely domesticated little woman with
five boy children. The proceeds' of the lec-
ture go to the Reps.’ patriotic sewing-circle,
the treasurer of which is Mrs. Garran, wife
of the Solicitor-General, and once Hilda
Robson, of Sydney.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Bridletrack”: I reckon the sugar-bag has
a place on the honors list beside the bottle and
kerosene tin. Starting with the straight-out uses of
the 7Mb. bag, it makes a holdall, nosebag, apron,
combined cap and cloak for a rainy day, strainer
fpr separating honey from native beehive, firebeater
kdee-rester for a butcher’s boy, tucker-bag on
Matdda, doormat, fisherman’s bag, mailbag, floor-
cloth, saddle-cloth, Prince Albert (bush sock), and
moccasin (shearers’ footwear). Stretched round a
frame it makes a good, cool bush safe; filled with
grass or feathers it makes a pillow, and it can be
turned into use for a bush barber’s cloth. Two
sugar-bags sewn together and stretched on a frame
make a good camp stretcher, or suspended on ropes
make a hammock. Out in strips and fastened around
a piece of stick and placed in a tin of fat the
bagging makes a good wick for a slush lamp. Pilled
with dirt a number of the bags make a good
foundation for a dam or watercourse, or you can
use them as a barricade. On a frame they make
a good partition for a bush home, especially when
artistically covered with wallpaper. Five or six
sewn together make a tarpaulin or tent fly. Miners
u f e bags a S' reat deal for carrying quartz out
of shallow or small workings; whilst in the city
the good old 701 b. is the recognised measure for
marketing beans, peas, etc.
* * * .
“Aneb”: As a rejuvenator of horses’ legs
which show the effects of metal and blocks or
strained tendons, Stockholm tar stands right at the
top. Apply daily, warm, for three or four days, or
until the animal clearly shows that he’s satisfied.
Do alternate legs at the one operation, i.e., near
fore and. off hind, so that the prad can stand at
ease while the embrocation is working. Wait a
week and then start painting the off fore and near
hmd. Cut or clip the hair closely first. As an
internal remedy, too, I ask if any Abo-vet. can
recommend the tar’s equal. Dilute with equal parts
honey or treacle and apply to roots of tongue in
tablespoonful doses twice a day with a wooden
paddle. It at once relieves coughs and colds and
often pneumonia. The patients like it.
“Dead Finish” (B. 12/10/’l6),
on the natural rotation of crops, touches a subject
worth further investigation. In September, 1880,
after rain, a thin crop of wild carrots, an indigenous
plant closely resembling, in miniature, the domestic
variety, above and below ground, appeared among the
permanent grasses on the Thomson River (West Q.)
flats. None were seen again till 1886. Heavy rain
had fallen at Christmas, 1885, producing a handy
growth of Mitchell and blue grasses, but no rain
intervening the ground was bare by April. In May
good rains fell, and everywhere the grandest crop of
wild carrots imaginable flourished, but scarcely any
other grass grew till August, when Mitchell and blue
grasses came in their usual profusion. In 20 years
on or near the Thomson these were the only occa-
sions on which I noticed wild carrots, which, by the
way, are the most nutritious of Australian pastures.
* * * *
“Pick”: When Tom Muffet and the bro-
thers Walter and Harry Francis contracted to haul
an 18-ton boiler from Goulburn (N.S.W.) to the
Croker’s Mint sluicing claim on the Shoalhaven
River they strung 56 bullocks ahead of the iron-
mongery , which was loaded on a specially-built jinker
with wheels tyred 14in. wide. Even then blocks
and wire-rope tackle had to be employed to get the
load over miles of the Nadjidgomar rotten country.
Also, when Ned Tickner and his son Peter under-
took to shift an 8-ton gibber from the Windellama
marble quarry to Marulan, they displayed more bush-
man’s ingenuity and grit than did “Rag’s” Oaks Rush
navigators (B. 2/11/’l6). The Tickners had to
wallow through half a mile of bogland before they
struck solid going, and they did it with eight horses
pulling ahead of 20 bullocks. Whenever the waggon
threatened to disappear in the Nirvana of slush the
drivers lifted her again by ramming bags stuffed with
bark under the wheels, and corduroying with saplings
to give the animals a foothold.
” * * *
“Vakai”: Recently I entered a Tongan
house wherein some 20 natives were smoking Tongan
tobacco. Never again. Imagination can’t make me
feel more sorrow for the Hun-gassed Tommy than I
felt for myself. The weed is wrapped in a fibre
rope in “pricks” of four to 10 pounds, and stored
for a time, then unwound and cut off as required.
Sufficient for a smoke is dried over a hurricane-lamp
wrapped in banana-leaf, and lit. Some stenches can
be cut with a knife; an axe is necessary for this
effluvia.
* * * •
Bex”: “Lou” (B. 12/10/’l6) omitted to
state whether horse-clippers or nail scissors should
be used “to nip the heads” off mallee shoots. In
fact, she failed to mention so many things that I
suspect she is pulling our ankle. If the land is in
crop the shoots must be cut off low enough to escape
the stripper, otherwise the wheat buyer will be
docking for mallee leaves. The surest exterminator
of the curse is fire, and the only present practicable
method of burning the shoots is with the aid of
stubble. Generally speaking a running fire can be
got with an oat stubble; failing this, the fire rake
must be used. Three implements are necessary to
exterminate mallee—the plough, the slasher and
the fire-rake. And the greatest of these is the fire-
rake.

“South Ors”: Charley had come down
from Nullabor Plains, and a week’s tramp through
sand > s P inifex > waterless waste and a temperature of
114 deg. had given even this nigger a Christian thirst
He reached the township
1 y & Sunday m ° rmns , and

m^n^^ed w ith the sound of a wheezy harmonium,
out of the little church. Charley crept in on
dusty feet just at the moment when the reverend
gentleman in charge was in the act of passing the
sacramental cup. Black brother knew what this act
of Christian worship meant, and promptly fell into
an attitude of devotion. The pilot hesitated and
then put the cup into Charley’s eager hands. There

black finished it with gusto. As he handed back the
empty vessel he whispered into the glory-trader’s ear
“Fill ’im up agen, boss; me plenty Christian.” ’
“E.S.S.”: In reply to “Splodger” (B.
2/11/’l6), the Darling and Murray rivers used to be
the great breeding-places of the pelican; also Bulloo
Lake and other large sheets of water farther west,
especially the lignum-lined lagoons of Western
Queensland. Its big nest of sticks and herbage is
built on sheltered shores above high-water level. The
eggs, usually two, are yellowish-white. The bird
plants its nest farther back as settlement advances.
Though never very wild it is a lover of quiet places.
The deep pouch under the oumbersome-looking bill is
a handy food-carrier when there are voracious young-
sters to feed and long distances to travel with the
dinner. Bushmen assert, too, that the bird also
carries in the pouch a supply of food and water to
sustain it on long flights. Some of those flights cover
a thousand miles, and still leave * the traveller a
flutter. When food is not very plentiful the huge
pouched-bill is used for lifting mud up from the
bottom of a lake or lagoon to the surface, where it
is strained through the beak for the purpose of ex-
tracting any animal life it may contain.
* * * *
“Huffin-Guffin”: Re “Aneb’s” query as to
using bisulphide of carbon to exterminate rabbits (B.
2/11/’l6). As much as 30 years ago we employed
carbon on Crown lands. This was the method. Fit
a plug of cotton-waste on the end of a piece of stick
two or three feet long. Pour the carbon over the
waste till it will absorb no more. Put this as far as
you can reach into the burrow and into all openings
connected or apparently connected thereto. Then well
cover the mouth of every burrow with sand, heaping
it up a foot or so, and beat well down with the back
of a shovel to block possible gas escapes. A lighted
match thrown at the mouth of one burrow after all
have been gassed, but before the final closing-up, will
produce a terrific bang, but no earth movements to
speak of. I don’t know whether bunny likes his
carbon raw or cooked; I rather think the raw stuff
was most effective. We rarely saw burrows that had
been opened from the inside. Don’t think the
weather makes much difference, only in the dry
season the sand needs careful beating down with the
shovel. Being dry then it is apt to run. P.S. —
Don’t pour carbon over your hands. It burns.
* * * *
“Beeritus” : Let me throw in a word for
the camel as a draught animal. In the north-east
of South Aus. the old style of camel-packing has
been almost entirely superseded by the collar and
hames. The collar is very like a horse’s, but
is put on with the opening underneath; hames
likewise. A team- of draught camels harnessed to
an ordinary waggon will easily pull half a ton each,
even on bad roads. Pack the same team and scwt.
each will be about their limit. The stations in the
North are entirely dependent on the “ship of the
desert” for transporting stores, etc., and it is now
rare to see the beast packed, except when in charge
of Abdul. He still sticks to the idea of his fore-
fathers, trudging along in front of a long string
of the animals with swaying packs, and the accom-
panying sore and fly-blown backs. Strange that the
white man, in spite of his comparative newness to
the game, can get more work out of a camel than
the ’Ghan, and he does not secure this result by
adopting Abdul Gunnybag’s system of a mallee rail
and a strong arm, either.

THE ROAD TO PALESTINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When through waxing dawnlight grey
Ceased the moon to beam on high,
And the lifting sun of day
Purple flushed the limpid sky,
Slow the pilgrimage began,
Like a lengthy caravan,
Heading east, in ragged line,
On the road to Palestine.
Trekked we through the wilderness,
Where the Moslem, battle rent,
Hurried east in dread distress,
Hunger weak and travel spent.
Trudging in the early morn
Down the soft and sandal-worn
Path where ancient Moses trod
At the bidding of his God.
’Twas a march of modern feet
Crunching through the yielding sand;
Never stirred by scent of sweet
Vineyards in a promised land.
Ere the white full glare of day
Camped we where the palm-trees sway,
Where the camel-keepers dwell,
By the dark seductive well.
Soared the sun enwreathed in smoke,
Trailing war-smoke dun and black,
And a low-hung cloud-wrack broke
When his furnace door swung back.
Slow the spent, unburdened horse
Dropped with hollow-eyed remorse
In the amber-spotted shade
Of a green oasis glade.
But the ruthless fire-edged blast,
Blown from burning sands at noon,
Mellows when the day is past,
Chills at peep of star or moon.
Like the wash of summer seas,
Sounds the gentle desert breeze
Through the leafy, drooping calm
Of the star-enchanted palm.
Oft the loaded rifles swayed,
Shoulder slung at dead of night;
Every trim and tempered blade
Scabbard-locked and out of sight.
Brooding clouds crept up the sky
When the escorts thundered by,
And we surged into the black
Vagueness of a formless track.
Out where heat waves madly dance,
East, the mounted troopers go,
Wide a-flank and in advance
O’er the drift hills, trekking slow.
Void of vine and flower and tree,
Song of bird and hum of bee,
Stars with double lustre shine
On the road to Palestine.
SINAI.

“Ses”: Most parrots are content with the
primitive unlined spout for a nest. These are as
easy to find as magpies’ nests, for the parrot is a
fool bird that gives itself away all the time. When
the nest is in a green hollow the parent birds chip
the bark off around the entrance, a habit common
to nearly the whole family, from the large cockatoos
down to the little greenleeks, and thus advertise their
domicile as clearly as if they hung a brass plate on
the front door. Further, the everlasting squawk of
the young ones locates the nursery. Almost any
small hollow suits the parrot, whether it be at the
top of a lofty gum, near the root of a small box or
gidya or in a rock. An exception is the ground
parrakeet. It frequents scrubby country, heath
lands and swampy flats. Its dress is a beautiful
green and black richly flecked with yellow, with a
long, speckled green and yellow tail. Shy and wary,
the owner is seldom seen until flushed from the rank
grass, though dogs easily find it by its strong scent. Its
flight is low and undulating. It and the myrlumbing
(night or spinifex parrot) are the only parrots to
construct a nest —a hole under a tussock, or among
tufts of rushes, well lined with grass: a deep and
rather neat repository for three or four oblong, white
eggs. The flesh is excellent, quite equal to
that of snipe or quail. The lovable little creature
has always had a hard time. Blacks levied a heavy
toll on it by robbing its nests, and besides nosing
dogs, native cats and browsing stock, the ever-ready
gun has made its feeble twitter a rare sound in its
native haunts. Now with the spread of the hated fox
its lot is a sadly precarious one.
* * f *
“Glendower”: Can any Abo-liar trot up a
clan with such a choice assortment of handles as
the Kellys own? Here are a few that I have been
introduced to while swinging the banjo up in N.Q.:
Kelly the Rake, Kelly the Rager, Cast-iron Kelly,
Broken-nosed Kelly, Kelly the Hum, Dog-faced
Kelly, Trooper Kelly and Rhinoceros Kelly—who has
the reputation of being able to kick up more row
than any six other Kellys combined. I heard him
once, and he didn’t injure his reputation in the
least. The occasion was when a mate became a
little too familiar, and had the temerity to address
him with his handle abbreviated. I have recollections,
also, of a bloque called Motor-car Kelly, but as he
appears to have lost his honk I will let him R.I.P.
* * * *
“Pick”: Don’t waste time waiting for a
kangaroo to jump 31ft. on the level, because the
hopper to do it hasn’t been built. At Windellama
(N.S.W.) I once saw a full-grown grey flier, admit-
tedly the fastest of the local ’roo tribe, pursued
by a noted galloper among kangaroo-dogs of his
day. The race was along a level road for half a
mile, and the flier won, after a most desperate burst,
by clearing the fence into an orchard. Subsequent
measurement showed the jumps to be 27ft. with
undeviating regularity.
“Waipa”: At the time of the Maori war,
in the Waikato district, army transport was all
done by horse, and there were an awful number
of jibbers, chiefly through incompetent or drunken
drivers. Mick Hogan, a road contractor living near
Otahuhu, bought all those jibs, and this is how he
dealt with them. He had a paddock with a steep
declivity running across it. Taking a pupil to the
top Michael would harness him to the skeleton of
a bullock dray. All jibs will back a little, and
when the one in hand backed to the brow of the
hill Hogan let go. Holy wars! away went the
whole shandrahan, whirling down the incline, with
a rattle you could have heard in the next shire,
head over tail, tail over head all the way. It’s
strange, but not two in ten horses would display
a scratch on arrival at the bottom, and not more
than three in ten ever required a second lesson. A
cute N.C.O. tumbled to the business and after an
interview with Mick the jibs greatly increased along
the line. Many of them were afterwards sold in
the Sydney market at from 60 to 80 guineas each,
and if the M.L. scrap had lasted half as long as the
Kaiser’s Hogan could have bought out Anthony
Hordern.
* * * *
“Yarrum”: Somebody asks the greatest
distance ridden on horseback in a day; and here
is my contribution. I rode from Umaralla, 15
miles the Queanbeyan side of Cooma, to Bega—99
miles—in much under 24 hours. I started at six
o’clock in the morning and reached the Bank Hotel,
Bega, at 11.30 p.m. Got off only once—at Nimity-
belle, for dinner—and when I told publican
Jimmie Kiss, a man who knew a heap about
horses, that I was going right through that day,
he said I’d never get there. He advised me to put
up at Numbugga (Garfield) for the night, but as
the mount was fresh at Tantawanglo I chanced it.
It was a tough journey; but in those days horses
were bred for endurance. Black Tommy was
the name of my horse, and he was well known at
Wollongong, where my brother reared him from
Denmark and a Sir Charles mare.
* * * #
“Try Fluke”: My Urewera bush-acquaint-
ance, Eria Rankura, gives me a recipe for gunshot
wounds. Whenever you are plugged, just reach out
for a supplejack vine, char the end in the fire and
with it cauterise the bullethole good and hard. Then
plug up with clay and fill your pipe and trust
in the Atua. The supplejack cauteriser usually did
the trick in bad flesh-wounds in the war days, says
Eria, but it was advisable to keep the prayers going
regularly for fear the Atua might turn rusty.
Another medicine-man, a Taranaki Maori, once
told me that the boiled juice of the mucilaginous
roots of the flax-bush was the best thing out for
gunshot wounds and bayonet jabs. And in his day
the big Enfield bullets made a considerable mess
when they arrived.

“Dead Finish”: Some of the many aliases
the bush-worker has for treacle: Cocky’s joy, bul-
locky’s delight, scrub-cutter’s agony, jackeroo’s night-
mare, rabbiter's friend, boundary rider’s stand-by,
rouseabout’s horror and C.S.R.
THE WORRIES OF MARRIED LIFE.
THE ANGRY YOUNGSTER: “ It’s no good — a, woman always manages to get what she
wants!”
THE CYNICAL OLDSTER: “What worries me is that as soon as she gets it she wants
something else.”
“Hay”: I have followed and captured kan-
garoos in various ways, and often went to the
trouble of measuring their strides. When hard
pressed about 19ft, is a fair average of the
length of the leaps. When closely pursued by
dogs the kanga’s gait looks more like running
than bounding - , as he doesn’t rise very high off the
ground. The pace then is wonderfully fast. I saw
a full-grown ’roo hunted by dogs dash straight at
a newly-erected five-wire sheep fence. He threw
himself against the tightly-strained wires with a
terrific crash. All five wires were broken and a post
lifted clean out of the ground into which it had
been planted 2ft. Except for a few muscular quivers
poor ’roo never moved again. Once I amused myself
watching the antics of a half-grown ’roo. The
animal was playfully leaping from side to side,
varying the performance by bounding high in the
air. I believe when at play a flier can leap as many
feet upward as it can over level ground.
* * * *
“McNorth” : I have found the ammonia
prescription for the sting of the blue-bottle, or jelly-
fish, or Portuguese man-o’-war, or whatever the float-
ing blue horror is called, of little use here in Torres
Strait. Perhaps we hfre a more poisonous variety.
After rubbing the tentacles off with sand, the appli-
cation of a towel wrung out in boiling water will
give relief. But the marks remain for life, which
illustrates the virulency of the acid, or whatever it
is, that is secreted by the tentacles. In these late
months of the year the waters of the western side
of Cape York become infested with the stinging
blue devils. It’s quite common to get a couple of
hundred of them in one haul of a 50-fathom drag-
net. There appear to be two varieties, almost iden-
tical in appearance; but while one can give its
victim a realistic taste of the things Dante pictured
in his Inferno, the other is quite harmless.
“Barcoo”: “The Hum’s” horses that have
been chewing the bark off his trees evidently
sojourn in a sour coastal area, where, after the
passing of winter, the animals are liable to suffer
from intestinal worms. So they have gone to their
own chemist’s shop for a supply of tannin, to clear
up the vermin..
* * * *
“Alidon” : There is a variation of the gun-
bursting feat much more artistic than the crude
methods described by recent Abfe. correspondents. Let
thenr try this. Load the rifle, and, holding it perpen-
dicularly, place a penny flat upon the muzzle. Then
press the trigger, and watch the coin and your head
being blown off simultaneously. The less-than-
momentary check caused to the air in the barrel will
scatter the steel to shreds.
* * * *
“Tiaki”: There are some very hollow
places in Maoriland. At Kereru station the people
lately heard dreadful rumblings from the stomach
of the overtaxed country. Horses and cattle acted as
if under the influence of mal de mer, running about
moaning and roaring in terror. I lived up in that
country (inland from Napier), and about the back
of Owhaoko station, towards the rear of the Kai-
manawa mountains, there are holes in the ground
that would hide all the sins of the Kaiser and the
National Debt into the bargain. Boulders rolled
down them grumbled and rumbled for fully half
a minute, and long after they seemed to have
reached the roof of I-lell they could be faintly heard
doing fresh leaps at a depth calculated to make a
man draw back and shudder. One explanation is
that they are volcanoes or fumaroles which died in
their infancy. I don’t like them.
* * * *
“Pick”: In warm, damp seasons thickly-
grassed meadows are affected with the red rust
nark-. I have seen clouds of the rust spores rising
into the air behind South Coast (N.S.W.) dairy herds
as they were being chased to the bails. Somebody
suggested a couple of seasons’ rest from wheat-
growing in Australia to allow the rust to die out.
There will be something doing when he further advo-
cates a secession from grass-growing by the cow
cookies.
* * * *
“Wee Mac”: How is this for a horse’s
endurance? A few weeks ago a crippled nag was
found inside the railway fence about a mile on the
Stanthorpe side of Dalveen (Q.). It had evidently
been injured by a train during the night. The two
hind feet were severed at the fetlocks, and, although
it’s most incredible, the poor beast was walking
about on the stumps.

“HEARNE’S”
Dear Bulletin,. — Re “Damaged Goods”
or “Damned Bads” or whatever it is. I
met with a “romance” in the long ago, out
back, in the same line. As follows: Only
son of wealthy pub-keeper contracted syph.,
unknown to himself. He consulted me. He
was riddled with “secondaries.” I told him.
He was shocked, and very properly brought
his young woman (the laundress at the
pub) for examination. She was ditto.
Father got to know —how I can’t say. He
sacked the laundress. Her folks found out.
They turned her out of doors. Then the
only son did —what? He was a man. He
married her. Then his folks kicked him
out and he went bushwhacking. Meanwhile
both were under as vigorous a treatment as
existed a quarter of a century ago, with
very good results. Next she came to
to engage me to attend her impending con-
finement. Treatment pushed. Child born,
with all the stigmata of congenital syphilis.
It looked like a diseased monkey. Child
put under treatment. Exit me—left district.
Successor warned about the cases and treat-
ment continued. Eater enter me—as locum
tenens. Scene: Billiard-room in father’s
pub. Curtain rises, discovering locum play-
ing 100 up with the policeman. Man sitting
on form with strapping two-year-old on his
knee. Locum: “Hello. That’s a fine kid!
Yours?” Man: “Yairs! You ought to
know!” Locum: “How so?” Man: “You
brought it into the world.” Locum: “Very
likely. Can’t remember.” Man : “Don’t you
remember me?” Locum: “No.” Man:
“Why, I’m !” (the only son). Locum:
“Good God! And how’s your wife?” Man:
“We’re all as fit as fiddles. Never had a
day’s illness —since. And we’re back with
the old man.” Tableau and curtain. —Yrs.,
Dr. Nil. (P.S. —No rights reserved.)
“E.D.”: Melbourne Bijou revisited. I was
long ago driven from this little house, affec-
tionately regarded as a storeroom of pleasant
memories, by an abominable outbreak of
mirror. The place was literally walled with
the outrages. I find they have been torn
out, and the Bijou is almost itself again.
One thing to be grateful for is the preserva-
tion of the old frieze above the proscenium,
the finest bit of mural decoration in Mel-
bourne, so far as my knowledge goes. It
represents all the arts of the theatre bring-
ing their gifts to Minerva for judgment, and
must be visual to all old Vic. theatregoers by
the simple process of shutting the eyes. The
same frieze decorated the original Bijou, and
came unscathed through the fire that de-
stroyed the theatre 20-odd years ago. It has
escaped an inrush of water and other vicis-
situdes and the wear and tear of time, and
is now rather better than new, age having
mellowed its quality while investing it with
the reverence due to an ancient instigation.
Unfortunately it was no luck-bringer. It
has looked down upon many a frost.
Manly, more power to it, means to hold
its annual carnival as usual next year. Why
not? There’s a hungry cottage hospital to
be fed and a motor-ambulance to be bought
Secretary Mann gives January 10-13 as the
dates of the money-spinner.
“Glen H.”: That popular shriek of Bland-
olt days, “Women and Wine,” known to the
irreverent as “Flossie and Fizz,” is being
successfully revived in darkest London. Ethel
Warwick is playing the Most Horrible
Female in Paris —the one the Theatre Royal
programme was wont to refer to as “The
Woman With the Geological Heart,” or
words to that effect
Marie Lohr has made good in the Ameri-
can light comedy “Her Husband’s Wife,”
produced by Dot Boucicault at the London
New Theatre. Archer, of the Stab, says
she “has done nothing so good as her Irene
Randolph.” This Irene fancies she is going
to die, so sets about selecting a second
wife for her husband, her choice falling
upon a dowdy and not particularly juvenile
friend. The latter promptly dissipates the
dowdiness and transforms herself into a
very smart and attractive person who fasci-
nates the husband. Whereupon Irene, of
course, gets furiously jealous and decides
not to die.
“Norbell”: The film was “John Halifax,
Gentleman,” and when the flood waters
swamped the tannery the unimaginative
female at the piano broke loose with “A
Hundred Fathoms Deep.” Later, when
John Halifax heroically climbed a ladder
and rescued a cripple, the lady obliged with
“Up I Came with My Little Lot.” But she
really made a hit when the starving rioters
set fire to the Quaker’s mill. The incen-
diaries did their I.W.W. job to the stirring
strains of “Keep the Home Fires Burning.”
About six years ago a wild but harmless
English enthusiast named Westlake over-
ran Tasmania seeking information about
the vanished Black Brother of those parts.
He collected one ton of stones, alleged to
be aboriginal weapons and tools, though lots
of them didn’t look like it, and two tons
on information, and was going
the one and only real book about the xas
manian blacks and setae where they came
from and who they were. But he touna
his mass of material so heavy and he had
to track down so many side issues that
at latest advices the book was hanging •
When last heard from Westlake had a
theory that he had discovered the diary
and private papers of G. A. Robinson, the
man who brought in the last of
manian aborigines and afterwards looke
after them when they were cooped up on
Flinders Island. Robinson ought to have
known more about the blacks than any-
body, but his published remarks concerning
them are bald and uninteresting.
Out of a total of 2000 who have passed
through Launceston Grammar School
(Tas.) 370 are now on active service. As
the school dates from ’46, very many of
the 2000 are over the age limit. Among
the ones to win distinction are Frank
Thornthwaite (D. 5.0.), Brab Hallowes
(French Military Medal), Lex Evans and
John Webster (Military Cross). Word has
just come through that Raymond Sherwin
has also annexed a Military Cross and
received a letter of congratulation tioin
General Birdwood for taking charge at
Pozieres when all his superior officers haa
been casualtied. Before the war R ie ut.
Sherwin was a keen sport and shone m tne
lacrosse field. He is a cousin of songstress
Amy.
Byron Moore, of the V.R.C., has literary
leanings, sometimes indulges himself in
rhymes, and is understood to have a book ot
reminiscences in hand. It should be an in
structive human document. If the V.K..L.
secretary confined himself to his racing
experiences he could turn out a. volume with
the capacity of a bath tub, with an item ot
interest on- every page. A sample yarn from
this Spring Meeting tells of the newly-dis-
covered wife of a State official who waylaid
Byron Moore on the lawn on Cup Day in an
indignant mood, and wanted to know about
added weight in the case of Shepherd King.
“You’ve put a lot of weight on him, _ said
the lady, with the air of one exposing a
great public wrong, “to prevent him winning,
I suppose. Why wasn’t I told of that be-
fore I risked my half-sovereign?”
The natural history of Flanders: —
In our quiet sector, where the trenches are nearly
two years old, and where the only visiting from
either side is done by us in occasional raids on
Fritz’s front line, the rats have full possession. Not
even a gas attack will dislodge them. It is a serious
crime for a soldier to lose his “iron rations”——tin
of “bully” and broken-up biscuits, usually carried
in the haversack. The morning after we took over
this ground, all biscuits were missing, and many
were the holes in haversacks. Now we are waiting
to see if all will be crimed for the losses. The
little beasts have no respect for tired and sleeping
soldiers. A pair will get together and settle a
conscription argument on a sleeper’s chest. One
nibbled off a portion of a private’s ear. Another
whose luck was rotten was observed at daylight on
the parapet; he had stopped a Fritz bullet through
the night. Many now are the snares and contri-
vances set to catch a rat, and, incidentally, when no
officer is about, many are the Mark VII. bullets that
chase the wily devils to their holes.
“Sere”: I have just read the lament of
“W.H.R.,” who tells (B. 27/7/’l6) of the“care-
less loss of two Stokes mortars, which the
Huns captured during a night raid.” On the
night the mortars were lost the Huns sub-
jected this particular lot to a whirlwind
bombardment, which smashed the breast-
work (there are no trenches in this locality)
to smithereens. The bombardment was so
sudden and heavy that the crew of the guns
had very little chance of getting their
weapons away. The general belief is that
the mortars were buried yards deep in the
battered breastwork, and that the enemy
did not even see them. I have seen Billjim
infantrymen buried in these breastworks or
barricades, and their bodies have never
been recovered. At any rate if the Hun
did discover the secret of the Stokes, he
hasn’t thrown any back at us yet, which is
about the best possible proof that he scored
a clean miss.
Angelina is about to bid a long farewell
to her barber-pole and tiger-striped boots.
Apart from the fact that shoes are usually
worn in summer-time for comfort, the long,
loud boot is to be discarded because the
world’s dress-designers have decided the
matter. Angy’s legs are to go into early re-
tirement because ankle-length skirts are to
be the correct wear, with no “fancy” boot-
tops visible. In future Angelina will only
put on her swankish “gabardines,” done in
grey and scarlet and old gold, when she is
helping mummer with the washing-up. Con-
sidering the prices charged for this class of
frill, Angy’s papa will give three cheers when
he hears the news.
The fissure in the Federal Labor party led
to at least one diverting scene. The other
day one young member, who had clung to
Hughes and had been rewarded for _ his
tenacity with an official position, determined
to celebrate his rise from obscurity by
annexing the special apartment of a Tudor
follower. So one morning the young man
invaded the room, heavily armed with docu-
ments, books of great learning and other
signs of his sudden political growth, and
got everything nice and comfortable for a
long stay. While he was absent on some
mission or other, the real tenant turned up,
and as his figure is plentiful and his manner
pugnacious, the neighborhood somehow felt
that there would be something doing. Sure
enough the door of his sanctum had hardly
closed upon him when there was a noise
like a rogue elephant in disagreement with a
house-full of furniture. The suspicion that
he was upset about something was confirmed
by the rapid opening of the door and the
ejection of the intruder’s property. Papers
and books flew out in vicious haste, and quan-
tities of language hurried after. When the
chucker-out had cooled down and departed,
the cause of the mischief returned, gathered
up his scattered belongings, and without a
word stole away to the coalhole or some-
where to nurse the affront And all the
feather-dusters chortled. A youngster who
gets bumptious on a small dose of authority
deserves a chastening.
ROMEO AND JULIET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The scene was handled very well
A garden fair down C,
A high white moon, an old wall L,
With pleasant greenery.
The house stood R, and parallel
The usual balcony.
Came Juliet. All white was she.
She spoke her maiden woe
In accents of soft melody,
The secret none should know.
Then broke from ’neath a dreaming tree
The voice of Romeo.
Fair Juliet in terror sped,
In fear stole back again
To hear what Romeo had said.
Then sighed and sang the twain;
And Juliet twice vanished,
And twice she would remain.
Then as he moaned, a brick or so
Took him an awful bat,
And up the wall I saw him go,
His poor ribs beaten flat,
My very finest Romeo,
My midnight Thomas cat.
VICTORIA.
S.S.
Business, Bobbery, etc.
Another Victorian farmer, Henry Davies,
of Lake Boga, is making an experiment in
rice-growing. Along the Murray, up Swan
Hill way, there is a good deal of swamp-
land which has been more or less reclaimed
and is used for general agricultural purposes
when conditions permit. Davies is putting
five acres of this land under rice, and hopes
to get something out of it which he wouldn’t
get, in the present state of the soil, if he
planted the usual crops. So far no enthusi-
asm has been shown by the Department of
Agriculture, though the success of the ex-
periment will be another step towards
making this country self-contained.
* * * »
“Y.Z.”: My butcher insists that this war
has planted a horror of sausages in the Aus-
tralian breast. “Where we had 20 orders
for ’em two years ago,” he says, “we haven’t
seven now, and it’s much the same in all
the shops I know of. No, it’s not the natural
outcome of the scarcity of beef and mutton;
it is an established prejudice based on the
belief that the sausage is of enemy origin.
Mention sausages in your little list to a
housewife these mornings, and 10 to one she
snorts and asks if you take her for a
German.”
“Spanner”: “R.A.Y.” (B. 2/11/’l6) was
wasting his time when he expressed pity for
the inefficiency of machinery salesmen, their
employers and their customers. The old
cocky is kept alive to machinery improve-
ments by the travellers, who are always pre-
pared to back up their “mendacious loqua-
city” by field trials. That manufacturers
know their business is proved by their
success. But there is a type of mahogany-
headed cocky who drifts on to the land, full
of theory and imagined mechanical know-
ledge. He is the Johnnie-know-all who piles
up useless cultivators and harvesters. That s
nobody’s fault; for you can’t separate a fool
from his folly.
* • # *
“N. or M.”: The kelp that fringes Maria
Island, Bass Strait, and is to be harvested
for potash and iodine, if it is not wanted to
keep the sun off the rock-cod, has historic
interest. Peron, the French naturalist who
visited the place in ISO 2 on the Naturaliste,
commented on the “enormous beds of Fucus
giganticus, several plants being not less than
250 ft. to 300 ft. long.” Smith O’Brien, the
Irish exile, got badly tangled up in it when
trying to escape from the island in ’5l. John
Mitchel gives a vivid description of the inci-
dent in his “Jail Journal.” The “escape”
was a farce. The chartering of the schooner,
by friends in Hobart, to lie off the coast and
pick up O’Brien, gave the show away, and
the prisoner was removed to Port Arthur. It
was one Bernacchi who cast a speculative eye
on the huge bull-kelp and proposed tanning it
for shoe-leather. That was something like an
idea. The Southport experiment in potash is
a tame thing beside it.

“Fait Lux”: A letter has been returned
through the dead-letter office to the W.A.
Taxation Department with this inscription:
“Undelivered. Left for Berlin; last heard
of in France.”
* * * *
“N. E. Bones” : The bottle-oh and the haw-
ker, in the eyes of the average householder,
occupy much the same privileged position;
but it isn’t so in fact. The bottle-oh must
be an honest man in the eyes of the law,
since—in Victoria at any rate —a record
even of seven days’ gaol stops, him from
getting his license. The mere objection of a
John Hop at the licensing court is enough to
block Bill Bottle-oh and no questions asked.
But no Government license is issued to the
hawker. If a barrowman wishes to cart
cabbages around in a suburb such as Prahran,
where there is a municipal market, all he
has to do is to get his cart and cabbages and
pay his market-dues to the local municipal
authorities, who take his money and pass him
out without troubling to inquire . into _ his
morals; and in marketless municipalities
even this little formality is dispensed with.
There a man just out of a seven-years’ stretch
may snoop around the backyards, cabbage in
hand, without molestation by the police. His
cabbage is his complete justification. Gov-
ernment licensing of hawkers would j largely
reduce their number by weeding out the
numerous time-servers in the profession; but
the fact that it would decrease the number of
petty thefts and put a bunch of undesirables
to work doesn’t seem to count for much when
a loss to municipal finances is concerned.
THE PARSON'S PRESENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A parson once, by all admired,
Received, of high esteem the sign,
From those his goodness thus inspired,
A present of a cask of wine.
But lo! when soon he came to draw,
A miracle, in mode as rapid
But quite unlike what Cana saw,
Had changed his wine to water vapid t
The parson never knew the cause,
For miracles are things of mystery,
Though some, like this, have had their laws
Explained by facts of private history.
Those whom his lore did aptly teach,
Wished all to share the gracious task,
So planned to bring a bottle each
And pour the wine in one huge cask.
Now one, by chance, thought: “None will know,
And, with the wine of all my brothers,
One pint of water well may go.”
And so, by chance, thought all the others I
VICTORIA.
T. R. H.
