WHAT DYSPEPTICS SHOULD EAT.
A PHYSICIAN’S ADVICE.
Indigestion and practically all forms of
stomach trouble are, nine times out of ten,
due to acidity; therefore stomach sufferers
should, whenever possible, avoid eating
food that is acid in its nature or which by
chemical action in the stomach develops
acidity. Unfortunately, such a rule elim-
inates most foods which are pleasant to the
taste as well as those which are rich in
blood, flesh and nerve-building properties.
This is the reason why dyspeptics and
stomach sufferers are usually so thin, emaci-
ated and lacking in that vital energy which
can only come from a well-fed body. For
the benefit of those sufferers who have been
obliged to exclude from their diet all starchy,
sweet or fatty food and are trying to keep
up a miserable existence on gluten products,
I would suggest that you should try a meal
of any food or foods which you may like in
moderate amount, taking immediately after-
wards half a teaspoonful of bisurated mag-
nesia in a little hot or cold water. This will
neutralise any acid which may be present, or
which may be formed, and instead of the
usual feeling of uneasiness and fullness, you
will find that your food agrees with you per-
fectly. Bisurated magnesia is doubtless the
best food-corrective and antacid known. It
is not a medicine, and has no direct action
on the stomach ; but by neutralising the acid-
ity of the food contents, and thus removing
the source of the acid irritation which in-
flames the delicate stomach lining, it does
more than could possibly be done by any
drug or medicine. As a physician I believe
in the use of medicine whenever necessary,
but I must admit that I cannot see the sense
of dosing an inflamed and irritated stomach
with drugs instead of getting rid of the acid
—the cause of all the trouble. Get a little
bisurated magnesia from your chemist, eat
what you want at your next meal, take some
of the bisurated magnesia as directed above,
and see if I’m not right.— Advt.
POLITICAL
POINTS
There is talk of another big loan for
Australia. Still in the hands of the
Jhughes!
The Bulletin was wrong. Besides rob-
bing the conscription party of tens of thou-
sands of votes and seriously prejudicing the
Defence Act, Hughes’s tragic blunder in
calling up Class 1 before the Referendum
was held cost the country, not nearly a
million, but over a million. The camps
alone ran away with £600,000; a couple of
hundred thousand men were non-productive
for a month, which means a loss of £500,000
or thereabouts; and thousands of others who
were passed out or exempted lost from one
to three days. One way and another Hughes
is proving a rather expensive “statesman.”
Durack, the temporary leader of the rem-
nant of the N. 8. Wales Labor party, has
been elected permanently to the position over
John Storey, the stop-gap chairman during
the embroglio with the last P.L.L. conference.
Even in politics possession may thus be
taken as nine points of the law. Storey
preferred to stay out of the band altogether
rather than work a second fiddle. So the
deputy-leadership goes to James Dooley, the
Lithgow miners’ representative. Dooley is
one of the little baud of thrashers who,
within party ranks, kept the former Holman
Government diving for its life.
It would just about crystallise the situation
if “the public recognition of Mr. Gregory
Wade’s great services” was solemnised at
the Town Hall by Premier Holman present-
ing his friend, amidst tumultuous applause,
with a frozen schnapper.
A FELLOW FEELING.
INDIGNANT SUBSCRIBER: “What the devil
do you mean by printing the birth of
my tenth kid under 24 Distressing
Occurrence’?”
COUNTRY EDITOR: “ ’Struth, that must
’ave been that foreman again! He’s a
married man himself!”
The so-called National Government in N.
S. Wales didn’t have to wait long to see
somebody following its bad precedent. The
week after it had refused to appeal to the
ballot, preferring to disfranchise the electors
for 12 months, the coal-miners said, “That’s
good enough for us!” and turned down the
ballot, too. Even the hardy old S. M.
Herald had to pull a wry face as it con-
demned them so soon after approving the
same tactics in its new political friends.
Minister for Education James says that
the N. S. Wales Education system must be
expanded. Of course. But The Bulletin
would urge on James to start at the right
end. In a place where most of the men
have voted to quit, every encouragement
should be given the able-bodied youth who
aspires to take on plain sewing.
Municipal ownership of public utilities
is liable to begin well, at any rate. In the
six months it has been run-
ning, the Melbourne-Burwood
tram has earned £22,095.
Working expenses came to
£16,616, and £2474 was appro-
priated for renewals, main-
tenance and sinking fund,
leaving £3005 to be divided
among the four proprietary
councils, Richmond getting
£1053, Hawthorn £9lB, Mel-
bourne City £737 and Camber-
well £297.
The coal-miners did not go
on general strike till Novem-
ber ; consequently September
was not a strike month—at
least, no more of a strike
month than usual. Yet, dur-
ing that month, 6506 N. S.
Wales miners were out at one
time or another and the total
lost days were 34,145. or, say,
5| days per man. And that
was a mere shower in the
fall of tools. Altogether, in
this one State, 14,173 workers
took 80,382 holidays and lost
something over £40,000 in pay. But
it doesn’t matter. Isn’t there a beautiful
war raging to make everybody jolly and fat?
Before it is too late, an emphatic protest
ought to be made against the proposal to
float Australian loans in N’York. Apart
from the general impropriety of adding to
our so-called public works debt at a time
when we are unavoidably piling up war debt,
to borrow from the U.S.A. will give that
nation a status inside Australia, with the
result that when our new really Protective
tariff comes before Parliament we shall
almost inevitably find Yankee dumpers busy
in the lobbies, using the Yankee loan as a
lever. There is also the point that, if we
get into debt to a foreign Power, we shall
have to risk the complications that have hit
China, where the foreigner put himself by
force into the Custom House.
The Scaddan Government commenced oper-
ations with a credit legacy of £13,299. and
the rest of the story of splodge, expressed
in terms of deficit, went this way:—
1912 .. .. £134,409
1913 .. .. 190,404
1914 .. .. 135,411
1915 . . . . 365,617
1916 .. .. 441,295
During the same period the public debt was
pushed up by nearly 15 millions and pay-
ments to the sinking-fund were made with
lOU’s. The net result is to send the new
Government hunting round for tuppences
and reduce the State to the condition of a
Domain dosser who has pawned his pants
for beer. Meanwhile, of course, we must be
on the alert against the Germans.
.The Victorian anti-Sosh farmer finds him-
self with a new appetite. He wants to try
another kind of Socialistic stimulant
and won’t be happy till he gets it.
Wheat-sacks to hold a mere three
bushels are on the verge of lOd. apiece;
wherefore the agriculturist has been
earnestly imploring Hagelthorn to go
the whole hog. import bags in bulk,
do the distributing, and thus “save the
farmers from extortion.” It is an
interesting spectacle, this of Anti-
Sosh pursuing the shy Socialistic ani-
mal across country, reaching out to
pat him with a gentle hand and roar-
ing : “Good tiger ! Good feller !” But
why can’t these farmers, be their own
tiger? There is nothing on earth to
stop them from forming co-operative
cos. and being their own importers or
manufacturers not only of bags but
of a dozen other things. Sosh has
not made such a success of things lately
that the farmers can be excused for waiting
for him to move.
In the last 10 years the deaths of little
Australians under 12 .months varied from
8163 to 10,963 per annum : and those figures
are larger than the death-roll of Australian
soldiers in this war. Yet they take no count
of deaths between one year and seven. That
half the awful loss is due to overcrowding,
bad housing, improper and insufficient feed
ing, and general ignorance is shown by the
fact that, whereas in the poor, crowded Mel-
bourne suburbs of Richmond, Fitzroy and
Port Melbourne the under-12-months death-
rate was 11.37 per 100 births, in compara-
tively spacious Kew, Malvern and Camber-
well it was only 5.39 per 100. The war
losses are bad enough, but they can’t be
helped; our baby losses would more than 5
recoup them if we could dissipate ignorance
and cut the claws of greed.
So far as The Bulletin is able to
diagnose his condition, Holman wants o pn
as the Amended Bill.

ishness of putting things in the wrong place

Of the N. S. Wales politicians
whom the P.L.L. has been declar-
ing bogus, several have been in
trouble with the organisation
before, and therefore probably have
hopes now. In ’94 J. B. Nicholson
refused to sign the solidarity
pledge and ran without endorse-
ment. Alf Edden also ran as an
Independent. In the same election
Hoyle opposed and was beaten by
McGowen. ’Arry was then a
Dibbs follower and the gate of the
Labor fold wasn’t opened for him until 10
years later. And Meagher only threw in
his lot with the party when he smelt some-
thing cooking.
Poor old .Cocky is going to have a dry
autumn. Last year the Wheat Pool ad-
vanced him 3s. a bushel (less 6d. exes.) for
a start. This year he is to get half a
crown; but the first payment, to be financed
by the Commonwealth Bank, will not run to
more than Is. 6d. ; and there won’t be much
left towards the store bill after he has met
his harvest exes, and his bag and fertiliser
bills. Yet there are still some farmers who
reckon they don’t want Protection and new
industries to consume wheat. They like
their market 10.000 miles away, with a few
hostile submarines in between.
“O. Blin” congratulates his friend :
Senator Ted Russell remains true to himself. Ten
or more years ago I knew him as a young and fervid
Socialist and a valiant in General Tom Mann’s army.
Later on, Teddy found that Sosh was productive of
voice-strain and ostracism, and started becoming
respectable. Wherefore as a prim Laborite he made
a vociferous attempt to reach the P’ran seat in the
Vic. Assembly. Subsequently, hoisted into the Senate,
he revealed more of the valuable quality of adapting
himself to the color of his environment. I felt he had
wronged himself when, later on, he promised to
enlist and resigned from the Hughes Cabinet as a
protest against the Prime Minister’s infamous
attempt to interfere with the Referendum ballot. It
almost seemed as if in his sacrifice he had over-
looked his guiding principle. But the absence of
his name from the recruiting lists and his acceptance
of a post from the man whom he had repudiated
convinces me that Teddy is still himself.
The Little Boy from Manly hopes that
some abler pen will take the matter up:—
Dear Bullet e bn, —That was a nasty one Lord
Northcliffe got off on the British Cabinet the other
day, when they wanted to give him the job of Food
Dictator. “Not me,” he says, “you can get someone
else for your Tucker Tribunal. I’ve been getting to
press on time and catching the trains for the past
20 years, and I can’t afford to join a push of 22
procrastinators who are always too late. My reputa-
tion wouldn’t stand it,” he says. “For me the
time was never yet unripe. Why wait and see?”
he says. “No great journalist ever does that. Be
punctual, and you will bang your belltoppers on
the hatrack of victory. Wait and see, and you will
be gathering fungus in the backyard of eternity.”
And his words found an echo in the sinking heart
of poor old King of Roumania. “Punctuality,” says
that monarch, trying to smooth out the dints in
his left flank, “is the politeness of princes. Won’t
someone tell Gasquith to remember Servia and
Montenegro, and Ireland, and Gallipoli, and Meso-
potamia, and put his watch on a bit ? Otherwise I
shall be done in. Can’t he reorganise his time-
table and catch the ’bus?” I am sorry for the
King of Rou. You would have thought he 'would
have been safe in backing the British Lion for a
place. But apparently the time is not yet ripe.
Westralia having had a glorious racket,
the new Government has decided to “fund”
the lOU, and let the kid finish paying for
father’s spree in 1946. Happy Jack!
Unhappy Jackson!
The advantage of making the People feel
an interest in their own undertakings was
never better illustrated than by the fate of
Private Enterprise in motor-’buses in Adel-
aide. The Tramways Trust is a municipal
affair, run by representatives from all of
Greater Adelaide, and liable to have its suc-
cess or failure reflected in the local rates.
So the idea was sensibly cultivated _ that
every ratepayer was black-legging on himself
if he patronised the new motor-’bus services.
This idea soon put the new jiggers out of
business in Adelaide, popular as they have
proved in other cities; and last week the co.
offered everything at auction. Incidentally,
it got no bids that were thought worth
accepting.
Anyhow, the Speck’s Labor party didn't
practise on John Earle for nothing. It has
now kicked out the local members of the
I.W.W.

THE LATEST COMMUNIQUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Devil squatted on his chair ;
His typist with the molten hair
Awaited with her pen in hand ;
An imp his sweating features fanned.
Dictated Nick: “Well, we have whirled
That muddled planet called the World.
And, though we welcome million souls,
We find that we are short of coals.
No-yessers they can stand and wait,
For they have time to meet their fate;
But, lock or strike, some sons of guns
Have-cooled the ovens for the Huns!”
N.S.W.
THE SNAKE.
THE LATE UNLAMENTED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Came as a stripling to the throne —•
The Hapsburg;
Misfortune marked him for her own —
Perhapsburg;
So that men call him, dead and gone,
Mishapsburg.
VICTORIA.

ROADSTER
Competent performance, unusual
petrol and tyre mileage, and
marked freedom from repair—
four effects flow out of one and
the same cause.
The cause is strength added to
lightness, and balance added to
both. Efficiency and economy

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The belated V.R.C. meeting concluded on
Saturday, when it put up another record in post-
ponements. Before the horses went out for the Steeple-
chase rain fell heavily, and as they fidgeted around
the starting place they experienced the wettest time
of their lives. The rain fell in blinding sheets, and
finally the field was called to shelter a while. Pre-
vious to this the dripping denizens of the flat, or
many of them, had jumped over the railing and
rushed for any shelter they could get. It was a
wild, lawless proceeding, but the rain washed their
guilt away. Later on the downpour slackened into
showers and the Steeple came to pass. It was run
"in quite a normal way. The veteran Booligal took
the lead at once in his old commanding' manner and
stayed out the three miles one furlong without falter-
ing. Seldom, a natty little ’chaser, paced it with
the big fellow until he fell, and then the race was
Booligal’s. Rendrock, who had spoilt his chance by
faulty jumping, did well to finish second, 20 lengths
behind the runaway.
The honors of the w.f.a. racing at Mel-
bourne carnival went to the infirm Lavendo. The
English horse was lucky, but he deserved his luck
for his consistency. Firstly he won the Caulfield
Stakes from Kilboy because the big, striding three-
year-old was not pushed along from the start. Then
he won the Melbourne Stakes luckily from Carlita,
and on Saturday he captured the Fisher Plate because
the breakdown of Wolaroi had left him only one
dangerous opponent. Lavendo was a bit lame and
so low-spirited that the stable declined to back the
invalid. Carlita also had gone off color, so that the
books, although they wanted odds on the mare,
would not lay more than 4 to 1 against Easteourt,
the other starter. The three made a good race of it,
and Lavendo beat the mare with a brilliant effort
in 2min. 35isec. What a good thing he was for
last year’s Caulfield Cup, when he carried little
more than seven stone!
There were only two unimported nags among
the six starters for the V.R.C. Handicap, and
one of them—Juan Fernandez—would evidently have
proved the winner in a fast-run race. As it was.
Aides lasted just long enough to beat him by half
a head. The Final Handicap, run on a sodden course,
was remarkable for the fact that the books, to a
man, were most persistent in laying 4 to 1 against
Rael Locin, the useful gee that had been widely
whispered of as a good thing. He didn’t look like
a moral cert, on paper, and the books, with their
eternal 4 to 1, gave the correct tip. Allegorv won
from Maltravers, which for certain reasons made the
booming of Rael Locin seem easily accounted for.
* * * *
The imported Aides, which annexed the
V.R.C. Handicap, a mile and three-quarters, at
Flemington on Saturday, isn’t much chop, and the
fact emphasises the contention frequently made on
this page that either the local nag has deteriorated
quite a lot or the aggregate Australian trainer is a
horse-murderer on a large scale. Aides ran up close
l n . a couple of minor events a few months back, and
nuked a race not long before the Melbourne Spring
carnival started out on its jolting journey. Where-
upon a misguided public, worried to death by the
constant victories of imported animals, didn’t” even
wait to scratch its head, but promptly stepped in
and backed Aides to win the Melbourne Cup. Any-
thing the immigrant did in that race was not the
reason for making him favorite for the V.R.C. Han-
dicap. Aides has now left the Wheeler stable, which
has had the key to so many rich prizes during the
last 18 months.
* » * *
Sasanof had, to all appearances, begun to
stale off within a week of his winning the Melbourne
Cup. , Quinologist is supposed to have “gone to
pieces ’ during the four days that intervened between
the Tuesday when the Cup race ought to have hap-
pened and the Saturday when it eventuated. The
ancient adage that the race is to the swift has a
very limited application in these cases. In a first-
class handicap held of thoroughbreds there are sev-
eral with the necessary swiftness to win at the
weights. The race is to the fittest on the day, and
the horses with the best tempers and soundest con-
stitutions are the most likely to be the most fit
when fitness is most specially required of them. Con-
sequently a wise tipster accepts latest form as the
only reliable guide in choosing between recent per-
formers. The books regulate their odds by the same
rule in a great measure. If an alleged “good thing”
that has been heavily backed for a particular long-
distance event shows only moderate form in w.f.a.
races preceding the event, the reasonable assumption
is that the animal is not swift enough, or not fit
enough. _ The assumption that the “good thing’’ was
not trying to win a substantial stake (when vic-
tory would not have entailed a penaltv) is merelv
the delusion of fly-flat punters, as a ' rule. The
books, who are the most unbiased of tipsters, always
believe in what they have recently seen, rather than
what is rumored. If a horse makes only a moderate
show in valuable w.f.a. races on two successive
Saturdays they see no reason why it should be ex-
pected to run brilliantly a few days later.
* * * *
Stageland followed up his respectable per-
formance in the Melbourne Cup by winning the
Bendigo Cup last week. The victory was nothing
to skite about, but it fully justified the stable con-
tention that the colt is much better than lie looks.
He is one of the light and narrow sort, and seem-
ingly he can only do his best when the going is
easy. Others of Comedy King’s progeny show the
same want of breadth, 3 r et they have good hearts in
their poor little chests.
It is not often the crowd is ready to lay
. 5 to 4 on in a field of 21, but the incident happened
at Canterbury Park (Sydney) last week. The bet
came off, too. It was in the Nursery Handicap, and
the rush of cash for Conacre, a son of Linacre, would
have kept the war going for quite five minutes.
Conacre is a smart-looking customer and may have
been worth the 400 guineas he cost as a baby.
L. K. S. MACKINNON, Chairman of the
V.R.C.
Now that Sir Bobadil is back to form it
behoves punters to keep a level head when he again
goes into action. Sir Bobadil raced faster than
anything else in Canterbury Park (Sydney) Flving
Handicap on Saturday, and landed home a length
ahead of the next nag. He is a compact little fel-
low, now five years old, and has had his full share
of success. He won at Randwick when a young-
ster, but in his old age prefers the smaller courses.
Aboard Sir Bobby on Saturday was Foley, the
mannikin who was on top of Sasanof in Melbourne
Cup.
* * * *
, So far as two-year-old form has been ex-
posed this season no youngster stands out from any
other. Sam Hordern has a good ’un in Pleasant
Moments, and Bronzetti, another Bronzino, mav
develop into a Derby possibility. Both these
juveniles belong to the Ma State and have proved
sound. Pleasant Moments won two events at the
much-delayed V.R.C. meeting, and Bronzetti cap-
tured Flemington Stakes on Saturday. Any nag
which wins down the straight at Flemington is
usually worth considering in a longer-distanee
event; for here it has to carry the weight all the
way and go straight without a guiding rail. N. S.
Wales has two other performers in Pah King, which
appropriated the Maribyrnong Plate, and Baltic Sea,
that may be doing things a year hence —if all goes
well. But so many things go wrong.
* * * *
A few days before the Caulfield Cup there
appeared in a Tasmanian paper the account of
a local wedding, the offenders being named Shepard
and King-. Result—a stampede of local tip fiends
for Ikey, who is now nursing a badly wounded neck
and heaping benedictions on the ’appy couple. ‘ I
don’t suppose they had a bob on it themselves either,
he wails. Perhaps not. They were having a bigger
gamble.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: In the 55 races for the Mel-
bourne Cup, not including the last, the favorite has
won only 11 times. It ran second eight times;
third, four times; fourth, six times; fifth, once;
sixth, thrice ; seventh, thrice; and eighth, twice. It
has also run absolutely last. That was the day I
walked home without a shirt.
* * * *
“Edo”: Coolgardie the other day parted
with its last shred of former glory by winding up
its racing club. The old town has long and keenly
suffered the pangs of poverty, but it stuck to its two
yearly turf meetings through all its misfortunes. Of
course, “the war” is blamed for the disaster, but the
truth is that the bottom has gone out of the onee-
famous place and great is the slump thereof.
“Mujik” : On a recent Saturday 26 races
out of 30 among good-class horses in Australia were
won by animals either imp. or by imp. sires. On
Saturday at Flernington every flat-race winner was
one or the other. And so it goes on with increasing
frequency. Yet there are any number of people
who say it all points to nothing or is a flash in
the pan or a rumor or something. Of course it
means something definite; but, like everything else
that matters just now in this cheerful country, it
will probably be let go hang. Some day a buyer
will get a brain wave and rush off to the Cold
Land looking for hurdlers and ’chasers, and when
he does it is 100 to 1 that the local article will
get snuffed out some more. In that type of thorough-
bred Australia is horribly deficient —yet it is one
that should be reared here with great success.
The law of compensations still holds good.
During the recent race week at Christchurch (M.L.)
Hugo Friedlander and G. D. Greenwood each saw
one of their horses break down in the second race
on the first day. Next day Friedlander won the
N.Z. Cup and Greenwood the Stewards’ Handicap.
J. B. Thompson had a £27,000 warehouse burnt
down. He won the New Zealand Trotting Cup next
day. That afternoon A. W. Rutherford lost the
brightest in his stable—Encore. The following day
the stable went for a big win on Furling and landed
the bacon.
* * * »
“S.T.S.”: As to the novel, “Wolaroi’s
Cup,” referred to in B. 9/11/’l6. Some of us, hav-
ing lost money on the beast on the strength of such
an obvious tip, want to bring an action for False
Expectations. But counsel says it would be a good
defence that Wolaroi may win a Cup some other
year. So evidently we have to wait till the beast is
dead.
Syd Godfrey (N.S.W.) and Patsy Bran-
nigan argued the matter again at Sydney Stadium
last week. On the first occasion the Yank got a
points verdict. This time he got a hiding and was
so used up by the seventeenth collision that Arthur
Scott stopped the proceedings. Patsy showed any
amount of courage, hut Godfrey’s right was a fre-
quent and purposeful visitor, and every time it
landed it hurt. After the affray Brannigan expressed
the opinion that he had never received such uncalled-
for treatment in the course of 400 fights. Young
Godfrey is a very promising lad. He looks good
enough to put a question or two to Jimmy Hill
about the feather-weight title.
* * * *
Bill Squires started his come-back cam-
paign at Melbourne on Saturday and collected severe
punishment. The big American Tom McMahon pre-
sented William with a varied collection of swipes,
rips, bashes and biffs as a warning against remain-
ing on the premises, and after William had dropped
with a loud bang on the floor three times Thomas
was not called upon for any more. The sixth round
had not commenced.
Tommy TJren and Harry Stone served
bash a la carte at Brisbane on Saturday night. The
ragtime pug gave LTren 61b. and much to think about.
Uren’s earnest endeavors mostly ended in a search
for the other fellow’s address. From the eighth clash
his right came more into prominence, and the last
five rounds were busy times for America. The
referee took a strictly neutral view of the situation
at the finish, and incurred the usual displeasure.
A Sydney journalist who specialises in
bash was handed a solid bill by a P.M. the other
day— £25 and costs. It all arose out of a letter
written to Les Darcy and handed to a passenger by
an American steamer for delivery. You mustn’t
compete with the post office like that. There was
nothing in the letter of any importance except one
sentence —“Your name here now is Putty.” That
puts it about right, and makes it a matter for some
regret that the letter did not get through. Someone
equally frank ought to spend 2d. on stamps and
give the young man the news. It would do him a
lot of good.
* * * *
“Eddy”: I am told that even the axeman’s
game as a popular sport is no longer as clean as it
might be. The bookie has butted in, and choppers
who were always square are now sometimes squared.
It’s a ghastly shame, for this sport, like all that spring
from the daily toil of competitors, was of the great-
est value and interest. Never have I met such
enthusiasts as I encountered among the keen blades
in Vic. and Tasmanian big timber. The men
exulted in their prowess with the passionate earnest-
ness of the true craftsman. A few years ago I
spent three days in a sawmillers’ camp in Gippsland.
There were four first-class axemen in the party, and
the pride they took in their ability to sweep down
a forest giant was as good as a little gold-mine to
the boss. One lad whom his mates called the Ringer
ate into the bush like a duck into a tub of butter.
I watched him for a whole afternoon, fascinated by
liis skill and almost miraculous industry. He was
perhaps 26, sft. Bin., lithe as a whip-lash, and as
lean. I went up to his hut with him after 5 o’clock,
and noticed a couple of sawn logs standing near.
“What are they for?” I asked. “The logs?” he
said. “Oh, a bloke might feel like lashin’ out a bit
after tea. Them’s for practice.”
* * * l *
It looks like being a nervous season for
the Sydnev surfer. Big sharks have been seen and
caught inshore at all the principal surfing beaches,
and though the prowling brute is often as amiable
as a policeman, there’s never any telling when his
mood will change. Apropos of sharks’ appetites,
one of the Williamson brothers who took the sub-
marine movies in the clear waters of Bermuda, found
that the shark was not the eager scavenger he is
supposed to be. After trying much “high” bait, the
Williamsons hung a slaughtered calf before their
submarine cabin window. Nothing happened for
half an hour. Then a 30-foot horror swam up,
studied the meal carefully from all angles, and
went away for his partner. The pair made a joint
inspection and then called up six friends. The
private view went on for six interminable hours
until there were 30 swimming round. There
wasn’t a sign of excitement until one shark in
passing risked a bite. Instantly the whole school
went mad, snapping not only at the bait, but at
each other and scraps of inedible floating debris.
Cowardice and gluttony late for dinner, and very
mad about it.
* * * *
“Groper”: Armchair sportsmen complain
that Westralia is a poor place to look for food for
gun and fishing line. They libel the country. With
a pal I had the time of my life recently on the
Blackwood River, within a few miles of the Leeuwin.
Grey duck, mountain duck, wood duck, teal, divers,
parrots and fish of great variety, including schnap-
per, bream, sea-mullet, whiting and salmon-trout
abound. Curried marran (the mammoth gilgie)
helped the gorge along. And kangaroo and wallaby
provided changes of diet. The climate of the dis-
trict, like the other well-favored south-western por-
tions of this Band’s End of the Commonwealth, has
the habit of making the oldest inhabitants feel like
mere striplings.
Fairly hooked on a light line, a big shark
seems to be worth anything from 60 to 90 minutes’
brisk play to an experienced angler. Sydney fisher-
men lately have been putting up some great -beach
fights with blue pointers and grey nurses of from Bft.
to 12ft. One brute ran out 150yds. of light thread
line and gave the expert with the rod a run of a
mile along Narrabeen Beach. Tackle used by one of
these big-game hunters is a tarpon rod and 300yds.
of American cutty-hunk line with a piano-wire trace
and three small brass swivels. Mullet bait. It
looks as light as cobweb, but it holds like a mort-
gage. If you dared to suggest more massive tackle
to these sports they’d stun you.
* * * *
Rowing breeds sound sports at Warrnam-
bool (Vie.). Of 59 members of the local rowing
club who have offered themselves for active service
only two have been turned down.
* * * *
Jolted by the increase in the number of
motor-car accidents, the Law in N. S. Wales has
decided to put the brake on hard. Where notices
are posted to that effect, speed is to be restricted to
four miles an hour ; and in the absence of a notice,
six miles is to be the limit when passing the entrance
to ferry wharves, schools during dismissal hours,
and upon any congested street. If the new regula-
tions are strictly enforced there is going to be much
angry grunting amongst the motor-hogs. Some of
them will probably sell their flying bedsteads in dis-
gust and rejoin the walking classes.
While Victorian motor-cyclist H. A.
Parsons was going great guns last week in the
attempt to fracture Yankee Erwin Baker’s 24-hour
record of 1027 miles, a mob of sheep upended him
into the dust and wrecked the hardware. Parsons
had stirred the dust up over 305 miles in 387 min.
at the time of the calamity, and was only warming
up for the journey.
* * * *
The Dunlop Rubber Go. got a good ad.
out of the carnival of motor-cars at Flemington on
Melbourne Cup Day. In the reserve for vehicles 728
cars were counted, and neaily half of them were
Dunlop-tyred. The foreigners that came second and
third scored between 400 arid 500 each, as against
1589 Dunlops. The Australian make won in a
canter. . There were 24 competitors.
* * * *
It has been decided to stage no senior
cricket in Hobart this season, but one ground—that
at Newtown—is being kept in some sort of trim,
and on it schoolboy matches run their course.
W. Hughes (North Perth) put up a rare
performance at the opening of Westralian premier-
ship cricket by taking nine wickets for nine runs
out of a total of 78 made by Claremont. Hughes
is fast, and is said to be the greatest left-hander
Groperland has yet known.
* * * *
Frank E. Allan, “the bowler of a century,”
celebrated the passing of 50 years since he donned
flannels for Albert Park in Victorian cricket by
umpiring the recent South Melbourne v. St. Ivilda
fixture. Jack Blackham and Allan are the only
Victorian survivors of the ’7B team; but of the
whole bunch eight are still going strong. Among
those who are out are Harry Boyle, W. Midwinter,
Billy Murdoch and Tommy Horan.
* * * *
Christian Brothers’ College has a fine
schools’ athletic record in Westralia. It has been
undefeated at premiership football for 10 years;
at rowing, this season’s win made the seventh in 16
years; and at the athletic championship meeting
recently the Collegians’ success made the ninth
offence in an unbroken run.
STUMPS DRAWN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where are all the flannelled clan
On this perfect playing day—
Spofforth, Murdoch, Bannerman,
Each a master in his way ?
Where are those that cheered them on
From the packed pavilion?
Shod in white with linen hat,
How they held our hearts in thrall—
Massey with his shining bat,
Turner with his cunning ball!
Hark again the shouts of old—
“ Bravo! Middle stump! Well bowled!”
Where is now that human hive
That rent Heaven with cheers to see
Bonnor’s most Homeric drive,
When he lifted mightily
High and still more high the ball
Over clock and tower and all ?
Many years have passed since then,
Since that most amazing sight;
Boys, who saw it, grown to men
Still recall it, pipes alight,
As companioned well they walk
Lost in old-time' cricket talk.
Ah, the joy, the thrill intense,
Watching from the crowded hill,
“Fourers” to the picket fence,
“Fivers” over! Ah, the chill
Or the loud, triumphant shout
When the umpire nodded —Out!
State and Test—what joys they were
When the game was in its prime!
How the blood in us would stir
Into ecstasy what time
Cutting, driving, hard and sweet,
Some bold batsman saved defeat!
How the noise roared round the town,
Making good the passing hours,
When the Vies, went tumbling down,
And we knew the match was ours!
How we drooped downcast, ashamed,
When the Vies, the victory claimed!
Where are they, the blithe, the bold,
Whom it was our joy to watch ?
Some are grey and all are old,
Some have played their final match—■
Shouldered bats and gravely gone
To the packed Pavilion.
\
Cone, too, are the watching throng;
Now no more their plaudits rise.
All is silent save the song
Of a lone lark in the skies ;
And—the sole life of the scene—
Swallows skim across the green.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.

Dr. W. J. Penfold, the first chief of the
Federal Serum Institute (Melb.), which is
going to make anti-toxins and vaccines and
thus set another useful Australian industry
afoot, is an Edinburgh graduate, a Bachelor
of Hygiene of the Durham University, and
put in eight years at the Lister Institute,
London. He was assistant bacteriologist
at the Institute when the gong sounded, and
he got the job of making typhoid vaccine for
T. Atkins at the R.A.M. College. After-
wards he helped to enfilade a spinal menin-
gitis epidemic at Salisbury Plains, and was
bombing the elusive microbe at King
George’s Military College (Lond.) when the
Commonwealth signalled him. He is 41.

From “Rosna” in London:—
Adelaide’s Parliamentary warrior, Lieut. W. J.
Denny, who is with the Australian Artillery, dashed
through London on leave the other day, looking
thoroughly fit. As far as I remember he was going
to “do” Shakspeare’s England and Dickens’s London
and get back for an appointment with the Hun the
same evening.
Artist Dora Meeson Coates, policewoman, quelled
a riot the other day. A huge soldier was fighting
a good-sized crowd and couldn’t be quelled until
the Australian woman came along and said a sooth-
ing word. Then he went with her to the lock-up
quite meekly and accepted 36 days with joy.
The Australian Red Cross has now taken our
Anzac prisoners of war in Germany to its motherly
heart, and is doing its bit to circumvent starva-
tion, freezing and similar German pleasantries. It
sends each man a good outfit of warm clothes, with
a weekly parcel of bread and cheese from Switzer-
land and a five-shilling lot of tinned edibles from
London. The uniform prescribed by Hun orders is
navy with violent yellow trimmings. If these are
not sewn on so that neither man nor beast could
ever get them off again, the whole show is confis- •
cated by the Kaiser’s minions.
“A.M.D.”: A word with “G.D.M.” (B.
16/11/’l6) regarding his one-eyed view of
the Y.M.C.A.. incidental to his attack on
wowserism. He bunched the association
with others as “a collective pestilential
nuisance, and as my inferiors as men and
women.” What arrogance is this! I
write as one who walked out of a comfort-
able, cheerful, anti-killjoy home in the month
of August. 1914, and changed into khaki
from the glad rags of ordinary society; ex-
changed a cosy bed for the hard ground, with
a kitbag for a pillow, and mysterious stew
and bread and jam for the comfortable table
of home-life; and after a voyage in a trans-
port helped for a year and nine months to
keep in safety “G.D.M.” and his ilk, who
all the time were able to comfortably drink
their “pint of claret with their dinner”
and practise “love of clubs and clubmen” to
their full. I write also as a clubman of
years’ standing, and I say to “G.D.M.” that
it is time he knew that no organisation on
active service, whether on the training-ground
or in the battlefield, is so useful to Billjim as
the Y.M.C.A. Its work is beyond praise;
and The Bulletin has itself borne testi-
mony thereto. It knows no sect, it makes
no attempt to thrust any doctrine down the
throat of any individual. It treats in
strictly impartial fashion every man in
khaki, be he general or private. Tens of
thousands pf Australian soldiers have writ-
ten home on Y.M.C.A. notepaper, with
Y.M.C.A. pens, at absolutely no cost to the
individual. This is but one item of its use-
fulness. The whole army could tell of the
clean and wholesome food supplied, very
often at less than cost; of entertainments
running from high-class music to rough-and-
tumble boxing bouts; informative lectures
by authorities on subjects immediately
affecting the life of Billjim; literature, from
the local daily of some wayback town to
The Bulletin ; libraries covering science,
art, militarism, fiction and everything that
adorns the old shelves at home. The
Y.M.C.A. huts are actually in the trenches
on the blood-soaked fields of France. Wow-
sers. are they? Killjoys, are they? If so,
“G.D.M.” will have to class every grateful
Australian soldier in the same category.
“Eddyson” on one of the traditions of Yam :
When Mrs. Pocoek died, leaving a lumbering husband
and two large, lumbering sons, aged 30, and known
in the district as “The Twin,” old Pocock prevailed
upon Mrs. Winnie from Seven Bells to become house-
keeper for the family at Wandong. Wandong was
a large farm with cows, including pigs, and an
orchard, likewise poultry. Mrs. Winnie received one
pound a week and keep, and she earned it. Her
duties were not defined —while there was something
to do she was expected to -do it, and there were
always at least two things waiting to be done. Mrs.
Winnie said she was 26, and she didn’t look a day
over 30, a wholesome, brown, hard-fleshed, amiable
dame. The Twin promptly fell in love with Mrs.
Winnie. The Twin had a bewildering habit of
doing things suddenly and simultaneously. Sam
and Dan invaded Mrs. Winnie’s kitchen and pro-
posed to Mrs. Winnie at the same moment, in the
same words. Sam and Dan were so alike that Mrs.
Winnie was to be excused for her inability to choose
between them. “Go ’way, man,” she said, “and
don’t make a fool of yourself.” The Twin was fre-
quently addressed as one. One or two, it was a most
persistent suitor till old Pocock butted in with
3ft. of red gum, and promised 10 days in bed to
the next man found interfering with his housekeeper.
There was comparative calm for six months, when
old Pocock died. On the way back from the inter-
ment the Twin proposed to Mrs. Winnie. Mrs. Winnie
temporised. Her inability to make up her mind
led to the first serious difference between Sam and
Dan. They had never been known to disagree over
anything before, but they disagreed most emphati-
cally over Mrs. Winnie, and the sight of them tumb-
ling but of the kitchen and fighting it out in the
back yard was so frequent that it ceased to interest
everybody but Andrew Carl. Carl was employed
at Pearce’s sawmill in the next paddock, and would
always take a few minutes off, if possible, to sit
round and watch the Twin knocking the hide off
each other. “Have it out, man,” he would say.
He had seen them at it a dozen times, and there
was no change in his demeanor —lie was always
desirous of more. Meanwhile Mrs. Winnie was ap-
parently wholly unconcerned. “Glad to have the
boy out of the house,” she explained. “When he’s
fighting himself he isn’t worrying me.” If it had
not been for Carl the Twin would have had no
encouragement at all. His presence often caused
what might have been mere scrims to develop into
pitched battles. The final encounter occurred on a
Sunday afternoon. Because they had nothing to do,
and as Carl was making it a very willing go, half
a dozen mill hands were assembled on the wood-
heap, watching the contest. After the tenth round
the Twin tired. Carl got them going again. In
spite of their weariness and their damages he got
two more rounds out of them. He hustled for a
third. “Better settle it,” he said. The Twin
shook their heads. “Oh-h, go on!” snorted Carl.
“Be a man. Have it out.” They declined. “Well,
look here,” said Carl, “you’ve simply got to eome
to a decision, because after this I can’t allow no
man to fight for my wife!” Proofs were forthcom-
ing. Mrs. Winnie was really Mrs. Winnie Carl.
She and Carl had been “separated,” and Carl had
followed her to Yam in the hope of effecting a
reconciliation. “But why didn’t you tell me?” wailed
the Twin in unison. Mrs. Winnie’s explanation was
satisfactory. “Well, you see.” she said, “I thought
it would do Carl good to let him see there was
another boy wanting me.”

BLACKHEADS, oily skins and enlarged
pores usually go together, but can be
instantly corrected by a unique new process.
A tablet of stymol, obtained from the chem-
ist’s is dropped in a tumbler of hot water,
which will then of course “fizz” briskly.
Secrets of Beauty
The Cream of Modern Beauty Advice
By “MIMOSA.”
When the effervescence has subsided the face
is bathed with the stymol-charged water and
then dried with a towel. The offending
blackheads, of their own accord, come right
off on the towel, the large oily pores imme-

quickly obtained.
BEAUTIFUL hair adds immensely to
the personal magnetism of both men
and women. Actresses and smart women
are ever on the look-out for any harmless
thing that will increase the natural beauty
of their hair. The latest method is to use
pure stallax as a shampoo on account of
the peculiarly glossy, fluffy and wavy effect
which it leaves. As stallax has never been
used much for this purpose it comes to the
chemist only in sealed original packages,
enough for twenty-five or thirty shampoos.
A teaspoonful of the fragrant stallax gran-
ules, dissolved in a cup of hot water, is more
than sufficient for each shampoo. It is very
beneficial and stimulating to the hair, apart
from its beautifying effect.
*** * *
IT is astonishing the number of women
who suffer from the humiliating afflic-
tion of superfluous hair, and I am sorry to
say that through ignorance or wrong treat-
ment the majority of them have made matters
considerably worse by causing the hairs to
grow more quickly and stronger than before.
It only requires a little thought to realise
the folly of it all. Removing the surface
growth for a few days may give temporary
relief, but in order to obtain permanent
results the hair roots must be killed. Caustic
depilatories are worse than useless, and on
no account should the hairs be pulled out.
Apart from the electric needle, which is a
costly and painful process, any chemist will
tell you that the best home treatment is
pure powdered pheminol, w r hich has a de-
vitalising effect upon the. hair roots, in addi-
tion to which it removes all trace of the
objectionable hair in a few seconds- and the
recommended treatment gradually, but
surely, dissolves out the hair follicles, which
is the only method of permanently ridding
oneself of this terrible disfigurement. [6

HALF-YEARLY REPORT of the Directors of THE BANK OF AUSTRALASIA
„ With the Accounts for the Half-year to 10th April, 1916,
Presented to the Proprietors at the Half-yearly General Meeting, held on Thursday, sth October, 1916.
REPORT.
The Directors submit to the Proprietors the Balance-sheet as at 10th April last, with the Profit
Account for the half-year to that date. After providing for rebate on bills current, for British and Aus-
tralasian rates and taxes, and after making provision for bad and doubtful debts and other contingencies,
the net profit for the half-year amounted to £206,662 14s. 2d.
Out of this the Directors have declared a Dividend for the half-year at the rate of 14 per cent, per
annum, or £2 16s. per share, and a Bonus at the rate of 3 per cent, per annum, or 12s. per share, to-
gether £3 Bs. per share, free of income-tax. This will absorb £170,000. The Directors have further
apropnated £30,000 to the Reserve Fund, leaving a balance of profit for the half-year of £6662 14s. 2d.
To this sum must be added £115,096 Bs. 4d., brought forward from the previous half-year, making a total
of £121,759 2s. 6d. to be carried forward to the next account.
Ju 6 and Bonus will be payable, in London and in Australasia, on the 6th October.
The Directors record, with deep regret, the death of their colleague, Viscount Quenington, M.P.,
Adjutant Royal Gloucestershire Hussars, who was killed in action in April last.
„r, T ° I? 11 the vacancy thus created, the Directors have appointed The Rt. Hon. Viscount Midleton,
K. 1., whose name will be submitted for the approval of the Proprietors at the Annual General Meeting
in 1917, in accordance with the provisions of the Deed of Settlement.
Since the date of the last Report, the Branch at Beechworth, Victoria, has been closed.
, , F. V. O. LIVINGSTONE-LEARMONTH, Chairman.
4 Threadneedle Street, London, 25th September, 1916.

We have examined the cash and securities in London, and the London books, and have verified the
transfers (from the several branches in the Commonwealth and New Zealand, and we beg to report
that, in our opinion, the foregoing is a full and fair balance-sheet of the bank, and that it exhibits
a true and correct view of the state of the bank’s affairs as shown by the books. The investments
were valued, so far as could be ascertained, at market price or under.

ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

has for years advocated conscription, so that the
ranks might be filled with the least possible dis-
turbance of industry and commerce. It applied the
other day for the exemption of one man because
(1) three-fourths of its male art contributors, both
married and unmarried, are already at the Front or
on the way, (2) because the work of that one man
couldn’t be done by the few others available, and
(3) because it was desirable that the wbrk should
be done, and that is proved best by the fact that
the recruiting and eonscriptionist bodies of at least
four States applied for permission to make use of
the work he was doing.

Confronted by this year’s one solitary
Agricultural student, Dr. Cherry, hoe-and-
furrow professor at Melbourne Uni., decided
to go and help the A.M.C. mend the boys
who are away lead-spraying Hun-infested
Europe. Another professor (Osborne, of the
Medical School) hopes to leave soon for the
battlefields via Frisco.
Rev. Frank Isitt, the waterwaggoner, con-
tested more electorates than any other
Maorilander. In electorates where only
one candidate offered, Isitt, in order to have
a poll taken and so give the anti-grog crowd
a local-option vote, would butt in with his
nomination. Sometimes he contested eight
or nine seats simultaneously. If a couple
of electorates had chosen him by any chance
there would have been trouble. But he
never found a place. The Isitts are full of
fight. Leonard was even a more prominent
protagonist than Frank; and Willard, Leo-
nard’s second son, battled in the trenches
and had to battle to reach them. He was
turned down because of a faulty eye, but,
determined to do his bit, got into a boxing
match and persuaded his opponent to wallop
the blockading organ and black it. Next
day with his optic in hospital he dropped
into a recruiting depot and beat the
examiners.
A horrible churn of wheels, a groan and a bang,
And a great “tank” moved out which gave Fritz a
pang!
Over a broken gun, a trench and a fence,
Straight towards the Huns, and hence
A terrible slaughter;
It was a mortar.
Yet still the Huns fought her,
Although they’d sooner bought her!
It stood and swept the German trenches clear,
Both right and left the Huns dropped in fear
With a lead bullet
Where sometimes a pullet
Meets the axe on a summer’s day—
That’s the way the “tanks” enter the fray,
And Tommy Atkins earns his pay,
In a distant land far away!

SAFETY
*][ Enjoyable touring and efficient commercial transport
are alike impossible without the sense of security imparted
by good tyres. The toughness of Avon rubber and the
strength of Avon casing reduce to a minimum the risk of
punctures or bursts, and the sure grip of the “ Sunstone '*
ladder-grooved tread makes you as skid-proof as it is
possible to be —whatever the weathei, whatever the road.
COMFORT
Superior resilience alone can reveal the maximum com-
fort of motoring on pneumatic tyres. Many a car owes
its reputation for luxurious springing and silky running to
the buoyant liveliness of its Avon Tyres.
ECONOMY
<}[ The same qualities of strength and resilience that
achieve safety and comfort necessarily cut down ultimate
costs —of petrol, repairs to chassis and tyres as well.
Avon economy is the economy of endurance —the typically
British sort of tyre beauty that is more than tread-deep.
The claim that Avon Tyres show lowest cost per mile rests
upon disinterested records of average mileage in everyday
service, open to investigation and capable of demon-
stration on your vehicle.

IN
BANANA LAND
A great silence, lasting nearly 30 years,
was broken in Brisbane the other day- when
Jimmy Cowlishaw, chairman of the South
Brisbane Gas Co., spluttered a few remarks
in the House of Fossils to protest against
an invasion of the rights of Private Enter-
prise.
“F. 8.”: Queensland Government Analyst
J. B. Henderson finds that Bananaland
wastes 10 million gallons of molasses every
year, which means 11 million gallons of
spirit. I have seen the fish in a Queensland
river come to the surface drunk and helpless
opposite the molasses vent that opened into
the stream opposite a big sugar mill. You
could pick up the boozers and lift them into
your boat.
“L.S.” : Minister for Lands Hunter, w T ho
is regarded as the business man of the Gov-
erpment, looks a certainty for the job of
Agent-General. Thomas Robinson, who is
now. in the chair in London, has no claim on
the job after January next; but the Govern-
ment intends to tell him that he can go on
for a month or two. Then Hunter will
arrive. Other Ministerial changes are likely
to take place about the same time, and it
is possible that Premier Ryan will take a
judgeship. Hunter and Ryan are insepar-
able in the political world, and when one
goes out the other will probably follow.
Theodore has his eye on the Premiership,
but it is doubtful if he will get it, as his
sure following in the caucus of 45 members
is only about 14.
The Auditor-General’s report makes sad
reading. There was a loss on State rail-
ways for 1915-16 of £610,536, owing to “re-
duced. hours and increased wages.” The
munitions experiment, a horrible fiasco, cost
£lO,OOO. The State sawmill shows a loss
for nine months of £1380; the State coal-
mine at Warra a loss for nine months of
£l6Bl. Still £335,461 has been now spent on
four cattle-stations, and the profit off those
is of course going to paint the
whole picture pink. How they are going to
do it and also provide cheap meat is another
matter.
A Northern Billzac suddenly recovered his
speech the other day, after being unable to
say a word for over six months. Wounds in
the head received at Gallipoli did the dam-
age, and all the efforts of the doctors were
in vain. Returning from Sydney by train
after consulting a specialist without success,
he heard a girl screech that there was
a nia > under the wheels; but the biggest
shock .me received was when she heard Bill-
zac’s sympathy in a hoarse, strained Amice,
“Poor devil!” He was the cheeriest Billzac
in Australia for the remainder of the trip.
As always happens, the removal of the
cheap nigger from the scene has been fol-
lowed in the sugar industry by no end of
mechanical improvements. Until a very
few years ago all that electricity did for the
business was to supply a little lighting. Now
the pumps for the big water supply neces-
sary are electrically driven, Avhile press-tlie-
button capstans haul the cane trucks between
the carrier siding and the cane rakes. There
is a cane grab at the Racecourse Mill.
Mackay. which travels along a revolving
yard arm, supported by a lofty steel frame
mast, and picks up the cane from the ground
within a half-acre area. So perfect is its
mechanism that it can lift a single stick of
cane or a pile three tons in weight and
deposit the load on to the carrier feeding
the rollers. Previously this work was done
by 20 men, and, oiving to the job being the
most irksome in the mill, Monday mornings
found many of the hands still down and out
at the neighboring pub and the plant held
up through insufficient cane going to the
rollers. The grab cost £5OOO and it is
reckoned that it saves about £2OOO a year.

FROM THE FOUNTAIN HEAD.
FATHER WILLIAM (reproaching his offspring): “You ain’t got no sense at all. You ain't
nothing but a good fer nothin’ higherant fool. I’ve learned yer every blessed thing
I know, an’ yer know absolutely nothing.”
duced on country in the WesJ which at one
time was a huge cattle-walk, and on all sides
declared fit for no other purpose, just as
practically similar country in the Territory
is so declared to-day by people who no doubt
have personal and particular reasons for
giving false evidence. The greasy wool men-
tioned came off Evora station, situated
within the edge of what used to be reck-
lessly described on the map as the Great
Australian Desert. It now carries millions
of sheep, besides cattle and horses. The day
will come when the so-called desert round the
foot of the McDonnell Ranges, Central Aus-
tralia, will be suffering from the same com-
plaint.

(14/11/’l6) :—•

The police and the fire-brigade are trying to
stop the rush.
“Norbell”: One more use for the kerosene
tin. A section sliced lengthways from each
side of it provides you with two shal-
low trays for holding letters, kills, etc.
These trays are used in all the A.l.r.
orderly rooms and offices in Bananaland, and
serve their purpose quite as well as the
expensive wicker or wire contrivances which
you’ll find in the cheapest city office. Ike
military heads are tigers for economy. They
have even been known to spend £417 6s. 9td.
on an inquiry touching an alleged overcharge
of 7d. on a bag of chaff.
The honor of introducing the porcupine
curse to the Taroom district belongs to one
Hook, who proudly brought the first seed-
lings to Roehedale station, somewhere about
the year when Queensland was sliced from
N. S. Wales. Hook had been across the
border, and carried tiie precious seedlings in
his .saddle-pouch a distance of 400 miles,
afterwards carefully planting them in a
choice spot in the station garden. The
beautiful pieces of horticulture now bloom
all over millions of acres of the local land-
scape,
“Barcoo”: Up North the most generally
useful animal (bar woman, perhaps) is
the common or garden goat. The male,
emasculated in its youth, holds its
condition even in drought, and when killed
and served as roast or boiled mutton not one
diner in a hundred can tell the difference.
' When corned the difficulty increases. Kid,
served as lamb, is just the thing. In the far-
back pubs the larder on coach days is always
replenished with fresh mutton from the goat-
herd. The gentle nannie, on her part, fills
baby’s feeding-bottle and improves the morn-
ing tea. Both flesh and milk are uncom-
monly pure, as the goat is an extraordinarily
healthy beast. In the country towns the
youth harnesses them in teams of from four
to 10 in small vehicles made for the purpose,
and thus draws firewood and water, the latter
at the rate of a shilling per cask. He like-
wise delivers goods from the stores or rail-
way station for a consideration. The skins
of the slaughtered animals, if cured cor-
rectly, make comfortable floor mats. Tanned,
they may be converted into boot tops that
wili wear out cast-iron soles; and the pelt
of the kid, when treated and made into a
waistcoat, would fetch a sigh of satisfaction
from Billjim battling through the winter in
the trenches.
You Are the Harp,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
You are the harp, and the wind that comes—
The bro'Wn Avest wind from the sun-dried
plain—
Makes you quiver and bend and sigli ;
The hot brown wind that goes SAveeping by
To harry the crackling grain.
You are the harp, and the soft south
breeze —-
The cool green breeze where the hills unroll,
Bringing the breath of the resined piue,
Delicate daisy and eglantine—
Makes laughter in your soul.
You are the harp, and the cold east wind —
The bleak grey wind with its taste of rain—
Will make you sob on a minor note,
Catching the laughter in your throat,
And send you to prayer again.
You are the harp, and the red north Avind—
The Avind that lashes the river Avhite —
Will mutter of martial moods to you
Till your pulses throb as the war drums do
When the heroes go to fight.
You are the harp, and from East and West
Your lovers seek you, from North and South ;
And I know, though I SAveep your strings
to-day
To find the notes that my touch can play
In the music of your mouth,
That you are the harp, and the day must
dawn
When other fingers response shall find,
My melody die beneath newer things—
For you are the harp of a hundred strings
And your lovers are the wind !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
Queensland Gossip.
Brisbane, November 25, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindaii, —
The row about the Residential Club for
returned soldiers is over. The R.S.A. will
take control of the institution, but all men
in uniform will be welcome. A building has
been bought, and the only thing that is
wanted now is the wherewithal to pay tor
the embellishments.
The Repatriation Fund is to get another
lift. This time the lift will be represented
by a Golden Casket, and for five shillings,
my dear, anybody is liable to win ioULKJ.
The Attorney-General has let it be known
that he will be in the front trenches when
the anti-gambling league rages to the front
with its brolly and elastic-side boots.
The Toy Fund for the kiddies of soldiers
and sailors is building up. The committee
meeting the other morning was enlivened by
the remarks of a lady who said that she
would not work with a lot of anti-conscrip-
tionists. As the only alleged “anti” present
had two sons at the Front the argument
fizzled out for lack of fuel. Fuel is
frightfully scarce just now, Thargomindah.
Requests for the establishment of baby
clinics are about to be handed in to Home
Secretary Huxham. It is possible that the
first of these helps to little Australia may
be established at the State Home for Infants
at Wooloowin —a home which is a credit to
the hamlet. There are 50 infants in the
domicile.
Eileen Kingsbury, the only daughter of
Crown Prosecutor and Mrs. Kingsbury, was
married this morning to young Sachs, a good
Australian despite his distant-looking name.
Florence Micheli brightened life’s worries
with a sing-song on Wednesday afternoon.
Doris Micheli (’cello) and Amy Mills (con-
tralto) helped her to attain the object of
her mission.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Died in Bundaberg, Dr. Thomas Henry
May. a big-hearted man who has doctored
Sugartown for nearly 40 years. May made
a pile out of his practice in the early days,
but investments in sugar relieved him of
much of it and an uncommonly generous
disposition prevented much subsequent accu-
mulation. He was a father to the poor, and
at 74 he was still too young to be spared
when the Black Camel called.
The Queensland Government’s Bill to dis-
allow lawyers who represent “monopoly
companies” from sitting in Parliament is
aimed at Macartney (of Thynne and Macart-
ney), one of the most awkward opponents of
the Byan Government. At the time of the
’9l strike Thynne and Macartney used to
do the legal battling for the unions; and
even Eat didn’t suggest that that was a
good enough reason to disqualify them as
legislators. Yet it may have been. There
are certainly 62 other good reasons why no
lawyer at all should be eligible for Parlia-
ment.
Retailed recently in the Assembly the
story of how Charles Lilley complimented a
man charged with murder on his able de-
fence by remarking that one would think
he came from the Legislative Assembly.
“Thank God,” said the accused, “I have not
come down as low as that.” It recalls a
Parkes yarn which had a finish in the same
Assembly. Many years ago a Sydney Domain
deputation headed by a fiery young orator
bearded Parkes. The Domain Demosthenes
poured out such eloquence as even the
old man had seldom listened to, and he was
half paralysed for a moment. At length he
rose. “Young man,” he said, solemnly, “be-
ware ! With your tongue, in 10 years you
will either be hin Parliament or hin gaol.”
Parliament it was. The young orator was
Joe Lesina, afterwards M.L.A. for Clermont.
“Bill, jun.”: After the attack on Abdul
at Bir-el-abd, where the Australians and
Maorilanders behaved never more gallantly,
there was an act of bravery that will always
live in my memory. One of the British
aeroplanes was brought down by enemy fire
in our lines. Although the landing was well
made the pilot was seen to get out and stagger-
in a drunken fashion towards our patrol.
On getting close to him we saw that he was
mortally wounded. At first the poor chap
did not know he was among friends, but
when at last his brain grasped the fact he
made his report and expired. A hero if ever
there was one! While dying, this brave man
brought his machine safely to earth and
finished his job before his spirit would give
in. In the ’plane was found the observer —
dead. His head hung over the side and the
front of the machine was covered with
blood.

What 1000 Motor Cars a Day Make Possible
THE latest improvements in the Overland
factory —which enable a production of
1000 complete motor cars a day —again
emphasize the enormous economy of enor-
mous production.
Never before has anyone produced
1000 cars a day of this size and class —nor
even half so many.
This production of 1000 cars a day makes
it possible for us to use the highest quality of
materials at a minimum of cost. It not only
permits, but actually enforces, an accuracy of
workmanship which is neither possible nor
required in the smaller production of other
popular-priced cars.
1000 cars a day make possible better,
larger and more comfortable cars than have
ever before been produced at anywhere near
the price.
Note these exceptional Overland advan-
tages: en bloc engine—easy operating clutch
—cantilever rear springs—electric control but-
tons on steering column large tyres —•
adjustable pedals complete equipment
electric starter —electric lighting.
Please see the nearest Overland dealer —
he will gladly demonstrate this exceptional
motor car for you. Learn for yourself what
this production of 1000 cars a day means for
your comfort—for your economy.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a hard thing to say, but it appeared that
a visit from a few Zeppelins to Australia would do
good. —Colonel Weir on the Referendum result.
Send them along! The darksome time is ripe.
In shop and mart the sickly candles shine.
In sombre shades men eat the lukewarm tripe
Or hold a wilted match what time they dine.
Send us a Zepp. with headlights burning clear
To shake our souls to some supreme elation;
Then in our darkness we may rise and cheer
The splendor of our new illumination !
N.s.w.
PAT O’MAORI.
Melbourne Argus is suspected of having a
member of the I.W.W. on its composing staff.
Anyway, the name of the paper’s favorite
suburb was the other day printed Toorank.
Ronald O. Duncan, of Christchurch
(M.L.), who has just offered his country
residence, Glenmark, formerly the home of
millionairess Mrs. A. Q. Townend, to the
Canterbury Patriotic Association as a con-
valescent hospital for Wounded soldiers, is
still a comparative youngster. He com-
menced to earn tucker with the late Arthur
Ollivier, accountant, as stamp-licker at five
bob a week, at the age of 14. He was head
of staff at 19; passed his exam, as associate
of the Institute of Accountants at 20, and
was thus the youngest in the Dominion;
commenced business on his own account at
22 with a capital of 13 pence; and presently
intermingled his auditing with some daring
speculations in station properties which
turned up trumps. He retired from business
last December with his pile made and roofed
over.
“Wairau”: The current number of M.L.
Agricultural Journal supports “Nick
Bottom” (B. 5/10/T6)
A Taranaki farmer has written to the Department
regarding a milking-apparatus which is evidently on
sale in the Dominion. His letter reads as follows:
“No doubt you have come into touch with the
milking-machine, composed of rubber tubes and silver
siphons to put up the cows’ teats. Unfortunately I
bought three of them last February, and I had not
used them three days before 10 or 12 cows were suffer-
ing from swollen udders. I had to stop using them,
and all the cows came back right again except one,
which became so bad that her back was up. I
drenched and rugged her and saved her life, but the
back half of her udder mortified and dropped off.
Will you kindly write me your opinion of this
machine, and if you know of anyone else who has been
‘had’ with it? I think it is a pity such things
are sold in M.L. for milking.”
The condition set up in these cows is just what
might have been expected to happen as a result of
the use of such an apparatus in the hands of a dairy-
farmer. Quite apart from the risk of setting up in-
flammation of the udder through direct injury to the
•tissues, resulting from the tubes being pushed too far
up the teat, or through the introduction into the
udder of germs capable of setting up an inflammatory
condition, the continued daily use of teat-tubes for
milking cows is very undesirable. Muscles guard the
outflow of milk from the teat, and in time it would
probably be found that muscular relaxation has been
caused to a sufficient extent to make the cow unpro-
fitable through leaking away of the milk.
From Arthur Baxter Bruce, honorary
six-and-eightpence of the N.S.AV. Society for
the Prevention of Cruelty to Animals: —
Apparently “Aneb” (B. 9/11/’l6) is unaware of
the difficulties which the Society has to face. He
complains that it does not extend its operations to
dairying districts. Unfortunately we have to rely
upon public subscription, subsidised £ for £ by
the Government. Subscribers are very bashful, ami
the Government does not extend any further help,
such as free railway passes to its officers, as is done
by other States. Consequently the whole of the
funds are consumed in attending to complaints of
cruelty, and checking cruel practices, within the city
and surburban limits. No matter how much it may
wish to do so the Society cannot go into the coun-
try and check even such cruelty as “Aneb” mentions.
That such cruelties as “Aneb” mentions are prac-
tised in the country is not at all improbable. Three
members of the Society only a short time ago
visited the Abattoirs and noticed a number of very
similar practices, and representations were made to
the Board in connection therewith. Prosecutions are
contemplated in order to prevent a recurrence, if
possible.
The Society would like “Aneb” and any other
readers who are sufficiently interested in the work
to communicate with the Society at its office, Post
Office Chambers, Sydney, and they will be convinced
that it is not dead, as suggested, but very much
alive. Unfortunately its objects only appeal to the
minority. The majority think that anything is
good enough for an animal, as long as it does its
assigned work and provides them with money.
And I am sorry that there are many even of the
minority who do not help us by becoming members.
“T.F.M.” collects the evidence: —
Who discovered King Island? The opinion most pre-
valent amongst our historical moles is that it was
Captain Black, of the brig Harbinger—the first
craft to sail through Bass Strait from west to
east. Baudin, the skipper of Le Geographe, also
lodged a claim, but it was promptly scuttled by
Governor King, who asserted that “the island was
first discovered by Mr. Reid in the Marthar (sic)
in 1798; afterwards seen by Mr. Black in the Har-
binger, and surveyed by Mr. Runav in 1802.” In
the latest of Fredk. Watson’s “Records” still
another claimant d rops anchor —Captain William
Campbell, of the brig Harrington. That hooker,
while lying in Farm Cove in 1807, was seized and
carried to sea by a gang of convicts, and in peti-
tioning the Home authorities to compensate him
for the loss of his ship, Campbell incidentally stated
he had “arrived in Port Jackson first in the Dept-
ford in 1797, in the Rebecca in 1799, and in the
Harrington in 1800, and was during that time
engaged in mercantile speculations in Basses
Streights; discovered King’s Island, and made other
useful discoveries in those seas.” It would, there-
fore, seem as if the question is still an open one.
In any case, Campbell and his brig were repeatedly
in the limelight in the infant days of N. S. Wales.
It was he who flouted the vice-regal order to “dress
ship” in honor of the French exploring vessels then
anchored in Neutral Bay, for the reason that, in
the eyes of a sturdy British sea-dog, the flag of his
nation was not displayed so conspicuously b.V the
Frenchies as it ought to have been. It was the
same Campbell who, after a trip to the South
American coast (in which he did a bit of privateer-
ing on his own account), brought to Port Jackson
the only tidings ever received as to the fate of
George Bass. Finally, after a stormy career afloat,
this ancient mariner dropped anchor on 2000 acres
of land in the Camden district, granted him by
Macquarie as a solatium for the seizure of his
brig, the which allotment is still known as Harring-
ton Park. A Sydney thoroughfare—in the Rocks
region—also acquired its name from Campbell's
everlasting hooker that finally became a farm.

majority “Bat to live,” but when your
digestion is faulty, eating causes your life
to be a misery. To properly nourish your
body your food must be thoroughly digested
and its various constituents pass to the
organs for which they are intended.
When this is not done the food does you
no good, but rather harm, for it lies in
your stomach, causing poisonous gases
and disorders of the stomach—causes con-
stipation, which means that the food, in-
stead of being expelled through the bowels,
lies there, and the poisonous matter is
absorbed into the system. All this can
be avoided by taking a course of Cham-
berlain’s Tablets. They aid digestion,
cleanse the stomach, stimulate the liver
and bowels to perform their work, and in-
vigorate the system in so doing. Cham-

gently and naturally that there is no fear

No one thing is more conducive to good

and liver. They eliminate all impurities

of trouble. When the stomach fails to
perform its functions the bowels become
deranged, the liver and kidneys congested,
causing numerous diseases, the most fatal
of which are painless and therefore the
more to be dreaded. The important thing
is to restore the stomach and liver to a
healthy condition, and for this purpose
no better preparation can be used than
Chamberlain’s Tablets.
If you were able to live just right all
the time there would be little need
for medicine, less need for doctors. But
some little thing is constantly coming
along to put us out of balance—exposure,
change of diet or drinking water, irregu-
lar meals are little things that will at
times upset the best of us. Nothing
particular the matter, but we don’t feel
good. At such times the wise among us
will take a few doses of Chamberlain’s
Tablets with certain knowledge that they
will put us right, right away.
Many women habitually suffer from
headaches which make life a daily purga-
tory. Frequently they are caused by in-
digestion, and to relieve them permanently
you must remove the cause. For indi-
gestion, biliousness, constipation, disorders
of the stomach and liver there is nothing
better than Chamberlain’s Tablets. Head-
ache cannot exist when Chamberlain’s
Tablets are taken.
Back up a sewer and you will poison
the whole neighborhood, clog up the liver
and bowels and your system is full of
undigested food, which sours and fer-
ments —this means indigestion, headache,
bad breath, yellow skin and everything
that is horrible. Chamberlain’s Tablets
quickly stop fermentation in the stomach,
and they make the liver lively, tone up

going.

The Inky Way.
“Pen”: Sydney pressmen have been sup-
plied with a new brand of police pass, which
authorises them “to enter public arenas or
lines held by the police,” and instructs the
particular member of the force to whom it
is produced that the pressman “should be
afforded every reasonable assistance in the
discharge of his duties.” One whisky-stunned
Wayfarer put an unexpected strain on this
last-clause the other night by thrusting his
pass beneath a busy Hop’s nose and demand-
ing reasonable assistance to get home. The
Hop deposited him gently on the right tram
and warned the conductor to see that he was
safely delivered at the right corner. Then
he winked at the motor-man and went back
to point duty.
* * * *
“Pen”: It happened to Bendigo (Vic.)
Advertiser some years ago. I did a ser-
mon in which the Darson talked about the
Twelve Apostles. Someone else wrote up a
rifle match. Next day’s ’Tiser said “The
Twelve Apostles went to Echuca and fired a
match against the local team. The scores
were”
* * * *
“Murkah” : What looks like sheer cussed-
ness soon settled the Navy Department’s
progressive move in broadcasting a press
service to ships at sea. The private enter-
prise which runs Australia’s mercantile
wireless issued instructions, so a ship’s
operator tells me, that the “press” messages
sent out by the Commonwealth stations
were not to be copied. He could offer no
reason for this surprising order. But the
motive can’t be economy, for the slip of
paper the stuff is written on would be the
only expense.
From that old money-spinner, the S. M.
Herald :
Many happy returns of the day to Mrs. So-and-so,
late of The Oaks and Camden. Your loving son,
Joseph, of Such-and-Such, Strathfield.
Joseph Reserves a bonus; a Birthday Greet-
ing column, if it caught on, might burst the
cash register in the front office.
“ Javon”: Sam Saunders, the editor who
made the Lyttleton Times the biggest
Radical force in Maoriland, has given up his
job of Liberal organising, and is now on
the staff of the Referee, the sporting section
of the Weekly Press —relative of the Press
which Saunders out-generalled for 20 years.
* * * *
The boys of the 3rd Divisional team,
lately in training at Salisbury Plain (Eng.),
have brought out the second number of their
paper, Astra, the first of tvhich was
hatched in Melbourne. The 32-page three-
penn’orth was sold out —1000 copies—in a
day. Young Schuler, son of Melbourne
Age’s editor, runs the literary side, and
Daryl Lindsay, brother of Norman, the
pictures.
“Chevron”: Dave Low’s exemption from
military service on the ground of his greater
value to the nation as a cartoonist is, in one
way, a pity. It robs his colleagues of the
opportunity of presenting him with the fare-
well gift of an automatic, inscribed, “Fire,
Low, and lay them out!”
“St. Jimmy”: A very wild man of Gipps-
land advertises in a hurry:—
I hereby give notice that anyone interfering- with
mv wife between Norwell and Traralgon will be
prosecuted.
Well, many a man wants a bigger Eden
than that for himself.
* * * *
The S. M. Herald, after attending a fire:
The office... .on the ground floor, where important
documents are held, was practically undamaged,
although the contents were well covered by insurance.
That “although” must have crawled in on its
hands and knees.
* * * *
There’s no doubt about Bean’s reputation
with his countrymen at the Front, anyway.
Says a Ballarat boy writing home after the
dishing of the Hun at Pozieres:—
Captain Bean was there, so you may rely on
getting the facts. He is the onlv “dinkum oil” news-
merchant. The flowery, dramatic, leg-pulling Eng-
lish correspondents write as if they depended largely
on the information supplied them by wounded men
at the bases back in England. We satisfy ourselves
time and again about their many inaccuracies. Cap-
tain Bean sees what he writes of, and writes just
what he sees.
A case of Bean and seen, evidently.
TELEGRAPH WIRES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Phantoms, snow-white, of close-packed Cumuli,
Float lazily across the lower air.
And, daubed with careless brush, the Autumn sky
In cold cerulean hue lies here and there
Like holes pierced in some tissue-paper orb.
So far, this verse meets with my best desires.
It must be what the editor requires.
Yet two things all the picturesque absorb;
They flaunt me till my anguished soul perspires:
Two overhanging telegraphic wires.
Here, where some brown leaves rustle in the blast,
A taint of rotting Nature haunts the air.
The haughty moon serenely wanders past
Across the lake; with calm, unending stare,
The passive planets in the background gleam.
The night has come, and still I make this rhyme.
Two verses only written by this time.
For if I glance above to catch a theme,
The simile, that Heaven alone inspires,
Gets tangled in those telegraphic wires.
The misty drapings in the valleys fade.
The virgin, Morn, climbs softly o’er the East,
Naked and blushing; then prude Night, dismayed,
Slinks from the sight on which his eyes should
feast.
See how the sky....
I raise my thoughtless eyes
And lose the theme. I tear my uncombed hair,
And fling these tortured verses through the air.
Perdition damn the hands that placed them there,
Those scornful, mocking wires that grin and stare —■
Picking my verses from some prickly-pear.
I turn for home, and thus soliloquise ;
While in the mood what lyrics rich and rare
I might have made if I had found some pliers,
And, climbing, cut those two distracting wires!
s.a.
GEORGE R. HAMBIDGE.
